Weaver v. Weaver


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


78 A.D.2d 771; 433 N.Y.S.2d 654; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13330



October 30, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband appealed the judgment of the Erie Supreme Court (New York), which issued a temporary injunction against him as part of a divorce action and required him to pay alimony and child support.


OVERVIEW: As part of a divorce proceeding the trial court issued a temporary injunction against the husband and directed him to pay certain amounts of alimony and child support. The husband did not dispute the propriety of the divorce and custody provisions but asserted that the court erred in the amount of the ordered alimony and child support payments. The court found that the trial court did not make complete findings of fact to support its determinations. The court opted not to reverse and order a new trial, but to make the findings of fact and conclusions of law that the record supported. The court modified the general alimony provision for payment of all expenses related to the use of the marital residence. It addressed the indefiniteness of the provision by amending it to direct payment for specific items, including the mortgage, utility bills, taxes, and insurance. The court examined the parties' assets and income and reduced the weekly alimony and child support payments. The court continued the temporary injunction with respect to the modified judgment until the husband made all the payments that were currently due.


OUTCOME: The court modified the amount of alimony and child support the husband was ordered to pay and affirmed that modified judgment, including the temporary injunction.

 

1021 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


UU. v. VV.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


78 A.D.2d 711; 432 N.Y.S.2d 411; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13246



October 9, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of an order of the Family Court of Broome County (New York), which directed the father to (1) pay the confinement expenses of respondent mother, up to a specific amount, and (2) pay child support.


OVERVIEW: The family court ordered the father pay the confinement expenses of the mother, up to a certain amount, and to pay weekly child support. The father appealed, arguing that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 514 was unconstitutional in that it violated the equal protection clauses of the New York State and United States Constitutions. The court held that when § 514 was read in a gender-neutral manner, it authorized the family court to impose the obligation of paying for the confinement expenses of the mother of the child upon either the mother or father or both as the court, in its discretion, might deem proper. Thus, under § 514, the family court was to consider the means and responsibilities of each parent without regard to the sex of the parent. Accordingly, there was no sex-based discrimination and § 514 did not violate the Equal Protection Clause. The mother was a recipient of public assistance. Consequently, no useful purpose would be served by reversing the order and remanding the matter back to the family court for further proceedings to determine the means and responsibilities of both parties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the family court directing the father to pay confinement expenses of the mother and to pay child support.

 

1022 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


78 A.D.2d 643; 432 N.Y.S.2d 121; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13130



October 6, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), convicting him of attempted rape in the first degree, robbery in the first degree, and assault in the first degree, after a nonjury trial, and sentencing him as a second felony offender under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted rape in the first degree, two counts of robbery in the first degree, and two counts of assault in the first degree. The trial court relied on a prior court-martial conviction to sentence defendant as a second felony offender under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of using court-martial convictions for enhanced sentencing purposes. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the trial court properly sentenced defendant as a second felony offender. The use of prior court-martial convictions to impose enhanced sentences upon convicted felons was recognized as permissible in New York. The case relied on by defendant, holding that a prior uncounseled misdemeanor conviction, valid in and of itself, could not be used to impose an enhanced sentence, was clearly distinguishable.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment was affirmed because the trial court properly sentenced defendant as a second felony offender by using a prior court-martial conviction to impose an enhanced sentence.

 

1023 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Svendsen v. Smith's Moving & Trucking Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


76 A.D.2d 504; 431 N.Y.S.2d 94; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12163; 29 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 609



August 18, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County, dated January 24, 1980, modified, on the law, by (1) deleting from the first decretal paragraph thereof the word "denied" and substituting therefor the word "granted" and (2) adding provisions thereto (a) declaring that subdivision (2) of section 7-210 of the Uniform Commercial Code is unconstitutional to the extent that it authorizes the ex parte sale of the bailed goods, without first affording the lienee the opportunity for a hearing, and (b) enjoining defendants, their agents, employees, successors, and anyone acting on their behalf from selling, transferring or disposing of plaintiff's property pursuant to subdivision (2) of section 7-210 of the Uniform Commercial Code without affording plaintiff an opportunity for a prior hearing. As so modified, order affirmed, with $ 50 costs and disbursements to plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which denied the tenant's motion for summary judgment (except as to damages) and for class certification in her action against defendant warehouseman for a declaratory judgment that N.Y. U.C.C. Law § 7-210(2) was unconstitutional, for an injunction, and for damages for the unlawful sale of her belongings.


OVERVIEW: Under the direction of a city marshal, the warehouseman removed the tenant's belongings from the apartment from which the tenant had been evicted and stored them at its warehouse. When the tenant failed to pay storage charges, the warehouseman notified the tenant that it had imposed a warehouseman's lien and gave her a notice of sale of the belongings. After the tenant learned that the warehouseman sold her belongings, she brought the action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief and damages on the ground that N.Y. U.C.C. Law § 7-210(2), which purported to authorize the sale, violated the due process clause of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. The tenant also sought to have her action certified as a class action. The court modified a trial court order to provide that the tenant's motion for summary judgment on the merits of her claim was granted. The statutory provision involved violated the state due process clause because it delegated exclusive sovereign power to resolve a dispute to the warehouseman, but failed to afford the tenant an opportunity for a hearing before the lien on her belongings was foreclosed. The denial of class action status was not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The order denying the motion for summary judgment was modified by substituting the word "granted" for "denied" in the paragraph disposing of the summary judgment motion, the challenged statutory provision was declared unconstitutional insofar as it authorized an ex parte sale of bailed goods without affording a lienee an opportunity for a hearing, and the warehouseman was enjoined from transferring the tenant's belongings without a prior hearing.


HEADNOTES

Warehousemen  [***2] -- Warehouseman's Lien -- Constitutionality

1. Subdivision (2) of section 7-210 of the Uniform Commercial Code, which authorizes the ex parte sale of bailed goods without first affording the lienee the opportunity for a hearing, is unconstitutional as violative of due process; by the provisions of the statute, the State has extended a creditor's rights beyond the common law and has so entwined itself into the debtor-creditor relationship as to constitute sufficient and meaningful State participation triggering constitutional protections; in addition, the procedure allowing ex parte foreclosure is a delegation to the warehouseman of the exclusive power of the sovereign to resolve disputes which is sufficient to involve due process protections.

Actions -- Class Actions -- Warehouseman's Lien

2. In an action to declare subdivision (2) of section 7-210 of the Uniform Commercial Code, which authorizes ex parte sale of bailed goods without first affording the lienee the opportunity for a hearing, unconstitutional, it is not an abuse of discretion to deny class action status; subsequent complainants will be adequately protected under the principle of stare decisis.

 

1024 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Plaza Health Clubs, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


76 A.D.2d 509; 430 N.Y.S.2d 815; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12164



August 14, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Judgments (denominated orders), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on February 7, 1980, modified, on the law, to the extent of dismissing the complaints for lack of standing, and otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sexually oriented businesses sought review of the judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which declared amendments to defendant city's zoning resolution proscribing certain adult physical culture establishments constitutional, denied a motion for a preliminary injunction, and granted sexually oriented businesses' cross motions to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The sexually oriented businesses filed this declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that the city's zoning resolution regarding their businesses was unconstitutional. The zoning resolution proscribed the operation of all premises equipped or arranged for cross-sexual physical services. On appeal, the court concluded that the additional details contained within the resolution sufficiently described the illegal conduct so the resolution was facially constitutional. Because the sexually oriented businesses were not engaging in the conduct prohibited by the resolution, they did not have standing to challenge the regulation. There was no merit the claim of the sexually oriented businesses that the resolution imposed sex-based discrimination. Because the sexually oriented businesses failed to establish standing in regard to any potentially justiciable issue, the court dismissed the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint brought by the sexually oriented businesses for lack of standing.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Zoning Resolution -- Vagueness

1. A statute or ordinance proscribing designated activities must clearly define the activity so as to leave as little discretion as possible to the agency charged with its enforcement; failure to so draft a law lays it open to constitutional attack for vagueness on the principle that it invites arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement. However, section 12-10 of the New York City Zoning Resolution, which proscribes adult physical culture [***2] establishments, described, with certain exceptions, as "any establishment, club or business by whatever name designated which offers or advertises or is equipped or arranged so as to provide as part of its services, massages, body rubs, alcohol rubs, baths or other similar treatment by members of the opposite sex", is plainly designed and intended to preclude offering, advertising and providing cross-sexual massages and other similar treatment and does not rest the proscription on the mere presence of equipment, which could conceivably apply to a wide variety of otherwise legal business and residential premises, and, so read, the resolution is facially constitutional; moreover, the resolution can be construed by the State courts to eliminate any constitutional issue.

Parties -- Standing -- Validity of Zoning Resolution

2. An action to declare a statute or regulation or resolution unconstitutional lies where a plaintiff concedes that his activity is proscribed by the statute or regulation or resolution as written or as it appears to be or is threatened to be interpreted and enforced, but the rule is otherwise where plaintiff specifically denies that his activity is within [***3] the proscribed parameters and, accordingly, plaintiffs, operators of "leisure spas for men", lack standing to challenge the constitutionality of section 12-10 of the New York City Zoning Resolution, which proscribes adult physical culture establishments, described, with certain exceptions, as "any establishment, club or business by whatever name designated which offers or advertises or is equipped or arranged so as to provide as part of its services, massages, body rubs, alcohol rubs, baths or other similar treatment, by members of the opposite sex", where plaintiffs, in the affirmations in support of motions for declaratory and injunctive relief, state that they are not presently advertising or offering cross-sexual physical services and that the only massage services now being provided by plaintiffs are by licensed masseurs, since, if that is the case, plaintiffs are no longer operating such proscribed establishment.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Zoning Resolution

3. The constitutionality of the use of zoning resolutions to restrict massage parlors where massages are given to members of the opposite sex has been upheld in the highest courts of other States, with the [***4] United States Supreme Court dismissing the appeals for lack of a substantial Federal question; such dismissals constitute adjudications on the merits.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Commercial Advertising

4. Commercial advertising is not entitled to constitutional protection where the subject matter of the advertising is itself illegal and subject to regulation. Accordingly, where plaintiffs, operators of "leisure spas for men", challenge the constitutionality of section 12-10 of the New York City Zoning Resolution, which proscribes adult physical culture establishments, described, with certain exceptions, as "any establishment, club or business by whatever name designated which offers or advertises or is equipped or arranged so as to provide as part of its services, massages, body rubs, alcohol rubs, baths or other similar treatment by members of the opposite sex", their constitutional attack is without merit if they are advertising such proscribed use; on the other hand, if they are no longer offering cross-sexual massages, as they claim, they lack the standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute on the free speech aspect of the resolution.  [***5] The resolution must be read as allowing advertising to be used as evidence of the use of the property, not as authorizing closing solely on the basis of the content of the advertising.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination Based on Sex

5. The proscription in section 12-10 of the New York City Zoning Resolution against premises providing commercial massages for patrons of the opposite sex is not sex-based discrimination, since the resolution does not prohibit the hiring of massagists of either sex.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Time Limitations for Removal of Nonconforming Uses

6. A time limitation on removing a nonconforming use is reasonable where the public gain to be achieved by such exercise of the police power will outweigh the private loss to the owner of the establishment conducting such activity; a balance must be found between social harm and private injury. Accordingly, the reasonableness of one-year amortization period to eliminate establishments offering massages and similar treatments to members of the opposite sex (NY City Zoning Resolution, § 12-10), is not amenable to preliminary injunctive relief, where the potential benefit to the community [***6] has been clearly demonstrated by statistics which link the burgeoning massage parlor trade and illegal prostitution, a development which is detrimental to the public and the exercise of legitimate business enterprise in the city; if plaintiffs have standing to show injury, the reasonableness of the time period might be subject to trial.

 

1025 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Union Sav. Bank v. Siebert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


77 A.D.2d 893; 431 N.Y.S.2d 88; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12684



August 11, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a state's banking board and superintendent of banks, sought review of the order of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York) that granted plaintiff bank's motion for a preliminary injunction and denied defendants' cross-motions to dismiss the plaintiff's declaratory judgment action. The bank had filed the action seeking a declaration that certain rules of the board were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendants approved a bank's relocation application. Plaintiff filed an action, seeking a declaration that certain rules of defendant board were unconstitutional. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court modified the trial court's order to the extent of granting defendants' cross-motions to dismiss plaintiff's first and second causes of action. The court noted that the approval of the application was an administrative act subject to review pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78. Plaintiff's first and second causes of action were barred by the four- month limitation period found in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217 because they were filed two years after they accrued. However, plaintiffs' third cause of action was commenced within the four-month limitations period. Because the elements of the first and second causes were impliedly present in the surviving cause of action, resolution by the trial court was proper. However, the court held that pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6301 a preliminary injunction did not lie against defendants because they had already acted to the full extent of their powers and no further action in regard to the application was required.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order and as modified affirmed. The court modified the order by deleting from the first decretal paragraph the words "and the State Defendants;" and by deleting the second and third decretal paragraphs and substituting provisions that granted defendants' cross motions to the extent of dismissing plaintiff's first and second causes of action.

 

1026 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Meaott Constr. Corp. v. Ross


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


76 A.D.2d 137; 431 N.Y.S.2d 207; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11740



July 31, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractors appealed the judgment in favor of defendant, the Industrial Commissioner of the State of the New York (commissioner)of the Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the contractors' action for a declaratory judgment, seeking an order declaring N.Y. Lab. Law § 220 illegal and unconstitutional and enjoining the commissioner from redetermining any wages payable under their contracts.


OVERVIEW: The contractors executed public works contracts. All of the applicants were informed that the wage obligations were susceptible to increase before they submitted their bids. The contractors maintained that the redetermination clause was an impairment of the contractual obligations of the contractors. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, concluding that the redetermination clause was both reasonable and appropriate because passing the risk of rising costs onto the contractor tended to insure that the state's monetary exposure was conclusively determined at the outset of the project, which precluded the need to repeat the costly and bothersome appropriations process whenever there was an increase of the wage rate. The court explained that it was only fair to place the risk of rising labor costs on the contractor who was intimately familiar with the construction market and would have been better equipped to estimate future costs. The court determined that all of the parties agreed in advance that the contractors would have paid all wage increases without reimbursement from the government, which was not violative of any principles of contract law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the contractors' action against the commissioner in the contractors' declaratory judgment against the commissioner seeking an order declaring the statute illegal and unconstitutional insofar as it permitted post-award wage redeterminations and enjoining the commissioner from redetermining any wages payable under their contracts.


HEADNOTES

Labor -- Prevailing Rate of Wages

 Section 220 of the Labor Law, as amended by chapter 752 of the Laws of 1975, which provides that contractors on public works contracts shall pay the prevailing rate of wages which, pursuant to the terms of said contracts, may be redetermined after a contract is let, is reasonable, is not in contravention of public policy, does not offend basic contract principles, and does not constitute an impairment of contract obligations in violation of the contract clause of the United States Constitution.

 

1027 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


76 A.D.2d 228; 431 N.Y.S.2d 209; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11750



July 31, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for two counts of murder in the second degree, kidnapping in the first degree, and robbery in the first degree by the Sullivan County Court (New York). Previously, the matter was before the court, and it reversed the robbery and the kidnapping convictions, and the matter was appealed to the United States Supreme Court which remanded the matter for further consideration in light of a recent Supreme Court opinion.


OVERVIEW: Following defendant's conviction, the United States Supreme Court changed existing law and held that absent exigent circumstances, the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution prohibited the police from making a warrantless and nonconsensual entry into a suspect's home in order to make a routine felony arrest. On the remand, the court was required to determine whether the new Fourth Amendment rule was to be applied retroactively. The court affirmed the lower court's conviction of defendant for two counts of murder in the second degree, and it reaffirmed its previous decision, which held that defendant's convictions for of kidnapping in the first degree and robbery in the first degree were reversed and that the indictments charging defendant with kidnapping in the first degree and robbery in the first degree were dismissed. The court ruled that the new Fourth Amendment rule was not to be applied retroactively because the warrantless entry for the purpose of a routine felony arrest was not essential to the very integrity of the fact-finding process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for two counts of murder in the second degree, and it reversed defendant's convictions for kidnapping in the first degree and robbery in the first degree. The court ordered the dismissal of the underlying kidnapping and robbery indictments.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Arrest -- Retroactivity of Payton v New York

1. Payton v New York (445 U.S. 573), which held that absent exigent circumstances, the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution, made applicable to the States by the Fourteenth Amendment, prohibits the police from making a warrantless and nonconsensual entry into a suspect's home in order to make a routine felony arrest, is not to be applied retroactively, since it is not so essential to the very integrity of the fact-finding process that all past reliances upon existing statutory law should be negated so as to rehabilitate with innocence those who by their intentional acts seriously offended the penal statutes of the State.

Crimes -- Retroactivity of Supreme Court Decision

2. The guidelines to be used in determining whether a rule of constitutional dimension should [***2] be retroactively applied are the purpose to be served by the new standards, the extent of the reliance by law enforcement authorities on the old standards, and the effect on the administration of justice of a retroactive application of the new standards.

 

1028 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kember v. Kember


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


77 A.D.2d 646; 430 N.Y.S.2d 382; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12376



July 28, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former wife and defendant former husband sought review of a judgment by the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which held that a portion of the parties' separation agreement pertaining to the husband's agreement to make support payments to the wife was void and that the wife was entitled to a certain weekly alimony sum.


OVERVIEW: The parties' separation agreement contained a provision that purported to relieve the husband of his support obligation if the wife obtained full time employment or after a period of three years. The trial court held that the agreement contravened N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 5-311 and was void as against public policy. The trial court also awarded alimony to the wife based on a de novo review of the parties' financial circumstances. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the cause to the trial court for a determination as to the amount of alimony, if any, that was owed to the wife. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional insofar as it denied husbands and wives the right to contract to relieve the husband of his liability to support his wife. The court stated that the wife's argument that the unconstitutionality of the statute should not have been applied retroactively was without merit. The court found that its previous judgment declaring the statute unconstitutional was applicable to all actions that were pending at the time the decision was handed down, including those in the appellate process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the action for a determination of whether alimony was owed to the wife.

 

1029 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Long Beach v. Flacke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


77 A.D.2d 638; 430 N.Y.S.2d 131; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12363



July 23, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the decision from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which determined that actual notification to the city was not required under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 70-0109(2)(a), that § 70-0109(2)(a) as so interpreted was not unconstitutional, and that the issuance of a permit by respondent New York Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC), to respondent water district was proper.


OVERVIEW: The water district applied for a permit to deepen a well into an aquifer, contending that, due to contamination in wells that had been closed, a severe water shortage was probable that could only be resolved by granting the permit. The aquifer served as the city's sole water supply. The Nassau Department of Health submitted a letter indicating that an engineer's report was remiss in failing to explore other alternatives and in failing to address the impact of the permit on salt water intrusion into, or chemical contamination of, the aquifer. At the time the letter was sent, DEC had four days to decide whether to conduct a public hearing on the application. DEC granted the permit and the trial court determined that issuance of the permit was proper. On appeal, the city claimed that it was entitled to notice under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 70-0109(2)(a). The court modified the trial court's judgment and vacated the permit. The court held that the health department's letter raised substantive and significant issues relating to the application and thus it was arbitrary for DEC to issue the permit without holding a public hearing as required by N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 70-0119(1).


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment and vacated the permit issued to the water district.

 

1030 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


76 A.D.2d 349; 430 N.Y.S.2d 626; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11760



July 21, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Second separate and partial final decree of the Supreme Court, Queens County, entered December 1, 1978, modified, on the law, by increasing the amount awarded claimants Marinello and Saveth for plottage from 5% to 10% (or from $ 187,795 to $ 375,590) and the total award to $ 4,131,490. As so modified, said decree affirmed insofar as appealed from, with costs to appellants Marinello and Saveth payable by the city.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city sought condemnation of certain land owned by appellant property owners. The Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), made awards on the condemned property. The property owners sought review.


OVERVIEW: A city decided to condemn a parcel of land for the purpose of urban renewal. Owners of a part of that land , in an effort to avoid condemnation, agreed to develop the property in accordance with the city's urban renewal plan. Additionally the owners agreed that if they were unable to develop the land, they would agree to the city's condemnation request and that the land would be valued as of a certain date. When several years later the owners gave up their effort to develop the property, they opposed the valuation of the condemned property as of the date previously agreed upon. The trial court ruled in favor of the city and the owners sought review. The court determined (1) that the owners were bound by their agreement and had waived their rights to contest the valuation of the property, (2) the city had not treated the owners in a discriminatory fashion, and (3) the owners were entitled to a 10 percent rather than a 5 percent increment figure on the issue of plottage.


OUTCOME: The court amended the decree of the trial court by increasing the amount awarded to the owners for plottage from 5 percent to 10 percent.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Waiver of Valuation Date

1. In a condemnation proceeding, it is not violative of public policy to enforce a contract whereby claimant landowners accepted a specific valuation date for their property, thereby waiving the right to have the property valued on the title vesting date, inasmuch as it is a private right with no considerations of public policy involved; claimants were not denied equal protection regarding the award dates for other parcels [***2] being condemned where there were cogent reasons presented as to the timing of these condemnations; claimants are entitled to a 10% rather than a 5% plottage increment in their award since their property had not reached the area of maximum utility at which point the law of diminishing returns would set in, although an increment for the property's double street frontage is inappropriate where it does not add to the value of the property.

Words and Phrases -- Waiver

2. Waiver is a voluntary and intentional relinquishment or abandonment of a known existing legal right, advantage, benefit, claim or privilege, which, except for the waiver, a party would have enjoyed.

 

1031 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nishman v. De Marco


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


76 A.D.2d 360; 430 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11761



July 14, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Interlocutory judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered March 26, 1979, affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant attorney challenged an interlocutory judgment of the Supreme Court of Nassau County, Special Term (New York) which held that defendant and plaintiff attorney were entitled to an accounting of partnership fees and expenses in plaintiff's action for an accounting after the dissolution of defendant and plaintiff's law partnership. After trial, the trial court found that the parties had agreed to divide postdissolution fees equally.


OVERVIEW: After 12 years as law partners, plaintiff attorney and defendant attorney agreed to dissolve their partnership. Plaintiff filed an action against defendant for an accounting of postdissolution partnership fees and expenses. The court entered an interlocutory judgment that the parties were entitled to an accounting and found after a bench trial that the parties had agreed to divide postdissolution fees evenly. Defendant appealed and the court affirmed. The trial court's determination of credibility issues and its conclusion that the parties had agreed upon an equal division of fees was supported by the record. A clean hands analysis did not mandate reversal of the interlocutory judgment. The parties stipulated that the trial would be concerned only with the issue of what their partnership agreement was, and intended that stipulation to be exclusionary of all other issues. Strong public policy in favor of stipulations indicated that they had the power to waive the clean hands defense. Defendant therefore could agree to refrain from raising the clean hands defense to defeat plaintiff's equity claim and the trial court had the power to enforce that agreement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's interlocutory judgment holding that plaintiff attorney and defendant attorney were entitled to an accounting of partnership fees and expenses in plaintiff's action for an accounting. The parties had the power to waive the clean hands defense and to agree that defendant would not raise it to defeat plaintiff's claim. The trial court had the right to enforce the stipulation.


HEADNOTES

Partnership -- Dissolution -- Accounting

An interlocutory judgment holding that two attorneys who dissolved their partnership are entitled to an accounting regarding the disposition of postdissolution partnership fees and expenses is proper where there is support in the record for the court's determination of the credibility issues and its conclusion that the parties had agreed upon an equal division of fees received after a certain date; the fact that the plaintiff may not have had "clean hands" does not mandate a reversal of the interlocutory judgment where the only conclusion to be drawn from the language used by the parties in a stipulation and from the circumstances surrounding its making is that they intended to limit the issue at trial to the existence of an agreement relating to fees received after dissolution and that upon [***2] the establishment of such an agreement the sum due would be the subject of a further stipulation, inasmuch as public policy considerations are not violated by a stipulation waiving issues relative to the clean hands doctrine.

 

1032 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wegman's Food Markets, Inc. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


76 A.D.2d 95; 429 N.Y.S.2d 964; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11732



July 10, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, without costs, and judgment entered in favor of respondents, in accordance with opinion by Cardamone, J. P.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company sought review of the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Monroe County (New York), which denied its motion for summary judgment to declare N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 214-h unconstitutional and to enjoin the enforcement of said statute.


OVERVIEW: The company operated 32 retail stores, which sold food, related grocery products, and general merchandise. The company had voluntarily provided a computerized unit price information in all of its stores. Subsequently, defendant state enacted § 214-h, which required certain retailers to provide unit price information to their customers. The statute originally covered only three categories of consumer commodities that were commonly found in food stores. The company did not contest this regulation. However, a fourth category of consumer commodities subject to unit pricing was added, including non-prescription drugs, female hygiene products, and toiletries. Many of the company's competitors were exempted from the requirement. The lower court later denied the company's motion for summary judgment to declare the statute unconstitutional and to enjoin its enforcement. The court affirmed the order, holding that the company did not meet its burden of demonstrating an unconstitutional interference with its right to due process because the required unit pricing was logically and actually related to the valid public purpose of providing consumers with comparison shopping data.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which denied the company's motion for summary judgment to declare a statute unconstitutional and to enjoin the enforcement of said statute.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Unit Pricing Statute -- Due Process of Law

1. Only a minimal level of rationality is necessary to save an economic regulatory statute from constitutional attack on due process grounds; moreover, one challenging a statute on these grounds must overcome a heavy presumption of constitutionality. Accordingly, the requirement of unit pricing of certain nonprescription drugs, female hygiene products and toiletries in food stores ( Agriculture and Markets Law, § 214-h) does not constitute an unreasonable interference with petitioner food store chain's business activities in violation of its right to due process; the requirement is logically and factually related [***2] to the valid public purpose of providing consumers with comparison shopping data, the Legislature could reasonably have concluded that unit pricing promotes accurate price information and enables the shopper to reduce his expenses and the Legislature found, based upon research, that the costs of complying with the statute would be minimal.

Constitutional Law -- Unit Pricing Statute -- Equal Protection of Laws

2. An economic regulation challenged on equal protection grounds is presumed constitutional and requires only that the challenged classification be rationally related to a legitimate State interest; in the area of economic regulation, only invidious discrimination, the wholly arbitrary act, runs afoul of constitutional guarantees. Accordingly, petitioner food store chain failed to meet its burden of proof on its claim that the provision of section 214-h of the Agriculture and Markets Law requiring unit pricing of nonprescription drugs, female hygiene products and toiletries sold in food stores, which products are available to purchasers in unregulated retail and drug stores, violates its right to equal protection in that it treats food stores and other stores selling [***3] identical products differently, where petitioner provided no economic data to overcome the basic premise behind the legislation, that people spend a significant amount of their consumer dollar in supermarkets and that it is there that they need the most protection; the legislative determination to include only food stores in the regulatory scheme, while underinclusive, is not unreasonable.

Constitutional Law -- Unit Pricing Statute -- Vagueness

3. Due process requires a sufficient degree of precision in legislation to provide a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct is forbidden by the statute, which requirement also protects against arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement by establishing boundaries sufficiently distinct for police, Judges and juries to fairly administer the law; statutes are not automatically invalidated on the ground of vagueness simply because of difficulty in determining whether certain marginal activities fall within the scope of the statutory regulations, and an interpretation which supports the constitutionality of the legislation is preferred. Accordingly, the term "food store", used in the statute requiring [***4] unit pricing of certain goods sold in such stores and defined as a store "selling primarily food" ( Agriculture and Markets Law, § 214-h), based on total dollar volume of food sales ( 1 NYCRR 345.5 [b]), provides a sufficient degree of clarity to satisfy the requirements of due process; moreover, with respect to the use of the term "toiletries" in the statute to indicate a type of product sold in food stores that must be unit priced, that term's dictionary meaning, articles used in bathing, dressing and grooming, is sufficiently precise.

Hickland v. Hickland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


77 A.D.2d 683; 430 N.Y.S.2d 15; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12409



July 3, 1980 

 

1035 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Goodell v. Goodell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


77 A.D.2d 684; 429 N.Y.S.2d 789; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12410



July 3, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant former husband appealed an order from the Family Court of Clinton County (New York), which granted an application for additional support filed by plaintiff former wife, finding that the wife demonstrated a change in her financial circumstances. On appeal, the husband contended, inter alia, that the alimony statute, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236, violated the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The husband argued that the wife failed to show a sufficient showing of a change in circumstances to prove her likelihood of becoming a public charge. The husband also argued that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 412, 442, 466 and N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236 were unconstitutional because they violated the Equal Protection Clause. The court found that the wife met her burden of proof for obtaining an increase in support because she showed: (1) an increase in her needs and in the husband's means; and (2) that her financial change of circumstances was for the worse and the husband's financial change were for the better. The court further found that § 236 of the Domestic Relations Law was sex based and violated the Equal Protection Clause. The court concluded, however, that § 236 could be purged of its sex-based discrimination because the statute could be read in a gender-neutral manner, making it applicable to either spouse and affording equal eligibility for alimony to both spouses. Section 236, as preserved, supplied the power to modify the support provisions of the divorce decree. Accordingly, the order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order of the family court, which granted the former wife's application for additional support from her former husband, was affirmed.

 

1036 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Glanville v. Johnson City


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


77 A.D.2d 692; 429 N.Y.S.2d 797; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12418



July 3, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant village appealed from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Broome County (New York), which granted plaintiff fireman's motion for a preliminary injunction, enjoining the village during the pendency of the suit from placing the fireman on light duty in defendant fire department or removing him from the payroll. The fireman was receiving disability benefits under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-a, as amended by 1977 N.Y. Laws 965.


OVERVIEW: The fireman was examined by the village's physician who found that the fireman could perform light duties. The fireman did not submit any contrary medical report indicating that he was unable to perform such light duties. The fire chief then ordered him to report for light duty and the fireman filed the instant suit. The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-a, as amended by 1977 N.Y. Laws 965, did not require a determination by the state comptroller disapproving an application for accidental disability retirement prior to ordering a disabled fireman fit to perform light duty if the ability of the fireman to perform such light duty existed. There was no legislative intention expressed in the original statute to guarantee a firefighter his salary unless and until he was able to return to exactly the same assignment that he was performing at the time that he sustained his disability and no other. If the fireman could only perform light duties, then the fire chief was required to assign him to light duties or allow him to remain on full pay but inactive duty status.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, on the law, without costs, and denied the fireman's motion for a preliminary injunction.

Adams v. Duquette


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.2d 1020; 429 N.Y.S.2d 440; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12142



 June 26, 1980 

 

1038 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Devereaux v. New York State Teachers' Retirement Board


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


75 A.D.2d 277; 429 N.Y.S.2d 743; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11230



June 26, 1980 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed his petition in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to annul a determination of respondent New York State Teachers' Retirement System (TRS) that his military service during World War II was not to be credited to his teaching service time for calculation of retirement benefits.


OVERVIEW: The teacher was a resident of New Jersey when he entered the military, resumed teaching there upon his return, and was credited for four years of military service by New Jersey. He then taught in New York, and after he retired the TRS credited him with 24 years of teaching service in New York and four years and five months' teaching service in New Jersey pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 509 (1947), but refused to credit him for military service. The trial court determined that he was not entitled to credit for service rendered while he was a resident of another state, and that 38 U.S.C.S. § 2021 did not require the TRS to grant him credit. On review, the court found that pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 509, 501, "service in a similar capacity in other states" was to be read in conjunction with "service as a teacher," and that it was not arbitrary or capricious to define such service as excluding military service. The court found that 38 U.S.C.S. § 2021 did not apply to a new or subsequent employer, but only to the right to reemployment after military service. The court affirmed and found that N.Y. Educ. Law § 503 did not unconstitutionally impose a residency requirement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the teacher's petition to annul a determination of the TRS that his military service was not credited to his teaching service time.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Retirement and Pension Benefits

1. Petitioner, who as a member of the Teachers' Pension and Annuity Fund of New Jersey was granted credit by the fund for the time he spent in military service, is not entitled to the same credit as a member of the New York State Teachers' Retirement System, pursuant to former section 509 (subd 2, par a) of the Education Law, inasmuch as the words "service in a similar capacity in other states" must be construed in conjunction with the meaning of the words "service as a teacher" and the word "teacher", as defined in subdivision 4 of section 501, does not include military service since no mention is made of it.

Schools -- Retirement and Pension Benefits

2. Petitioner, who as a member of the Teachers' Pension and Annuity Fund of New Jersey [***2] was granted credit by the fund for the time he spent in military service, is not entitled to the same credit as a member of the New York State Teachers' Retirement System, pursuant to section 2021 of title 38 of the United States Code, inasmuch as the statute, which provides for a right to re-employment by the same employer or persons inducted into the military service, and for the protection of certain benefits such as seniority, status and pay of such persons upon re-employment by the same employer, has no application to a new or subsequent employer.

Declaratory Judgments -- Conversion of Article 78 Proceeding to Declaratory Judgment Action

3. A CPLR article 78 proceeding is not an appropriate vehicle for review of a constitutional question; accordingly, such a proceeding will be considered as converted to a declaratory judgment action in order to reach the constitutional issue raised.

Schools -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Credit for Military Service

4. Subdivision 10 of section 503 of the Education Law does not impose an unconstitutional residency requirement on the obtaining of military service credit, in violation of article IV (§ 2, cl 1) of the [***3] United States Constitution, inasmuch as a disparity in treatment between residents of one State and those of other States is permissible when the classification is substantially related to the object of the statute, and a residency requirement which does not specify a specific period of residency is not unconstitutional; the residency requirement of subdivision 10 of section 503 does not contain a durational disability, and the classification is substantially related to the object of the statute, which was the desire of the State of New York to express its gratitude to its sons who entered their country's service in time of war by conferring certain benefits to them to compensate them for the disruption of their way of life and, in many cases, of their employment by service in the armed forces, which benefits cannot be realistically held to infringe or penalize the right to travel.

May v. Marcus


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


76 A.D.2d 903; 429 N.Y.S.2d 241; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11995



 June 23, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former resident and defendants, owner of a nursing home, sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court of Suffolk County (New York), which granted summary judgment to the former resident in her causes of action for compensatory and punitive damages and dismissed her action for a declaratory judgment on future rent in the former resident's action to recover damages for violation of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-o.


OVERVIEW: The former resident lived in the nursing home. After the legislature passed a law that persons such as the former resident were entitled to a personal allowance of income not considered in determining eligibility for additional benefits and authorizing punitive damages if funds were intentionally misappropriated or withheld, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-o. The owners placed that money in escrow pending the outcome of a suit brought by other nursing home owners to determine the constitutionality of that statute. Milowitz v. University of New York


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.2d 1004; 429 N.Y.S.2d 308; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12127



 June 19, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:

Educ. Law §§ 6509(2), (9), to a hearing panel for a new hearing after an initial hearing resulted in a recommendation that charges against petitioner be dismissed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with placing his architectural seal and signing his name to an untruthful, deceptive, and fraudulent statement, that he had not completed a report, associating himself with a project known to him to be fraudulent and dishonest in character, and with providing a deceptive and misleading in a professional report. 1041 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Charles S. Wilson Memorial Hospital v. Axelrod


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.2d 968; 429 N.Y.S.2d 63; 1980 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12089



 June 12, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of Health of the State of New York challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Broome County (New York), which granted appellee hospital's motion for summary judgment in an action to determine the operability of an amendment to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807(2)(i).


OVERVIEW: In May 1978, the hospital increased its charges to private patients. In July 1978, the governor signed into law, effective immediately, an amendment to § 2807(2)(i), which froze hospital charges. On appeal, the commissioner argued that the amendment required all rates to have been frozen as of their May 1, 1978 level. The court modified the summary judgment. In doing so, the court held that contrary to the decision of the lower court, whose interpretation was selected for its grammatical accuracy, the clearly expressed intent of the New York Legislature was to contain "hospital costs," and in order to accomplish such a purpose the May 1 date should have been held to modify "charges for in-patient care services" and not "services" only. Accordingly, the charges must have been frozen at the May 1, 1978 level. The court further held that the conclusion did not mean that the amendment had retroactive application because lacking legislative indication of retroactivity, the statute spoke as of its effective date, July 24, 1978, and applied to and affected only the charges incurred on and from July 24, 1978 through December 31, 1978, freezing such charges at the May 1, 1978 rate level.


OUTCOME: The determination of the lower court was modified to declare that all charges, occurring on or after July 24, 1978 and prior to January 1, 1979 were subject to the statute.

Albany v. Lee
