Petitioner then inquired of the zoning board of how to apply for a special permit to operate his shredder. The zoning board of appeals granted petitioner a special permit but he failed to obtain a building permit within six months and it expired. Petitioner filed a motion for declaratory judgment alleging he had relied upon the special permit granted by the zoning board of appeals, and the special term court granted petitioner's request. Appellant planning board asserted that the trial court erred in its finding that operation of the shredder was a permitted use. The court reversed, holding that the zoning board of appeals lacked authority to grant a special permit and petitioner acquired no vested rights through it. The court remanded the matter to the zoning board of appeals for an in-depth hearing as to whether the petitioner's proposed use violated any city code standards by being injurious, hazardous, noxious or offensive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision from the special term court granting petitioner junk dealer's request for a special permit to operate an automobile shredder on his premises. The court held that the zoning board of appeals lacked authority to grant a special permit. The court remanded the matter to the zoning board of appeals for an in-depth hearing as to whether the petitioner's proposed use violated any city code standards.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

1. The operation of an automobile shredder [***4] on petitioner's premises is not a permitted use as of right without the need for a special permit in a light industry zone under section 85-154 of the Brookhaven Town Code, which permits all uses as a matter of right, except 78 enumerated uses and any other use that may be injurious, hazardous, noxious or offensive; since petitioner was improperly advised to proceed through the vehicle of a special permit, an in-depth hearing before the zoning board of appeals is to be held on the issue of whether the proposed use may be injurious, hazardous, noxious or offensive, and if petitioner demonstrates that his proposed use will not be injurious or hazardous, it will have to be decided whether his right to use the shredder vested before the law was changed to prohibit use of an automobile shredder, the change having been effected after petitioner's special permit was granted by the zoning board of appeals, which had no authority to grant it, and after the planning board denied petitioner the use, under the guise of denying site plan approval, following which the special permit expired due to petitioner's failure to obtain a building permit.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Power  [***5] of Planning Board

2. While a planning board may review and approve or disapprove site plans, it does not have power to approve or disapprove a use.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Special Exception

3. The granting of a special exception does not entail making an exception to an ordinance but, rather, permitting certain uses which the ordinance authorizes under stated conditions; a special exception is one allowable when the facts and conditions specified in the ordinance as those upon which the exception is permitted are found to exist.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Vested Rights

4. In determining whether a party has acquired vested rights to a building permit prior to a zoning resolution amendment which would prohibit such use, (1) where no permit has been issued, the party is entitled to the permit when the public officials in question willfully withhold and refuse to issue the permit and, in addition, mislead and hinder him, to the end that if they had acted with reasonable promptness his permit would have been granted and he could have conducted the business in question and thereby could have acquired a vested right prior to the zoning amendment,  [***6] and (2) where a permit has been issued, vested rights are acquired where the property owner has commenced work of a substantial character on the property prior to the amendment.

 

1088 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


69 A.D.2d 536; 418 N.Y.S.2d 932; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11834



July 30, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Trial order of dismissal of the County Court, Rockland County, made September 27, 1978, affirmed.

Appeal from an order of suppression of the same court, dated November 17, 1978, dismissed as academic in light of the determination on the appeal from the trial order of dismissal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of orders of the Rockland County Court (New York), which suppressed certain evidence and dismissed the state's action against defendant on an indictment for sale of drugs. The state contended that, pursuant to recent case law, its appeal was not barred by the principles of double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: Before trial, the trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence contained in tapes made by police listening to his conversations with a confidential informant. At trial, the state sought to introduce the officer's testimony regarding the conversations, but that motion was denied. The state announced it could not proceed without the evidence, and sought to hold the trial in abeyance pending an appeal of the evidentiary ruling. The trial court stated that double jeopardy had attached and dismissed the action. On appeal, the court affirmed because the trial court's termination of the proceedings favorably to defendant was made on a basis related to his guilt or innocence and thus a retrial was barred. The court found that recent case law addressing double jeopardy only dealt with circumstances where the defendant sought to have the trial terminated. Because defendant had not sought the dismissal, the state was barred from bringing the action again. The court rejected the state's contention that the trial court should have declared a mistrial, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 280.10, because the exclusion of the officer's testimony did not occur before the trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the indictment against defendant. Because the dismissal barred further prosecution of defendant for the same offense the court dismissed the appeal of the order of suppression as academic.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

1. A trial order of dismissal made after the jury had been impaneled and sworn, and after the prosecutor stated that the People's proof had been rendered so weak that the People could not proceed to trial since the court had denied the prosecutor's request that a certain police officer be allowed to testify as to conversations overheard by means of a transmitter, the tapes of the conversations having been suppressed, is affirmed, since although it is questionable [***4] whether the dismissal of the indictment is a trial order of dismissal within the meaning of CPL 290.10 and 450.20 (subd 2), the Trial Judge's termination of the proceedings favorably to the defendant was made on a basis related to the defendant's guilt or innocence and, therefore, a retrial of the defendant is barred.

Judges -- Characterization of Judge's Action

2. A Trial Judge's characterization of his own action cannot control the classification of the action.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

3. In a jury trial, jeopardy attaches when the jury is impaneled and sworn.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

4. The People are precluded from taking an appeal from an adverse trial ruling whenever such appeal if resolved favorably for the People might require the defendant to stand retrial, or even if it would then be necessary for the trial court to make supplemental findings; double jeopardy principles will bar appeal unless there is available a determination of guilt which without more may be reinstated in the event of a reversal and remand.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

5. A defendant who deliberately chooses to seek termination of criminal proceedings against [***5] him on a basis unrelated to factual guilt or innocence of the offense of which he is accused, suffers no injury cognizable under the double jeopardy clause if the government is permitted to appeal from such a ruling of the trial court in favor of the defendant; moreover, the same reasoning applies where the defendant, instead of obtaining a reversal of his conviction on appeal, obtains the termination of the proceedings against him in the trial court without any finding by a court or Jury as to his guilt or innocence.

 

1089 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Arthur Treacher's Fish & Chips, Inc. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


69 A.D.2d 550; 419 N.Y.S.2d 768; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11836



July 26, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified by vacating the warrant issued November 21, 1977, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York) that dismissed its petition to annul a notice and demand for payment of sales and use taxes determined due under N.Y. Tax Law § 1141(c), and to vacate a warrant issued under N.Y. Tax Law § 1138 to execute on the assessment. The trial court had remanded for an administrative hearing before respondent tax commission.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer bought out its franchisee. The tax commission issued a notice and demand for payment of sales and use taxes due to the taxpayer pursuant to N.Y. Tax. Law § 1141(c). The court affirmed that portion of the judgment that found the taxpayer liable for the sales and use taxes not paid by the franchisee because the taxpayer had the duty to collect the taxes from the franchisee upon the buy out, and its failure to notify the tax commission of the sale 10 days prior thereto rendered the taxpayer personally liable under § 1141(c). N.Y. Tax. Law § 1141(b) which authorized the tax commission to issue a warrant against the liable taxpayer did not violate procedural due process because it only effected a lien on the taxpayer's property to secure payment, and it did not cause a seizure of that property or deprive the taxpayer of its incidents of ownership. But the court did find § 1141(b) unconstitutional because it deprived the taxpayer of its property without due process of law to the extent that there was not required a prompt post levy hearing to determine the validity of the tax commission's warrant against the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that found the taxpayer was liable for sales and use tax found due, but it modified the judgment to vacate the warrant issued to execute on that assessment.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Assessment against Purchaser in Bulk

1. A notice and demand for payment of sales and use taxes due was properly issued against petitioner, the purchaser of several restaurants, since petitioner failed to comply with subdivision (c) of section 1141 of the Tax Law, which requires a bulk purchaser to notify the State Tax Commission of the proposed sale at least 10 days before taking possession, and therefore became personally liable for the seller's unpaid sales taxes, which liability may be assessed and enforced in the same manner as the liability for tax under article 28 of the Tax Law; moreover, since subdivision (a) of section 1138 of the Tax Law provides that where a return required [***2] by article 28 is not filed or is incorrectly filed, the State Tax Commission may issue a notice of determination to the person liable for the collection or payment of the tax, and since the seller filed incorrect sales tax returns for the period in question, the Tax Commission was authorized to issue the notice against petitioner.

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Warrant against Person Liable for Tax

2. Although the issuance and filing of a warrant ( Tax Law, § 1141, subd [b]) against petitioner, a bulk sales purchaser who failed to comply with subdivision (c) of section 1141 of the Tax Law, which requires a bulk purchaser to notify the State Tax Commission of the proposed sale at least 10 days before taking possession, and therefore became personally liable for the seller's unpaid sales taxes, did not in and of themselves violate procedural due process since there was no physical seizure of petitioner's property subject to the warrant and since petitioner has been deprived neither of the use or possession of its property nor of the incidents of ownership, subdivision (b) of section 1141 of the Tax Law is unconstitutional in that it amounts to a deprivation of property [***3] without due process insofar as it fails to provide the opportunity for at least a prompt postlevy hearing into the probable validity of the State Tax Commission's claim against petitioner; although the State's interest in collecting revenues is sufficiently important to justify seizure of a taxpayer's assets without a preseizure hearing, it is very doubtful that this interest justifies a seizure causing irreparable injury without a preseizure hearing or a prompt postdeprivation hearing into the basis underlying the State Tax Commission's assessment.

 

1090 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tindal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


69 A.D.2d 58; 418 N.Y.S.2d 815; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11326



July 13, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously modified and, as modified, affirmed, in accordance with opinion by Simons, J., and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Monroe County, for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which suppressed the victim's identification of defendant, subsequent admissions made by defendant, and physical evidence obtained from him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with assault. He filed a motion to suppress the victim's identification of him, as well as admissions he had made to the police and physical evidence obtained from him. The trial court granted the motion, and the state sought review. The court modified the suppression order. The court held that the evidence of the victim's identification of defendant was properly suppressed because the identification procedure was impermissibly suggestive. However, the court held that defendant's admissions and the physical evidence obtained from him were improperly suppressed. The court said that this later-acquired evidence was not the product of any illegal police activity that violated defendant's constitutional rights and that no deterrent purpose was to be served by suppressing it. The court concluded that because there had been no illegal police conduct, there was nothing to "poison" the evidence subsequently but lawfully acquired. Once the victim's identification was removed from the case, the court said, the prosecution could go forward on the basis of the other evidence in the case whether acquired before or after her identification.


OUTCOME: The court modified an order that suppressed the victim's identification of defendant, as well as the state's evidence of admissions made by defendant and physical evidence obtained from him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant

1. The procedure by which a rape victim identified defendant as her attacker from five photographs one of which was that of defendant and which was distinguishable from the other photographs, the victim having previously selected the photographs of defendant and another from the police files, was impermissibly suggestive and any identification based thereon, including the victim's subsequent identification of defendant from a lineup, must be suppressed; furthermore, in the absence of clear and convincing proof of an independent source free from the taint of the photograph array, the victim's in-court identification must also be suppressed.

Crimes -- Confession -- Unlawful  [***2] Search and Seizure

2. Although the procedure by which a rape victim identified defendant as her attacker from photographs was impermissibly suggestive and subsequent lineup and in-court identifications were tainted thereby and must also be suppressed, admissions by defendant and physical evidence obtained from him should not have been suppressed; there having been no illegal police conduct that violated defendant's constitutional rights, the lawfully acquired admissions and physical evidence were not the fruit of the poisonous tree; there was no primary taint to be dissipated and once the identification had been removed from the case, the prosecution could go forward on the basis of the other evidence.

 

1091 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Lindner


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


71 A.D.2d 829; 419 N.Y.S.2d 375; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13058



 July 13, 1979 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison inmates sought review of a decision from the Court at Special Term (New York) that denied applications by the inmates for the appointment of two physicians for mental examinations in accordance with N.Y. Correct. Law § 402.


OVERVIEW: Two prison inmates applied for the appointment of physicians for mental examinations in relation to the emergency commitment procedure for transferring allegedly mentally ill prison inmates to a psychiatric center. The inmates were transferred by the acting superintendent of the correctional facility to a psychiatric center pursuant to N.Y. Correct. Law § 402(9). Under the provision, an emergency transfer of an allegedly mental ill prisoner was permitted only upon the certification of two examining physicians that the inmate suffered from a mental illness which was likely to result in serious harm to himself or others. The procedures utilized for the nonemergency transfers were to be immediately commenced. Subsequent to the transfer of the inmates, an application for the appointment of the physicians was denied by the lower court which held the provision was unconstitutional. Upon review, the court held that the section was not unconstitutional. The court reversed the decision of the lower court and granted the application for the appointment of the physicians.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court and granted the application for the appointment of the physicians to examine the inmates.

 

1092 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Childs v. Childs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


69 A.D.2d 406; 419 N.Y.S.2d 533; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11822



July 11, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Order dated December 30, 1976 reversed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.

Order dated July 11, 1978 modified by deleting therefrom the provision which granted plaintiff's application for a judgment representing arrears in counsel fees. As so modified, said order affirmed, without costs or disbursements.

This case is remitted to the Supreme Court, Westchester County, for further proceeding in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant ex-husband sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which awarded counsel fees to plaintiff ex-wife in a child custody dispute, and awarded the ex-wife a judgment representing arrears in counsel fees under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 237.


OVERVIEW: In a child custody dispute, the trial court awarded plaintiff ex-wife her counsel fees to be paid by defendant ex-husband under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 237, and subsequently awarded the ex-wife a judgment representing arrears when the ex-husband failed to pay the fees pending appeal. While the ex-husband's appeal was pending, a similar statute was declared unconstitutional, and thus, the court was required to decide the constitutionality of § 237. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment awarding counsel fees to the ex-wife, and modified the order granting the ex-wife the arrears judgment. The court held that § 237 was constitutional insofar as it authorized the trial court to award counsel fees to either party on a gender-neutral, needs-only basis. The court further held that law should be prospective only to proceedings and applications pending on March 5, 1979.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order awarding plaintiff ex-wife counsel fees to be paid by defendant ex-husband as a result of a child custody suit. The court modified an order awarding the ex-wife an arrears judgment against the ex-husband for unpaid counsel fees.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Counsel Fees for Wives Only in Matrimonial Actions

1. Although section 237 of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that in actions for annulment or divorce and upon applications to modify alimony or custody,  [***4] visitation or maintenance of a child, the court may direct the husband or father to pay counsel fees, and section 244 of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that where the husband, in an action for divorce, separation or annulment, defaults in paying any required sum, the court may order entry of judgment for the amount of the arrears, must fall, in light of Orr v Orr (440 U.S. 268), which struck down as violative of the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution an Alabama statutory scheme which permitted alimony to be granted only to wives, since they grant counsel fees purely as a function of gender, they need not be deemed irrevocably unconstitutional and annulled, inasmuch as an appellate court has an obligation to pursue every reasonable path of reconciliation of a challenged statute with the Constitution, rather than setting it aside; sections 237 and 244 of the Domestic Relations Law are deemed to authorize counsel fees to either spouse on a gender-neutral, needs-only basis, since without such an expansive reading of the statutes, the orderly process by which indigent spouses seek legal recourse would come to a halt producing extremely inequitable [***5] circumstances. Additional authority for this expansive reading of the statutes is found in section 238 of the Domestic Relations Law, which presently allows the trial court, in enforcement proceedings, to require either party to pay the expenses of the other, including counsel fees, and in CPLR 8303 (subd [a], par 2), which provides for the granting by the court of a discretionary allowance to any party to a difficult or extraordinary case, where a defense has been interposed.

Husband and Wife -- Counsel Fees -- Retroactivity

2. The construction of section 237 of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that in actions for annulment or divorce and upon applications to modify alimony or custody, visitation or maintenance of a child, the court may direct the husband or father to pay counsel fees, as authorizing counsel fees on a gender-neutral needs-only basis in light of Orr v Orr (440 U.S. 268), which invalidated an Alabama statutory scheme providing alimony for wives only as violative of the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution, shall have prospective effect only in proceedings pending on the date of the Orr decision, March 5, 1979, and [***6] all other awards of counsel fees which had been conclusively adjudicated as of that date shall not be affected by this construction, since retroactive application prior to March 5, 1979 would produce chaotic, inequitable results.

Parties -- Standing

3. In a postdivorce custody proceeding commenced by the defendant husband, wherein the plaintiff wife was awarded counsel fees and disbursements, the defendant has standing to raise the issue of the constitutionality of section 237 of the Domestic Relations Law, which provides that in actions for annulment or divorce and upon applications to modify alimony or custody, visitation or maintenance of a child the court may direct the husband or father to pay counsel fees, since the Supreme Court of the United States, on a writ of certiorari to that court, ordered that the case be reconsidered in light of Orr v Orr (440 U.S. 268), which struck down as violative of the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution an Alabama statutory scheme which permitted alimony to be granted only to wives, and held that the burden borne by the plaintiff in that case, a man, which he would not bear were he female, was sufficient [***7] to establish standing; the gravity of the issue being beyond doubt in light of Orr, the Appellate Division may reconsider the question upon remand despite the defendant's failure to request the award or raise the issue at trial.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Alimony for Wives Only

4. A State statutory scheme which imposes alimony obligations on husbands but not wives violates the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution; to withstand scrutiny under the equal protection clause, classifications by gender must serve important governmental objectives and must be substantially related to achievement of those objectives, and a statutory scheme may not be upheld on the basis of the State's preference for an allocation of family responsibility under which the wife plays merely a submissive dependent role.

Statutes -- Construction

5. An appellate court has an obligation to pursue every reasonable path of reconciliation of a challenged statute consonant with the Constitution; every presumption in favor of the statute will be indulged, and every effort will be made to construe ambiguous language so [***8] as not to conflict with fundamental law.

Statutes -- Construction

6. While courts traditionally refrain from construing a challenged statute in such a way as to expand or add to the language as opposed to merely excising from it, nevertheless, under appropriate circumstances, a court may read a statute expansively, or in tandem with other statutes, so as to prevent serious damage to overriding public policy.

Statutes -- Construction

7. Where a statute has been found to violate the equal protection clause, courts will traditionally read into it the improperly excluded class.

 

1093 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re P.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


68 A.D.2d 719; 418 N.Y.S.2d 597; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11310



June 28, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Order, Family Court, New York County, entered on or about December 5, 1977, and order of said court, entered on January 13, 1978, reversed, on the law, and the case remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion of this court filed herein, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State of New York sought review of two orders of the Family Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed a charge of prostitution against respondent juvenile based upon a finding that N.Y. Penal Law §§ 130.00 and 230.00 violated equal protection, and which dismissed a delinquency proceeding against respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was a juvenile who allegedly approached a man, offered to perform sexual acts in exchange for money, then took the man to a hotel room where he was beaten and robbed. The State charged respondent with robbery, assault, and prostitution in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 230.00. At the trial on the prostitution charge, respondent filed a motion to dismiss, which was granted. Subsequently, the trial court also dismissed the delinquency proceeding on the robbery charge, finding respondent had been denied her right to a speedy trial because more than two years had passed since respondent's arrest. On appeal, the court reviewed the record and concluded that § 230.00 was not an invasion of any constitutionally protected right of privacy because there was no familial or quasi-familial interest to be protected. The court also found that respondent's claim that the prostitution statute violated the equal protection clause in that it was discriminatorily enforced on the basis of sex was without warrant in law and was not properly challenged without an evidentiary hearing. The court further held that there had been no unnecessary period of delay under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 30.30.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the family court and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court held that respondent was not denied her right to a speedy trial, and that the family court improperly declared the penal statute unconstitutional since the family court failed to conduct an evidentiary hearing to establish that the statute criminalizing prostitution was discriminatorily enforced.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Family Court -- Juvenile Delinquents

1. The exclusive original jurisdiction conferred upon the Family Court over persons alleged to be juvenile delinquents (Family Ct Act, § 713) is controlled by the age of the respondent at the time the delinquent act allegedly was done (Family Ct Act, § 714, subd [a]) and not by the age of the respondent at the time of the hearing.

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions

2. In a case which raises matters of constitutional dimension and grave public interest, important in the context of juvenile justice and bearing upon the operation of the criminal justice system, which matters are apt to recur, albeit in fact patterns that may vary in degree, an appellate court may properly decline to dismiss an appeal as moot and, instead, resolve [***4] the merits.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Right to Speedy Trial

3. While the right to a speedy and public trial, as defined by the Federal Constitution (US Const, 6th, 14th Amdts) and by State statutes (Civil Rights Law, § 12; CPL 30.20), speaks only of criminal proceedings, it would take a distorted view to believe that adult felony criminal proceedings were designed to be more tender of the rights of detained adults than the Family Court proceedings are of juveniles; indeed, so important does this State consider the right, that it has encompassed it within a specific time frame. ( CPL 30.30.)

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial

4. In determining whether the constitutional right to a speedy trial has been infringed, there must be a balancing of factors including the extent of delay, the reason for the delay, the nature of the underlying charge, whether or not there has been an extended period of pretrial incarceration, and whether or not there is any indication that the defense has been impaired by reason of the delay; mere passage of time is not, in and of itself, sufficient to trigger the constitutional right to dismissal upon the ground that a speedy trial [***5] has been denied. Accordingly, in the absence of a hearing demonstrating consideration and balancing of these factors, there is no basis for holding that a defendant's constitutional right to a speedy trial has been infringed.

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial

5. The time period after which a person charged becomes entitled to a dismissal for failure to accord her a speedy trial excludes a reasonable period of delay resulting from other proceedings concerning the defendant, including but not limited to pretrial motions and the period during which such matters are under consideration by the court ( CPL 30.30, subd 4, par [a]). Accordingly, dismissal of a juvenile delinquency proceeding on the ground that respondent has been deprived of a speedy trial is improper where, when the period between the time respondent's Law Guardian moved to dismiss a charge of prostitution and the time it was finally submitted and decided is excluded, the period of delay does not exceed the time limitations of CPL 30.30, since it is, therefore, plain that there was no infringement of respondent's statutory right to a speedy trial, even though, at the time her fact-finding hearing was held in [***6] Family Court, the juvenile delinquency charges against her had been pending for more than 10 months.

Appeal -- Matters Reviewable

6. Although section 1112 of the Family Court Act provides that appeal may be taken as of right in juvenile delinquency proceedings only from an order of disposition and in the discretion of the appropriate Appellate Division from any other order in such proceedings, section 1118 of the Family Court Act makes the provisions of the CPLR, where appropriate, applicable to appeals from the Family Court. Accordingly, while an order of the Family Court, dismissing a charge of sexual misconduct against respondent prior to the final judgment dismissing the juvenile delinquency proceeding against her, is not a final order of disposition and could not be the subject of an appeal as of right, it is, nonetheless, reviewable as an intermediate order, upon an appeal from the final order, since under CPLR 5501 (subd [a], par 1), an appeal from a final judgment brings up for review any nonfinal order which necessarily effects the final judgment, provided that such nonfinal order has not previously been reviewed by the court to which the appeal is taken.

 [***7] Constitutional Law -- Reaching Issue of Constitutionality

7. Constitutional questions should not be reached unless there is a need for their determination to resolve the issue at hand and the question is squarely presented. Accordingly, upon an allegation that respondent offered to perform a deviate sexual act for United States currency, which allegation merely particularizes the sexual conduct in which respondent offered to engage so as to bring the charge within the ambit of the definition of prostitution, the Family Court erred in reaching out to consider the constitutionality of statutes defining deviate sexual intercourse (Penal Law, § 130.00, subd 2) and consensual sodomy (Penal Law, § 130.38); no need for resolution of these issues existed, nor was the issue presented, since, were respondent to be found guilty of the sexual misconduct with which she is charged, the finding could only be that she had performed acts which, if committed by an adult, would constitute prostitution.

Constitutional Law -- Right of Privacy

8. The right of privacy encompasses and protects the personal intimacies of the home, the family, marriage, motherhood, procreation and child [***8] rearing; such right is limited to those acts deemed fundamental or implicit in the concept of ordered liberty, and does not exempt from State regulation those matters in which government has a legitimate interest. Clearly, the alleged right to use the public streets to solicit, agree or offer to engage in sexual conduct with another person for a fee is hardly one to be deemed fundamental or implicit in the concept of ordered liberty, and the important State interest in proscribing such conduct is emphasized where the offeror is a 14-year-old child; accordingly, section 230.00 of the Penal Law, the statute criminalizing prostitution, is not an invasion of any constitutionally protected right of privacy, since there is no familial or quasi-familial interest to be protected and the act is, therefore, well within the reach of the police power of the State.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws

9. The claim of unequal protection is treated not as an affirmative defense to criminal prosecution or the imposition of a regulatory sanction, but as a motion to dismiss or quash the official action and, thus, an evidentiary hearing is required so that the court may determine [***9] the constitutional issue.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws

10. To sustain a claim of unequal protection with respect to a criminal statute, a showing is required that the law, fair on its face, is enforced consciously and deliberately against some and that, with knowledge that a crime has been committed by others, there is an intentional and premeditated abstention from enforcing it against others, which selective application is deliberately based upon an impermissible standard such as race, religion or some other arbitrary classification; the conscious exercise of some selectivity in enforcement of the law is not, in itself, a constitutional violation. Accordingly, although classification based upon sex is clearly suspect under the equal protection clause, where the preliminary step necessary to the establishment of selective application on that basis -- an evidentiary hearing -- is not conducted and respondent fails to show selective application, which is not demonstrated by a discrepancy between the number of females prosecuted for prostitution and the number of males prosecuted for patronizing a prostitute, as they are discrete crimes necessitating separate [***10] acts to effect their commission, a holding that section 230.00 of the Penal Law, the prostitution statute, was discriminatorily enforced is, under the circumstances, without warrant in law.

 

1094 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Falcone v. State Education Dep't


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


68 A.D.2d 470; 418 N.Y.S.2d 169; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10964



June 21, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Determination modified by annulling so much thereof as suspends petitioner's license for two years and stays execution of the suspension for the last 18 months and substituting therefor a provision suspending petitioner's license for a period of 18 months with the suspension stayed and petitioner placed on probation for the period of 18 months, and, as so modified, confirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner optometrist brought a proceeding initiated in the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial District (New York) pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6510(4) to annul a determination of respondent education commissioner, which suspended the optometrist's license to practice optometry for two years based on professional misconduct in violation of N.Y. Educ. Law § 6509(7), (9).


OVERVIEW: The optometrist, in the course of his practice, employed an ophthalmic dispenser whom he allowed to fit contact lenses, which was prohibited by N.Y. Educ. Law § 7121. The optometrist was charged with professional misconduct because of the employment upon the grounds that he was permitting or aiding and abetting an unlicensed person to perform activities requiring a license and/or engaging in unprofessional conduct, in violation of N.Y. Educ. Law § 6509(7), (9). The education commissioner suspended the optometrist's license for two years, and he petitioned for review. The court annulled the two-year suspension and imposed an 18-month stayed suspension because the record did not disclose any reason to believe that the employee was exceeding his training or field of specialty in view of his ability to act under the supervision of a physician. Further, there was no showing that the misconduct was harmful to the public. Since the error was at most a technical violation of the Education Law, the punishment should not have been more than technical. Thus, the punishment imposed was shocking and bore no rational relationship to the nature of the conduct charged.


OUTCOME: The court modified the education commissioner's determination by annulling the two-year suspension of the optometrist's license, and instead suspended the license for 18 months with the suspension stayed.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- Optometrist

1. A determination suspending the license of an optometrist for 2 years and staying execution thereof for 18 months for employing an ophthalmic dispenser whom he allowed to fit contact lenses in violation of section 7121 of the Education Law, which provides that contact lenses may be fitted by an ophthalmic dispenser only under the personal supervision of a licensed physician, is modified by substituting an 18-month suspension with [***2] the suspension stayed and the optometrist is placed on probation for 18 months; while there was substantial evidence to establish the charge, the 6-month absolute suspension of the license is shocking and bears no rational relationship to the technical violation of the statute, there having been no showing that the misconduct was harmful to the public.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Constitutional Question

2. The issue as to whether section 7121 of the Education Law, which provides that contact lenses may be fitted by an ophthalmic dispenser only under the personal supervision of a licensed physician, is unconstitutional may not properly be treated in a CPLR article 78 proceeding and while the court has the power to convert the matter to a declaratory judgment action, the merits of the allegation of unconstitutionality will not be considered inasmuch as the proceeding does not disclose a justiciable controversy.

Kaplan ex rel. Bonilla v. Bell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


70 A.D.2d 822; 417 N.Y.S.2d 687; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12377

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In his N.Y. C.P.L.R 78 proceeding, petitioner youth filed a motion seeking to declare unconstitutional certain portions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10, to enjoin vacatur of youth's guilty plea to manslaughter in the first degree, and to enjoin the trial of the youth upon the charge of murder in the second degree. The youth's guilty plea to the lesser crime was accepted when everyone mistakenly believed that he was 14, when he was only 13.


OVERVIEW: Under N.Y. Penal Law § 30.00(2), a 13 year old could only be criminally responsible for acts constituting murder in the second degree, and therefore could not plead guilty to a lesser crime, in accordance with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 220.10(5)(i). Petitioner secured no relief. C.P.L.R 7803.

1096 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


68 A.D.2d 503; 417 N.Y.S.2d 966; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10969



June 18, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, rendered May 30, 1975, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York), which convicted defendant for second-degree escape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court erroneously excluded evidence that substantiated the defenses of justification and duress, which would have shown that his escape from prison was motivated by threats on his life made by prison guards and other inmates. Defendant also argued that conditions in the prison were intolerable and that the trial court failed to charge the jury with justification. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that defendant was required to show that he was in imminent danger of physical injury when he escaped in order to assert the defenses of justification and duress, which defendant had not shown. The court further held that although conditions in a prison might be unhealthy, such conditions alone did not justify an escape and that if defendant did escape based upon a justified reason then defendant was required to turn himself into law enforcement officials immediately, which defendant did not do.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of second-degree escape.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Escape -- Defenses of Justification and Duress

Although a defendant prosecuted for escape from a penal institution can raise the defense of justification (Penal Law, § 35.05) if in fact the escape was compelled by the existence of conditions posing an imminent danger of personal injury to the prisoner, which cannot be avoided by the defendant through resort to the authorities or other legal means, and if the defendant surrenders to custody within a reasonably prompt time, a prisoner who escaped from a hospital where he was undergoing treatment and remained at liberty for almost one week until he was apprehended was not under any imminent danger of personal injury at the time of his escape, and his offer of proof of threats by prison guards, the existence of litigation challenging the conditions in the prison he was confined in, and psychiatric testimony concerning pressure on an individual confined under [***2] unhealthful prison conditions to escape, does not satisfy the tests which the defense of justification demands and was therefore properly excluded; the defense of duress (Penal Law, § 40.00) was also unavailable under the circumstances of the case, since no physical force or threat of physical force was exercised on the defendant at the time of the escape, and, accordingly, defendant was properly convicted of escape in the second degree.

Karp v. Karp


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


70 A.D.2d 813; 417 N.Y.S.2d 481; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12361



June 14, 1979 

 

1098 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Quinton A.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


68 A.D.2d 394; 417 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10952



June 11, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Two orders of the Family Court, Kings County, one in each proceeding, both dated March 17, 1978, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juveniles sought review of the orders of the Family Court, Kings County (New York). The orders were based on findings that the juveniles had committed acts, which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted the crimes of robbery in the first degree, a "designated felony act" under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 712(h), and burglary in the second degree. The trial court ordered that they be placed restrictively with the youth division.


OVERVIEW: There were two issues in the action: whether the age of the victim was proven to be 62 years or older; and whether the provisions of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 712(h) para. ii, 753-a(2-a) et seq., requiring "restrictive placement" of a youth 14 or 15 years of age for committing a "designated felony act" of robbery in the first degree, violated the juveniles' constitutional rights. The juveniles were placed in mandatory restrictive placement because they had inflicted serious physical injury upon the victim, who was over 62 years of age. The trial court was justified in concluding that the assistant director had authority to certify the hospital record and that the entry in the record, that complainant was 73, was admissible. The Legislature determined that youths who perpetrated acts of robbery and assault upon elderly persons had to reside in a residential and secure facility for a period of time, both to protect society and to enable the division for youth to provide intensive and concentrated rehabilitative services in a controlled setting. That was not violative of the juveniles' right to procedural due process. There was a rational basis justifying the restrictive placement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Evidence -- Hospital Records

1. There was proof beyond a reasonable doubt that the age of a robbery victim was 62 years of age or more where the Family Court based its conclusion on a statement that she was 73 years of age contained in the hospital record of her medical treatment resulting from the attack, which record was admitted into evidence (CPLR 4518, subd [c]), and the Trial Judge's statement at the dispositional hearing that based on his personal observation of the victim's appearance at the fact-finding hearing, she was over 62 years of age; it was [***3] proper to admit the hospital record into evidence without a notarized signature of the assistant director of the hospital who certified it since CPLR 4518 (subd [c]) contains no requirement that the signature of the authenticator be notarized, nor need a witness be called to testify that the record was kept in the ordinary course of business as the purpose of CPLR 4518 (subd [c]) is to allow the admission into evidence of municipal hospital records or copies thereof without calling such a witness (CPLR 2306, 2307), and the Family Court was justified in concluding that the assistant director of the hospital had authority to certify the hospital record (CPLR 4518, subd [c]; 2306, subd [a]); the entry in the hospital record that the victim was 73 years of age was properly admitted in evidence inasmuch as a patient's history, necessary or helpful to the observation, diagnosis and treatment of the patient, is ordinarily admissible as part of the hospital record and, in view of the injuries inflicted upon the victim during the robbery, knowledge of her age was a relevant factor in the diagnosis and treatment by the hospital's medical staff.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents

 [***4] 2. Subdivision 2-a of section 753-a of the Family Court Act, which requires restrictive placement of a youth 14 or 15 years of age for committing a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person who is 62 years of age or more, does not eliminate the need for a dispositional hearing nor render it a nullity (Family Ct Act, § 753-a, subd 1) so as to deny a youth his right to procedural due process of law; moreover, although subdivision 2-a mandates restrictive placement, if the Family Court determines prior to the entry of its dispositional order that a juvenile does not require supervision, treatment or confinement, it may then grant a new fact-finding hearing (Family Ct Act, § 761) and dismiss the petition if it believes the allegations contained therein are not established. (Family Ct Act, § 751.)

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents

3. Subdivision 2-a of section 753-a of the Family Court Act, which requires restrictive placement of a youth 14 or 15 years of age for committing a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person who is 62 years of age or more, does not violate substantive due [***5] process upon the grounds that it denies the right to appropriate treatment in the least restrictive alternative as a quid pro quo for the juvenile's loss of liberty inasmuch as the Legislature, disturbed by its prior unsatisfactory experience with the lenient treatment of delinquents, had the constitutional authority to enact subdivision 2-a requiring that rehabilitative treatment be administered for this narrow class of delinquents within a framework of restrictive placement as a means of reconciling the legitimate interest of youth in their liberty with the interest of society in its own protection.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents

4. Subdivision 2-a of section 753-a of the Family Court Act, which requires restrictive placement of a youth 14 or 15 years of age for committing a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person who is 62 years of age or more, is not violative of equal protection on the grounds that no rational basis or compelling interest justifies the alleged discrimination perpetrated on the youths in the form of restrictive placement inasmuch as segments of legislative reports submitted by the Attorney-General [***6] on appeal reveal a high incidence of crime against the elderly in New York City in recent years and the relation of juveniles as perpetrators of such crimes.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents

5. Subdivision 2-a of section 753-a of the Family Court Act, which requires restrictive placement of a youth 14 or 15 years of age for committing a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person who is 62 years of age or more, does not violate the youth's equal protection rights because he is not accorded dispositional rights and options equivalent to youthful offender treatment or probation inasmuch as the possibility that the court processing felony cases might place on probation a minor over 16 years of age found guilty of the same conduct for which the youth was held responsible pales in significance when it is realized that a sentence of imprisonment may be imposed upon a youthful offender (Penal Law, § 60.02) and a youthful offender may be subjected to a public trial in the felony court which, unlike juvenile delinquency proceedings, is clearly a criminal process; furthermore, the court must sentence the youthful offender to a term of imprisonment [***7] should probation be revoked. (Penal Law, § 60.01, subd 4; § 60.02.)

Richard's Service Station, Inc. v. Huntington

 June 11, 1979 


CASE SUMMARY:

People v. Robinson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


68 A.D.2d 413; 417 N.Y.S.2d 88; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10954



June 7, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered July 13, 1976, modified, on the law, to reduce the term to one year to life imprisonment, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which, upon a verdict convicting her of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree, sentenced her to the mandatory term minimum of six years to her natural life. Defendant contended that the application of a mandatory sentence was unconstitutionally because the extent of her involvement in her husband's heroin trade was slight.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree. The record showed that it was clear that she was utilized in the narcotic transaction by her husband as a messenger to convey the package containing the heroin from a location that was apparently the residence of defendant and her husband. The extent of her involvement, other than the aforesaid, was not delineated with any further degree of specificity, but she had no prior record. She was shown to have a low level of intelligence. The trial court held that the law was mandatory that her sentence was a minimum of six years and a maximum of her natural life. Modifying the order sentencing defendant, the court held that defendant's case presented that rare case which on its particular facts compelled the reasoned conclusion that the statutes had been unconstitutionally applied insofar as the sentencing of this defendant was concerned. The trial court's order was modified, on the law, modified, to have reduced defendant's term to one year to life imprisonment, and, as so modified, affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court rendered the trial court's order modified, on the law, to have reduced defendant's term to one year to life imprisonment, and, as so modified, the order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence

A judgment convicting defendant of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree and sentencing her to an indeterminate term of from six years to life imprisonment is modified, on the law, to reduce the term to one year to life imprisonment, where the defendant was used by her codefendant, who was also the father of the couple's two children, as the messenger in a narcotics transaction, there was no further delineation as to the extent of her involvement, and there was some evidence that the defendant was arrested because her codefendant failed to co-operate with the authorities.

Silvian v. Shang


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


70 A.D.2d 704; 416 N.Y.S.2d 671; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12188



May 17, 1979 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant New York State Department of Social Services appealed from part of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which declared N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 460-f and 461-b(7) unconstitutional in plaintiff owners and association's action for a declaratory judgment declaring §§ 460-f and 461-b(7) unconstitutional as in violation of their due process rights under the constitutions of the United States and the State of New York.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought an action against the New York State Department of Social Services (department), seeking a declaratory judgment that §§ 460-f and 461-b(7) were unconstitutional as in violation of their due process rights under the constitutions of the United States and the State of New York by exposing them to prospective criminal sanctions for unenumerated potential crimes declared at the discretion of the department. The court modified the order by deleting the paragraph which stated that those sections were unconstitutional and substituting a paragraph declaring that they were, and as modified affirmed. The court held that while the legislature could not pass on its law-making functions to other bodies, there was no constitutional prohibition against the delegation of power, with reasonable safeguards and standards, to an agency or commission to administer the law as enacted by the legislature. N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 460 was sufficiently specific and clear in establishing the standards and guides for the department to follow in establishing its regulations.


OUTCOME: The court modified the supreme court's by deleting the paragraph declaring the sections of the New York Social Services Law, which stated that violations of those sections or regulations of the New York State Department of Social Services were class A misdemeanors, were unconstitutional and by substituting therefore a paragraph declaring that said sections were constitutional, and as so modified, affirmed.

Aldens, Inc. v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


70 A.D.2d 720; 416 N.Y.S.2d 425; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12208



May 17, 1979 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a taxpayer, sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which dismissed the taxpayer's petition to annul a determination of respondent, the State Tax Commission pursuant to N.Y. C.L.P.R. art. 78.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer operated a nationwide mail-order sales business and maintained local offices in four counties of the state for catalog sales through a subsidiary. The court held that the interpretation of the Commission that the taxpayer was subject to the local sales and use tax provisions of the N.Y. 1105 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Merola on behalf of People v. Bell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


68 A.D.2d 24; 415 N.Y.S.2d 992; 1979 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10513; 5 Media L. Rep. 1033



May 10, 1979 



DISPOSITION: Applications for orders in the nature of writs of prohibition and mandamus denied and the petitions dismissed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on two counts of murder in the second degree. The trial court (New York) granted defendant's motion to bar the public and the press from the courtroom during his suppression hearing. The government filed an application for an order in the nature of a writ of prohibition, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78.


OVERVIEW: The victim was shot and killed while waiting for the subway. Defendant made statements to police that implicated his involvement in the murder. At the hearing on defendant's motion to suppress, numerous print and television reporters crowded the courtroom. While the trial judge originally denied defendant's motion for closure, he later granted the motion, noting the extensive treatment of the story in the media. In denying the government's application, the court found that the trial judge properly exercised his discretion in granting the motion for two reasons. First, closure was necessary to prevent disclosure of defendant's statements to potential jurors. Second, closure obviated the necessity for a retrial, which likely would have been mandated because of the adverse effects of publicity. The court also noted that the case had attracted widespread publicity because defendant was the first 13-year-old to be prosecuted as an adult under recently enacted amendments to the New York Juvenile Law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the government's application for an order in the nature of a writ of prohibition in defendant's murder trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Public Trial -- Exclusion of Press from Suppression Hearing

At the suppression hearing of the first 13-year-old to be prosecuted as an adult under the recently enacted amendments to the Penal Law dealing with juvenile crime, an order excluding the press and the public from the courtroom, except for the immediate members of the family of the defendant and the victim, and making available to the press daily copy of a transcript of the proceedings redacted to omit any statements attributed to defendant was a proper exercise of the court's discretion, in light of the facts that defendant was charged with complicity in the murder of a subway passenger, there had been extensive publicity about the case, and public officials were engaged in a well-publicized campaign against crime in the subways; the right to a public trial is not absolute and must be balanced against other interests which might justify [***2] the closing of the courtroom to the public, and permitting courtroom access to the media, with its capacity to disseminate the details of defendant's confession, creates a reasonable probability of prejudice which would threaten the impaneling of a constitutionally impartial jury.
