Pagliaroli v. Zoning Board of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


66 A.D.2d 997; 411 N.Y.S.2d 767; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14360



December 15, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, village zoning board of appeals, sought review of an order of the Oswego Supreme Court (New York), which upon reargument, reversed its decision that granted the board's motion to dismiss the proceeding brought by petitioner property owners to reverse a decision of the village building inspector that a structure on their lot was a temporary structure prohibited by the zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The property owners sought to review a determination of the board that denied their request for an order reversing a decision of the village building inspector that a structure on their lot was a temporary structure prohibited by the zoning ordinance. Although the owners were represented by counsel before the board, their counsel was not notified of the board's action. The trial court granted the board's motion to dismiss pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(f), holding that the board had duly filed in the office of the village clerk its final decision denying the application and that the proceeding had not been commenced within 30 days after the filing as required by Village of Phoenix, N.Y. Village Law § 7-712(3). On reargument, the court reversed its decision. The trial court held that because the attorney that had represented the owners at the hearing had not been notified of the decision, the 30-day period under § 7-712(3) had not commenced to run. The court reversed and held that no notice requirement existed. The proceeding should have been dismissed since the petition was not served within 30 days from the date of filing in the village clerk's office pursuant to § 7-712(3).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and dismissed the petition.

 

1130 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


T. L. C. Medical Transp. Services, Inc. v. Commissioner of Dep't of Health


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


66 A.D.2d 999; 412 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14362



December 15, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the operator of a registered invalid coach service, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to review a determination of the respondent, the Commissioner of the New York State Department of Health, which found that the operator violated N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 30 and the regulations promulgated thereunder when it transported the victim of an industrial accident to a hospital emergency room.


OVERVIEW: The victim fell 12 to 15 feet from a ladder injuring his right knee and hip. His superior telephoned and requested that the operator transport the victim to the hospital, stating that no emergency existed. An invalid coach was dispatched to the scene of the accident. At the hospital, it was determined that the victim had fractured his hip in five places and suffered a bone chip in his right knew. In affirming the Commissioner's determination that the operator violated N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 30, the court stated that the definition of persons permitted to be transported by an invalid coach service did not include "injured" persons. In so ruling, the court noted that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3001(1), (6) and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 800.22 9(d) specifically prohibited an invalid coach service from providing emergency care in transit or emergency medical service. Emergency medical service was defined as providing initial emergency medical assistance including, but not limited to, the treatment of trauma, burns, respiratory, circulatory, and obstetrical emergencies. In contrast, an "ambulance service" was permitted to transport "sick, disabled or injured persons."


OUTCOME: The court unanimously confirmed the determination.

 

1131 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gulf Oil Corp. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


65 A.D.2d 157; 411 N.Y.S.2d 698; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13072



December 14, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner company filed a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 in the Supreme Court-Special Term, Albany County (New York), which was transferred to the court. The company sought review of a decision made by respondent State Tax Commission, denying the company's application for a re-determination of a tax imposed under N.Y. Tax Law § 181. The trial court affirmed the Commission's determination, and the company sought review.


OVERVIEW: The company issued 300 million shares of capital stock without par value but with a stated value, which were to replace the company's then authorized 150 million shares of par value capital stock. As a result, the Corporation Tax Bureau of the State Tax Commission claimed an additional license fee under N.Y. Tax Law § 181. Thereafter, the Bureau changed the company's license fee allocation percentage. Following a formal hearing, the Commission concluded that the change from par value to no par value stock was a change in capital structure within the meaning and intent of § 181. It further held that the license fee was properly recomputed. The court held that: (1) the company was given credit for the fees it had previously paid so the company had not been taxed twice; (2) § 181 did not violate the Equal Protection Clauses of the state and federal constitutions because there were material differences between the two kinds of stock so classifying them separately for taxation purposes was justified; and (3) it was not unconstitutional for New York to tax the no par value stock of a foreign corporation at a higher rate than similar shares of domestic corporations.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the trial court's determination and dismissed the company's petition.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- License Fee on Foreign Corporations

1. Petitioner, a publicly held, multinational Pennsylvania corporation [***2] qualified to do business in New York, is liable under section 181 of the Tax Law for an additional license fee where it discontinued its shares of par value stock and authorized twice that number of shares of no par value stock at a stated value of one half the value of the discontinued shares, in that the change from par value to no par value stock was a change in capital structure insofar as par value shares establish an arbitrary amount as the value of share interest in the corporation whereas, in contrast, there is greater flexibility in distributing no par value shares in exchange for property and services because no valuation needs to be established for such consideration as long as it is legal consideration for the valid transfer of stock. Furthermore, all money received for par value stock except amounts in excess of par value must be made part of petitioner's stated capital and must be maintained. However, under the laws of Pennsylvania, petitioner's directors may allocate a portion of the consideration received for no par value stock to capital surplus which may be entirely depleted without affecting future earnings; therefore, the utilization of no par value stock provides [***3] directors with greater authority and flexibility to manipulate available surplus to provide for greater dividends and redemption of issue stock.

Taxation -- License Fee on Foreign Corporations

2. Petitioner, a publicly held, multinational Pennsylvania corporation qualified to do business in New York, which discontinued its shares of par value stock and authorized twice that number of shares of no par value stock at a stated value of one half the value of the discontinued shares, is liable for an additional license fee computed on the basis of the total number of no par value shares allocable to New York, as despite petitioner's payment of the license fee on its par value stock, the no par value stock is not exempt from the additional licensing fee insofar as exemptions are allowed only where they are clearly granted by some provision of law and no such exemption is herein applicable; there is no denial of due process as petitioner is entitled to a credit in the amount of the licensing fee previously paid (Tax Law, § 181) and therefore is not being taxed twice with regard to its no par value shares which existed as par value shares before the stock split.

Taxation  [***4] -- License Fees on Foreign Corporations

3. Section 181 of the Tax Law, which provides a different method for the computation of licensing fees as between par value and no par value capital stock, does not violate the equal protection clauses of the United States Constitution and the Constitution of the State of New York as there are material differences between par and no par value stock, and, consequently, the Legislature can justifiably classify them separately for purposes of taxation; furthermore, it is not unconstitutional for the State of New York to tax the no par value stock of foreign corporations at a higher rate than similar shares of domestic corporations.

 

1132 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Haitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


65 A.D.2d 172; 411 N.Y.S.2d 57; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13404



December 8, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Genesee County Court (New York), admitting testimony of his refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194 and convicting him after a jury trial of driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving while intoxicated and refused to submit to a breathalyzer test. At trial, comments were made by plaintiff state, witnesses, and the trial court concerning his refusal. Defendant was convicted of driving while intoxicated. On appeal, defendant challenged N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194, which permitted the admission at trial of testimony of his refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test, as violative of his privilege against self-incrimination. The court affirmed his conviction. U.S. Const. amend. V was not a barrier to admission of defendant's refusal. The privilege against self-incrimination did not attach because defendant possessed no constitutional right to deny plaintiff physical evidence. Plaintiff had a right to obtain the chemical test. Refusal was merely a statutory option. Further, the refusal was admissible to show consciousness of guilt.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed because admitting his refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test did not violate his constitutional privilege against self-incrimination.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Refusal to Submit to Breathalyzer Test

1. Section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which permits the admission at trial of testimony of a defendant's refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test for intoxication, is not unconstitutional as violative of the Fifth Amendment privilege against self incrimination since the State has a clear right to obtain the test results; no religious scruple or claim of pain or danger can seriously be alleged as a reason to refuse a breathalyzer test; the admission of defendant's refusal is not a penalty for his exercise of a constitutional right, for he has no constitutional right to refuse to take the test; conduct of an accused indicative of a guilty mind is admissible against him on the theory that an inference of guilt may be drawn from consciousness of guilt and the admission of refusal evidence is in accordance with the current national trend.

Motor Vehicles --  [***2] Chemical Tests -- Miranda Warning

2. The failure to apprise a person, arrested for driving while intoxicated, of his Miranda rights does not bar the admission of evidence that he refused to take a breathalyzer test.

 

1133 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alvarez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


65 A.D.2d 146; 410 N.Y.S.2d 840; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13071



December 7, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered on April 20, 1977, unanimously reversed, on the law, and as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which affirmed defendant's conviction for the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree and received a sentence of imprisonment for six years to life.


OVERVIEW: A confidential informant arranged to have a police officer purchase cocaine from an alleged drug dealer, but the dealer failed to appear at the informant's apartment. The informant and officer then met defendant, the dealer's girlfriend, by chance on the street. Defendant arranged a meeting with the dealer for them at her apartment, where defendant handed the officer a bag of cocaine. Defendant was convicted of criminal sale of cocaine. On appeal, the court found that defendant was deprived of a fair trial because the prosecution failed to make a diligent effort to produce the informant as a witness at trial. The court noted that the trial court had modified its prior order by eliminating pretrial disclosure upon the prosecution's express representation that the informant would be produced at trial. The court also found there was no reason for the prosecution to equate defendant with a street junkie when the record did not demonstrate that she was a junkie, and that there was a misstatement of the law when the trial court, at the conclusion of its charge to jury, declared that defendant had to prove her affirmative defenses of duress and entrapment beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment convicting defendant of criminal sale of a controlled substance and ordered a new trial. The court also noted that based upon the information as to defendant, including the level of her involvement and the frequency of times in which she was involved in narcotics, it was the prosecution's belief that that minimum term allowed to be imposed would be the appropriate sentence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Informers -- Disclosure

1. A spectre of bad faith was raised entitling the defendant to a new trial when the People failed to make a diligent effort to produce an informant, who was under the employ or control of law enforcement officials and whose whereabouts were known but who was unwilling to testify as a witness at defendant's trial, since the People had the duty to make the informant,  [***3] whose testimony was relevant to the guilt or innocence of the accused, available to the defense for examination at trial in order to protect defendant's right of confrontation, due process and fairness, and the trial court, which originally ordered the People to produce the informant at both pretrial disclosure and at trial, relied upon the People's express representation on reargument of the motion by the People after the jury was selected, that the informant would be produced at trial and called to testify, in subsequently ordering that the People only need to produce the informant at the trial.

Crimes -- Argument and Conduct of Counsel

2. The reference in the prosecutor's summation to expressions such as "sounding," "freaked out" and "do a solid" utilized by a defendant, charged with drug-related crimes, in her testimony which are street words and are not indicative of narcotics' jargon which would demonstrate defendant's familiarity with the narcotics trade, was error.

Crimes -- Argument and Conduct of Counsel

3. A defendant, charged with drug-related crimes, is entitled to be acquitted or convicted on the evidence presented at trial and, therefore, there [***4] was no reason for the People, at the trial, to equate defendant with a street junkie when the record did not demonstrate that defendant was such a junkie.

Crimes -- Instructions

4. A misstatement of law occurred when the trial court, in charging the jury on the affirmative defenses of duress and entrapment, initially described the defendant's burden of proof as being by a preponderance of the evidence, which was correct, but later in the charge declared defendant's burden to be beyond a reasonable doubt.

Pomeroy v. Whalen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


65 A.D.2d 896; 411 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13802



November 30, 1978 

 

1135 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Salla v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


64 A.D.2d 437; 409 N.Y.S.2d 903; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12746



November 10, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, county and the Attorney General of the State of New York, sought review of a judgment by the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, an out-of-state construction company and workers, and adjudged N.Y. Lab. Law § 222 unconstitutional. On a direct appeal pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5601(b)( 2), the case was transferred to the court.


OVERVIEW: After beginning work on a construction project in Monroe County, New York, respondents were forced by appellants to comply with N.Y. Lab. Law § 222, which required that preference in employment on the project be given to New York state residents. The trial court declared the law unconstitutional and enjoined appellants from enforcing it. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The court held that the individual employees had standing to challenge the statute because their injuries were likely to be redressed by a favorable decision. The court ruled that respondents did not waive their right to challenge the validity of the statute by failing to object to its preference requirement until after the start of construction or by making substantial efforts to comply with it. The court held that the statute violated the commerce and privileges and immunities clauses of the United States Constitution. The statute's purpose, to alleviate unemployment in New York, did not bear a substantial relationship to the particular evil it was aimed at. The effect of the statute, protecting against out of state labor competition, resulted in improper economic protectionism.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with costs, the judgment of the trial court, which declared the New York Labor Law unconstitutional and granted summary judgment in favor of respondents.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing

1. Individual employees have standing to challenge section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that with respect to the construction of public works, preference in employment shall be given to New York State citizens, because their injuries are likely to be redressed by a favorable decision; an employee has standing to challenge the unconstitutional interference of third persons with the employer-employee relationship.

 [***3] Parties -- Standing

2. While a party may waive constitutional rights where they are exclusively private and no consideration of public morals or policy is involved, acquiescence in the loss of fundamental rights is not presumed; a corporation's acceptance of a public works construction contract should not be conditioned upon a waiver of constitutional rights, and its attempts to comply with section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that, with respect to the construction of public works, preference in employment be given to New York State citizens, in order to avoid breaching the construction contract is not a waiver of the right to challenge the statute's constitutionality.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom to Travel between States -- Employment Purposes

3. A resident of one State is constitutionally entitled to travel to another State for purposes of employment free from discriminatory restrictions in favor of State residents imposed by the other State.

Constitutional Law -- Discrimination against Noncitizens

4. A State may not discriminate against citizens of other States in situations where there is no valid, substantial reason for such discrimination;  [***4] in order for a discriminatory statute to be valid, the noncitizens must constitute a peculiar source of the evil which the statute seeks to remedy and there must be a reasonable relationship between the evil and the discrimination.

Labor -- Preference in Employment -- Violation of Privileges and Immunities Clause

5. Inasmuch as the purpose of section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that with respect to the construction of public works, preference in employment shall be given to New York State citizens, is to alleviate unemployment in New York, and the statute grants an across-the-board preference to all New York residents, it does not bear a substantial relationship to the particular evil aimed at and violates the privileges and immunities clause of the United States Constitution (art IV, § 2), since in order for a State to discriminate against citizens of another State, the noncitizens must constitute a peculiar source of the evil which the statute seeks to remedy and there must be a reasonable relationship between the evil and the discrimination.

Labor -- Preference in Employment -- Violation of Privileges and Immunities Clause

6. Where a contract for [***5] construction of a county sanitary sewer line was awarded to an out-of-State corporation, and out-of-State residents who were permanent employees of the corporation, in addition to newly hired New York State residents, came to the county to work on the project, in violation of section 222 of the Labor Law, which grants a job preference to New York residents with respect to the construction of public works, the project does not fall within the "State ownership" exception to the privileges and immunities clause of the Federal Constitution (art IV, § 2), which allows a State to discriminate against noncitizens with respect to resources owned by the State, since the exception is not absolute in that it applies only where the resource is owned wholly by the State, the exception is inapplicable to basic and essential activities, interference with which would frustrate the purposes of the formation of the Union, the sewer line construction project is not the type of State resource within the exception, and the presence of Federal funding casts doubt upon the project's status as State owned.

Commerce -- States' Right to Regulate

7. In the absence of Federal legislation, commerce [***6] is open to control by the States so long as they act within the restraints imposed by the commerce clause (US Const, art I, § 8).

Labor -- Preference in Employment -- Commerce Clause

8. Inasmuch as the effect of section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that with respect to the construction of public works, preference in employment is to be given to New York State citizens, is to protect New York residents from the competition of out-of-State labor and results in improper economic protectionism, the statute violates the commerce clause (US Const, art I, § 8) since the purpose of the clause is to prevent the States from becoming isolated economic units.

Labor -- Preference in Employment -- Equal Protection

9. It has been held that section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that with respect to the construction of public works, preference in employment is to be given to New York State residents, violates the equal protection clause (US Const, 14th Amdt, § 1) insofar as it discriminates against resident aliens.

 

1136 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Capital Dist. Regional Off-Track Betting Corp. v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


65 A.D.2d 842; 410 N.Y.S.2d 160; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13743



November 9, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a regional off-track betting corporation, appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted the motion to dismiss of defendant, the director of the state employees' retirement system, on the ground that the corporation lacked standing to maintain an action contending that 1976 N.Y. Laws 890 and a directive promulgated thereunder were inapplicable.


OVERVIEW: The state employees' retirement system was amended by 1976 N.Y. Laws 890 to provide that all full-time employees hired thereafter would be members of the system. The director contended that all full-time employees were required by the amendment to become members of the system and contribute accordingly, and so directed the corporation. The corporation argued that the amendment's terms were unconstitutionally vague, particularly with respect to the definition of full-time employees, and that the directive was improperly promulgated. The trial court ruled that because the corporation was created by the legislature, it lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of acts of the legislature. The court reversed that ruling because one subdivision of the state could challenge another's statutory interpretation. Proceeding to the merits, the court held that the amendment did not significantly expand the corporation's pension obligations and that there was no difficulty in differentiating between part-time and full-time employees because N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 513(a)(1) provided a clear definition. The director was, therefore, entitled to judgment as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order by reversing so much of it as dismissed the corporation's claims, reinstated those claims, and directed that judgment be entered in the director's favor declaring that the act and directive were applicable to the corporation.

 

1137 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mandelkern v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


64 A.D.2d 279; 409 N.Y.S.2d 881; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12254



November 3, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and its corporation counsel, appealed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Erie County (New York), which adjudged that a city ordinance was unconstitutional and enjoined the city from enforcing the ordinance against respondent employee in the employee's declaratory judgment action.


OVERVIEW: The employees were attorneys for the corporation counsel. They resided in the city but wished to live elsewhere. The challenged city ordinance provided that an employee who failed to reside in the city forfeited his employment. The employee contended that the ordinance was inconsistent with N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c)(1) and, therefore, unenforceable due to its conflict with general state law. The city likened its ordinance to the residency qualifications for state and local officers. The court reversed, holding that the ordinance was not invalid. State statutes expressly required continuing residency throughout the period of employment for public officers under N.Y. Pub. Off. Law §§ 3, 30(1)(d). The ordinance was designed with the legitimate purpose of encouraging city employees to maintain a commitment and involvement with the government that employed them by living within the city. Residence was a consideration unrelated to job performance, misconduct, or competency. It was a qualification of employment and a qualification that the city was allowed to impose without running afoul of the state constitution or the general laws of the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order declaring the ordinance unconstitutional and dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Residency Requirement for Employees

1. Local governments may lawfully require as a condition [***2] of employment that employees reside within their territorial limits.

Municipal Corporations -- Residency Requirement for Employees

2. A city ordinance which provides that employees of the city who fail to reside in the city shall forfeit their employment is valid and does not conflict with section 75 of the Civil Service Law which provides that certain civil service employees may not be removed except for misconduct or incompetence, inasmuch as residency is unrelated to job performance, misconduct or competency, and section 75 has nothing to do with eligibility for employment; there is nothing unlawful about a local government requiring that its employees reside within its territorial limits.

Civil Service -- Removal of Employees

3. Employees protected under section 75 of the Civil Service Law may not be deprived of their section 75 rights by rules establishing that certain acts of misconduct during employment result in automatic sanctions.

 

1138 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Santiago


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


64 A.D.2d 355; 409 N.Y.S.2d 716; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12755



November 2, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered on November 5, 1975, reversed, on the law, the motion to suppress granted, the plea vacated, and the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which entered a judgment convicting him, upon his pleas of guilty, of attempted possession of a weapon as a felony.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was standing for some time outside of a liquor store, was kept under surveillance by police officers who believed that he was casing the store. A rifle was found on defendant when he was approached and his coat was opened. At trial, neither officer testified that he feared for his physical safety or that he noticed any bulge in defendant's clothing before discovering the rifle. Defendant's motions to suppress the rifle and statements made subsequent to the arrest were denied by the trial court. Defendant pleaded guilty to attempted possession of a weapon as a felony. On appeal, the court found that defendant's conduct was not sufficient to create a founded suspicion that a crime was at hand, as his earlier acts were consistent with innocent behavior and the entire sequence of events was devoid of any objective evidence to justify the officers' suspicions. The seizure of the rifle and arrest were thus unconstitutional, and defendant's post-arrest statements should have been suppressed as fruits of an unconstitutional search. As such, the court reversed the conviction, granted the motion to suppress, vacated the plea, and dismissed the indictment.


OUTCOME: Because defendant's conduct was insufficient to create any suspicion in officers that a crime was being committed and the subsequent seizure of the rifle and arrest of defendant was unconstitutional, defendant's post-arrest statements should have been suppressed, as they were fruits of an unconstitutional search. The court reversed defendant's conviction, granted his motion to suppress, vacated his guilty plea, and dismissed the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Justifiable Police Intrusion Short of Probable Cause to Arrest

1. There is a sliding scale of justifiable police intrusion, short of probable cause to arrest, which correlates the allowable intensity of police conduct to the nature and weight of the facts precipitating intrusion. There are three distinct levels of intrusion: the first is the right of a police officer, in the absence of any concrete indication of criminality, to approach an individual for the purpose of requesting information if he has some articulable reason sufficient to justify the action and the basis for the inquiry need not rest on any indication of criminal activity on the part of the person of whom the inquiry is made; the second and more intense level of intrusion is the police officer's common-law right to inquire which is activated by a founded suspicion that criminal activity is afoot and carries with it the right to interfere with a citizen to the extent necessary,  [***3] short of a forcible seizure, to obtain an explanation; and the third level encompasses the statutory right ( CPL 140.50, subd 1) authorizing a forcible stop and detention of a person where a police officer entertains a reasonable suspicion that the person has committed, is committing, or is about to commit a felony or misdemeanor, and as a corollary to the statutory right to detain, an officer also has the statutory right to frisk if he reasonably suspects himself to be physically endangered. ( CPL 140.50, subd 3.)

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure

2. In order to determine the legality of police conduct regarding a search and seizure, the first consideration is whether the action was justified in its inception and then whether the conduct was reasonably related in scope to the circumstances which rendered its initiation permissible.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Reasonable Suspicion

3. In order for police to have the right to detain an individual, to the extent necessary to obtain explanatory information, they must have a founded suspicion that criminal activity is afoot and in testing the bona fides of a founded suspicion of criminal activity,  [***4] a mere hunch or gut reaction will not suffice, nor will a sincere, good faith belief by the police that a crime is about to be committed, without objective evidence of criminal activity.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Reasonable Suspicion

4. Two police officers, who approached defendant while in a telephone booth as he held the telephone receiver in one hand and had his other hand in his coat pocket after he had been observed by the police officers for most of the previous 20 minutes standing near a liquor store, may not constitutionally search defendant where neither officer feared for his physical safety nor noticed any bulge in defendant's clothing as defendant's conduct was not such as could generate a founded suspicion that a crime was at hand in that while it may be argued that his conduct was not inconsistent with the possibility that defendant was "casing" the liquor store, his conduct was equally consistent with innocent behavior and as such there was no element of objective evidence to justify the officer's suspicion; accordingly, the weapon found on defendant's person, by reason of the unconstitutional search, must be suppressed.

Crimes -- Unlawful  [***5] Search and Seizure -- Police Inquiry

5. Neither the common-law right to inquire nor the statutory right ( CPL 140.50, subd 1) to inquire permits seizures, but only entitles the police to ask questions of an individual where they have a founded suspicion that criminal activity is afoot and a search is not authorized unless the officer reasonably suspects that he is in danger of physical harm. ( CPL 140.50, subd 3.)

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Police Inquiry

6. The mere placement of one's hand in one's pocket does not create a suspicion that an individual is armed so as to permit a police officer to search the individual pursuant to CPL 140.50 (subd 3) which authorizes such a search where the officer reasonably suspects that he is in danger of physical harm.

Crimes -- Confessions

7. Postarrest statements, preceded by Miranda warnings, are rendered constitutionally infirm if a prior seizure and arrest are illegal; accordingly, such statements must be suppressed as they are the fruits of an unconstitutional search.

 

1139 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. Lombard


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


64 A.D.2d 130; 409 N.Y.S.2d 30; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11847



September 15, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously modified, and, as modified affirmed, without costs, and matter remitted to Monroe Supreme Court, Provenzano, J., for further proceedings, in accordance with opinion by Dillon, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pretrial detainees sought review of an order of the Supreme Court of Monroe County (New York), which denied plaintiffs' class action suit against defendants, county jail and officials, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief with regard to visitation, recreation, and discipline in the county jail.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed a class action suit against defendants seeking declaratory and injunctive relief with regard to visitation, recreation, and discipline in the county jail. However, the trial court denied plaintiffs relief. On appeal, the court affirmed but modified the trial court's order, holding that it was neither rationally nor constitutionally supportable to deny contact visitation to each plaintiff. The court further held that defendants were charged with the responsibility of implementing a contact visitation program that would not compromise the jail's security. The court also held that the economic factors in implementing the contact visitation program may be considered in implementing the plan, but such costs could not be used to deny qualified plaintiffs contact visitation. Finally, the court held that plaintiffs had received adequate recreational activities and that the length of the visitations was best deferred to defendants' determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and modified an order of the trial court, which denied plaintiffs' class action suit against defendants seeking declaratory and injunctive relief with regard to visitation, recreation, and discipline in the county jail.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and Prisoners -- Limitations on Constitutional Rights

1. The fact of the lawful incarceration of prisoners and the needs of the penal institutions impose limitations on the constitutional rights of prisoners.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Administration of Prisons

2. While the courts should defer to the discretion of correction officials in matters concerning prison administration and the needs of institutional security, prisoner complaints relating to restrictions and procedures must be examined in terms of the legitimate policies and goals of the correction system and the courts will intervene to stop unconstitutional or illegal practices.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Conditions of Confinement -- Equal Protection of Law

3. Pretrial detainees in the Monroe County Jail have not been denied equal protection of the [***3] law when their conditions of confinement are compared with the conditions of sentenced prisoners in State correctional facilities, for they rely on the treatment of members of a class distinct from their own and the differences afford a rational basis for disparate treatment.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Contact Visitation

4. The failure to provide a program of contact visitation constitutes a deprivation of the constitutional rights of pretrial detainees, but all such prisoners are not to be afforded contact visitation rights without regard to considerations of institutional stability and security, for to do so would be an unjustifiable incursion into matters within the expertise of those entrusted with prison administration to whose judgment the manner of implementation of an acceptable program must be left. Such a program for female detainees in the Monroe County Jail must be instituted within a reasonable period of time and, while a lack of finances may not be employed as a basis for denial, economic factors may be taken into account in determining the manner and method used to provide contact visitation.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Duration of Visitation Periods

 [***4] 5. The duration of visitation periods within a county jail is a matter properly left to the judgment of those entrusted with prison administration.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Recreation Facilities

6. The mental or physical well-being of female inmates in the Monroe County Jail is adequately safeguarded by current practices with respect to recreational facilities and access to the gymnasium; any expansion of recreational opportunity for such inmates is not constitutionally mandated.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Disciplinary Rules

7. An inmate's attitude and general adjustment to the discipline and order of a correctional facility, as well as the facts and circumstances of an infraction, are proper considerations in the imposition of sanctions; it is sufficient that the imposition of punishment or the withholding of privileges are subject to review.

Attorney and Client -- Compensation

8. In a class action by female inmates of the Monroe County Jail under the Federal Civil Rights Act for declaratory and injunctive relief with regard to visitation, recreation and discipline, the issue of whether the plaintiffs are entitled to attorneys' fees is one addressed [***5] to the discretion of the trial court.

 

1140 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Catapano v. Goldstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


64 A.D.2d 88; 408 N.Y.S.2d 527; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11384



August 25, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, dated August 18, 1978, reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, application granted, and the Board of Elections is directed to remove the name of Gloria Goldstein from the appropriate ballot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which denied their petitions to invalidate a petition designating respondent candidate as a candidate for the public office of judge of the Civil Court of the City of New York in a party primary election.


OVERVIEW: The candidate petitioned to be listed as a candidate for election to a judgeship on the Civil Court of the City of New York, which the legislature had created and allocated among the five counties of the city and had required to be elected "in and from the residents" of the respective counties. The candidate, who was a resident of Queens County, sought election to a vacancy allocated to Kings County. Appellants petitioned to invalidate the petition designating the candidate as a candidate. The candidate claimed that N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 15, only required that Civil Court judges be city residents and that the statutory requirement of residence in a specific county was unconstitutional. The court reversed a judgment of the trial court, which had denied appellants' petition. The constitutional allocation of Civil Court judgeships by counties within the city requirement was a minimal standard intended to confine the judgeships to city residents and implied a constitutionally permissible requirement of residency within the county from which elected. The additional statutory requirement of county residence for Civil Court judges was consistent with the constitutional scheme.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, appellants' application to invalidate was granted, and the Board of Elections was directed to remove the candidate's name from the ballot.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- Judges of Civil Court of City of New York -- Residency Requirement

1. A Queens County resident is not eligible to run in a party primary election for nomination to the office of Judge of the Civil Court of the City of New York where the vacancy to be filled is to be chosen by the electors of Kings County; the candidate must be a resident of Kings County.

Elections  [***3] -- Judges of Civil Court of City of New York -- Residency Requirement

2. Since Judges of the Civil Court of the City of New York are elected by voters of a county or of a district within a county, they must be residents of the county or district from which they are elected.

Elections -- Judges of Civil Court of City of New York -- Residency Requirement

3. Although Judges are not elected to represent a particular constituency but to apply the law, the constitutional allocation of Civil Court Judgeships by counties or subcounty districts within the city (NY Const, art VI, § 15) necessarily implies a constitutionally permissible requirement of residency within the political subdivision from which elected.

Elections -- Judges of Civil Court of City of New York -- Residency Requirement

4. Although the New York State Constitution (art VI, § 15) requires that Judges of the Civil Court of the City of New York be residents of the city, it does not require that they be elected city-wide, and, the Legislature having the discretion to determine the geographic allocation and manner of choosing such Judges, the city residency requirement is no more than a minimal standard [***4] and does not bar an additional requirement of county or subcounty residence for Civil Court Judges.

 

1141 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Neuner v. Weyant


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


63 A.D.2d 290; 408 N.Y.S.2d 89; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11345



August 7, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  Order of the Supreme Court, Orange County, dated June 8, 1977, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner challenged the decisions of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Orange County (New York), which dismissed her petitions for review of respondent municipality's tax assessments on the landowner's property.


OVERVIEW: A landowner sought review of the lower court's dismissal of her petitions to review the municipality's tax assessments of her realty. The landowner challenged the constitutionality of the legislature's retrospective delay in operation of a forest land exemption under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 480-a. The court affirmed the dismissal of the petitions because the five days of retrospective application was not so arbitrary as to be unconstitutional, and it was clear that legislative delay in implementation was designed to cure significant defects in the exemption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's dismissal of the landowner's petitions for review of respondent municipality's tax assessments on the landowner's property.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Matters Reviewable 

1. An order which denied a motion by a petitioner for summary judgment is a nonfinal order which necessarily affects the final judgment dismissing her petition and, hence, is reviewable on her appeal from that final judgment. ( CPLR 5501, subd [a], par 1.)

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Forest Land 

2. Section 480-a of the Real Property Tax Law (as added by L 1974, ch 814), creating a tax exemption [***4] for forest land and providing that forest land values are to be "annually * * * certified by the state board [of equalization and assessment] to the assessor[s]", does not require formal certification of forest land values by that board as a jurisdictional condition precedent to the enjoyment of the benefits of the exemption, but simply directs that such values be communicated to local assessors.

Constitutional Law -- Retrospective Tax Legislation 

3. Retrospective tax legislation is not necessarily unconstitutional, but it constitutes a denial of due process when, in the light of the tax and the circumstances in which it is laid, it is so harsh and oppressive as to transgress the constitutional limitation; relevant considerations include whether the taxpayer took voluntary actions in reasonable reliance upon the previously existing law, the length of the period of retrospection, and the public purpose to be served by the retrospective legislation.

Taxation -- Exemptions -- Forest Land 

4. Chapter 68 of the Laws of 1975, which was enacted May 6, 1975, five days after the taxable status date for cities and towns, but was expressly made retroactive to September [***5] 1, 1974, the effective date of section 480-a of the Real Property Tax Law, creating a tax exemption for forest land, and which amended that section so as to delay the implementation of the forest land exemption until taxable status dates occurring on and after July 1, 1976, did not result in a denial of due process to an owner of forest land who, but for the retrospective application of the chapter, might have enjoyed the benefits of the forest land exemption in 1975.

 

1142 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Spears v. Berle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


63 A.D.2d 372; 407 N.Y.S.2d 590; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11352



July 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  Determination vacated, with costs, and matter remitted to the commissioner for a decision, pursuant to ECL 24-0705 (subd [7]), whether to grant the permit requested or proceed under the Condemnation Law to acquire title and for further proceedings regarding the imposition of costs in accordance herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant sought review pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 of a determination of respondent Commissioner of Environmental Conservation (commissioner), which denied an application for a freshwater wetlands interim permit.


OVERVIEW: The applicant purchased two properties for the purpose of obtaining raw materials for its manufacturing business. Pursuant to the commissioner's authority, one of the properties was declared freshwater wetlands, which prevented the applicant from making commercial use of the property without first obtaining a permit from the environmental conservation department. The applicant's request for an interim permit for the alteration of the freshwater wetlands properties was denied. The applicant argued that the use restrictions imposed on the applicant's land by the Freshwater Wetlands Act and its regulations, unless struck down, constituted a taking. The court reversed based on its determination that the restrictions imposed on the applicant's land were invalid under due process considerations because they rendered the property unsuitable for any reasonable income productive or other private use for which it was adapted, thereby destroying its economic value. The commissioner was directed to decide whether to grant the permit requested or, if he deemed the wetlands sufficiently valuable for society's purposes, proceed under the condemnation law to acquire title.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the determination of the commissioner vacated with costs and the matter remitted to the commissioner for a decision whether to grant the permit requested or proceed under the condemnation law to acquire title.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Freshwater Wetlands 

1. Statutes, such as the Freshwater Wetlands Act ( ECL art 24), which impose use restrictions on wetlands are an effort to preserve a particular piece of private property for the benefit of the community at large, not an attempt to direct the course of future development, or zoning in the usual sense, but an effort to foreclose any development, and thus may be subject to a standard for measuring their constitutionality other than that applied to measure [***2] the constitutionality of zoning, i.e., whether the zoning restriction in question prohibits all reasonable use of the property; however, even if the Freshwater Wetlands Act is generically a mere form of zoning and the traditional test of constitutionality is to be applied, the denial to the owners of private wetlands of a permit which would allow them to remove humus, sand and gravel from the properties, a use concededly inconsistent with the expressed policy of the act, but, on the evidence adduced at the hearing, the only use of the properties which could produce a reasonable economic return, constitutes a taking without just compensation; accordingly, the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation must either grant the permit or, if he deems the wetlands sufficiently valuable for society's purposes, proceed under the Condemnation Law to acquire title. ( ECL 24-0705, subd 7.)

Environmental Conservation -- Freshwater Wetlands -- Costs of Hearing 

2. There is no express statutory authority to impose costs on an applicant for a permit under the Freshwater Wetlands Act ( ECL art 24), and, while the enactment of the act impliedly authorizes the recoupment of necessary expenditures [***3] from the applicant, it does not authorize the recoupment from him of mere conveniences to the agency; accordingly, an applicant for a permit cannot be required to pay for the presence of the stenographer at the hearing or for the preparation of the original transcript and copies for the agency, although he may be required to pay for any copy of the transcript requested by him and to pay postage fees.

 

1143 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McCallin v. Walsh


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


64 A.D.2d 46; 407 N.Y.S.2d 852; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11382



July 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgments, Supreme Court, New York County, each entered June 29, 1977, modified, on the law, to the extent of denying class action status in the McCallin suit, declaring all sections of Local Law No. 5 and the fire department's fire drill and evacuation rules valid and enforceable on their face, and vacating the permanent injunctions, and, except as thus modified, affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, fire department and others, challenged judgments entered by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) that declared certain sections of the fire safety requirements and controls applicable to high-rise office buildings in New York City and sections of the fire drill and evacuation rules, void and enjoined enforcement thereof. The judgment awarding appellee property owners' class action status was also challenged.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 5 (1973), as amended by New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 26 (1975), was unconstitutional, extremely costly, and imposed an unreasonable burden upon plaintiffs without contributing significantly to fire safety. On appeal, the court found that the law was constitutional and the product of a reasonable exercise of police power. In so holding, the court determined that (1) its contribution towards the achievement of public safety in high-rise office buildings and the capability of building owners to comply with its requirements were matters for legislative insight, not judicial hindsight; (2) the extra-legislative hearings to determine whether enactment would contribute to achieving greater public safety were inappropriate; (3) promulgation of rules by the Fire Commissioner to carry out the basic purpose of law was a valid exercise of his powers; and (4) the trial court made no attempt to compare the cost of compliance with the full or assessed value of any affected parcel, although that test was often used where the validity of a law requiring additional facilities to assure public health and safety was challenged .


OUTCOME: The judgments were modified to deny class action status in the property owners' suit, and to declare all sections of a particular local law, and the fire department's fire drill and evacuation rules, valid and enforceable. The permanent injunctions were vacated and the judgments were otherwise affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

1. The fire safety requirements and controls applicable to certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York (Local Laws, 1973, No. 5, as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26), by "exempting" existing buildings, in which complete sprinkler protection is provided, from the requirement to provide either a stairwell pressurization system to control smoke or a massive vertical smoke shaft penetrating the entire height of the building with duct work extending throughout each floor (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C26-504.15, subd [c]), which system, because of impracticability, is applicable only to new construction, did not eliminate sprinklerization [***4] as an option to smoke control thereby leaving the stairwell pressurization system as the only acceptable method of smoke control in unsprinklered buildings since the legislative history of Local Law No. 5 indicates that complete sprinkler installation was perceived as being in lieu of smoke shafts and stairwell pressurization.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

2. Local Law No. 5 of 1973 of the City of New York (as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26) which, in amending the city's Building Code and Fire Prevention Code, requires certain existing high-rise office buildings to provide a fire safety plan "for fire drill and evacuation procedure" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C19-161.2); an "interior fire alarm and voice communication system", including elevator recall, to alert occupants to danger and to direct safe evacuation and effective internal fire-fighting to be completed within three years (Administrative Code, § C26-1704.5); and, in centrally air-conditioned buildings, either complete sprinklering or both subdivision of open-floor areas with fire-resistant partitions and a system of smoke control to provide for safe evacuation to [***5] be completed within 15 years unless the commissioner, because of practical difficulty or undue hardship, waives or modifies the requirements and accepts alternatives (Administrative Code, § C26.504.1), is constitutional being the product of reasonable exercise of the police power in the field of public safety in high-rise office buildings, a field best left for legislative insight, not judicial hindsight.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety

3. A Legislature may, as a proper exercise of the police power, require building owners to make improvements essential to the reduction of fire hazards even though experts might differ as to the most appropriate way to cope with fire hazards; courts should be wary of substituting their economic and business judgment for that of legislative bodies and should avoid the temptation to sit as a super-Legislature to weigh the wisdom of legislation.

Constitutional Law -- Police Power

4. The doctrine that due process authorizes courts to strike as unconstitutional laws which they find are the result of unwise legislative action has been discarded and is no longer a valid constitutional concept nor does it represent the law of [***6] this State. Accordingly, when dealing with the police power to protect the public safety, it is for the Legislature to decide what regulations are needed to reduce fire hazards to the minimum and it may properly adopt the most conservative course which science and engineering offer to avoid taking the chance that human life will be lost.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

5. Since the alterations and installations proposed under the fire safety requirements and controls applicable to certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York (Local Laws, 1973, No. 5, as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26) including the installation of an interior alarm-communication system, and, in centrally air-conditioned buildings, either complete sprinklering or both subdivision of open-floor areas with fire-resistant partitions and a system of smoke control, are reasonably calculated to obtain their objective of fire safety, the danger to which the valid exercise of police power relates being "reasonably apprehended", and, therefore, extra-legislative hearings to determine whether the legislative enactment would contribute to achieving greater public safety are [***7] inappropriate.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

6. Trial Term, in invalidating most of the major fire safety innovations required for certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York (Local Laws, 1973, No. 5, as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26) including the installation of an interior alarm-communications system (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C26-1704.5) and, in centrally air-conditioned buildings, either complete sprinklering or both subdivision of open-floor areas with fire-resistant partitions and a system of smoke control (Administrative Code, § C26-504.15), upon the grounds that compliance "would impose an unreasonable burden upon plaintiffs without contributing significantly to fire safety" based upon an estimation of the cost of compliance per square foot and the gross cost to the buildings industry as a whole, improperly made no attempt to relate the cost of compliance to capital investment, market value or even assessed value of all the buildings affected or to any particular property even though those are the tests often used in instances where the validity of a law requiring additional facilities to assure public [***8] health and safety is challenged. The effect of a law on the economics of a particular property, rather than its impact on an industry generally, determines whether regulation constitutes confiscation. Although compliance with the fire safety requirements imposes an economic burden on building owners, the costs are not so onerous as to render the legislation invalid.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

7. Whether the stairwell pressurization system required for certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York as an alternate method of controlling smoke (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C26-504.1), which system must comply with a reference performance standard, is technically sound and reliable is not dispositive of the constitutionality of such fire safety requirement since scientific precision is not a criterion of constitutional power. So long as there is a reasonable basis in available scientific information and rationality in chosen courses of conduct to alleviate an accepted evil, there is no constitutional infirmity. In any event, variances from the performance standards are available from the Board of Standards and Appeals [***9] where compliance presents "practical difficulties or unnecessary hardship". (New York City Charter, § 666, subd 7.)

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

8. Trial Term improperly invalidated the statutory fire safety requirement that certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York install interior alarm-communications systems within a three-year period (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C26-1704.5) on the ground that "the time for production and installation of such systems set forth in the law is inadequate" since several alarm systems had been approved for use by the Board of Standards and Appeals in 1973, and, in any event, extensions of time were readily available whenever necessary for good-faith compliance with the law. (New York City Charter, § 666, subd 7.)

Municipal Corporations -- Installation of Safety Equipment

9. Where a Board of Standards and Appeals is authorized by law to modify statutory provisions requiring the installation of safety equipment whenever practical difficulty or unnecessary hardship might otherwise result in an unconstitutional deprivation or denial of due process (see New York City [***10] Charter, § 666, subd 7), invalidation of the statutory provisions prior to application for and denial of such relief is premature since there is no deprivation until there has been application for a permit and unreasonable refusal. A statute does not violate the Constitution where it does not deprive an owner unreasonably of his property if the statute is properly administered according to its terms.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

10. The Fire Commissioner of the City of New York, in carrying out the lawful statutory requirement that certain high-rise office buildings provide a fire safety plan "for fire drill and evacuation procedure in accordance with the requirements of the commissioner" in which all "occupants of the building shall participate and co-operate" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § C19-161.2), validly promulgated fire drill and evacuation rules which created the positions of fire warden and deputy fire warden to be filled by employees of tenants in the buildings since the creation of the positions was a logical extension and implementation of the legislative intent of the statute.

Actions -- Class Actions --  [***11] Suit to Invalidate Fire Safety Requirements

11. Granting class action status in a suit brought by building owners to invalidate fire safety requirements and controls applicable to certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York (Local Laws, 1973, No. 5, as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26) because compliance costs would constitute a confiscation of private property, is improper since each real estate owner and each parcel of property subject to the law is affected to a different degree. Since each owner is entitled to seek an extension or variance or modification from the Board of Standards and Appeals (New York City Charter, § 666, subd 7), the appropriate remedy for those aggrieved by the statute is to apply individually to the board and then any adverse ruling from the board could be reviewed in an article 78 proceeding.

Municipal Corporations -- Fire Safety in Office Buildings

12. Since any defects and inequities inherent in the fire safety requirements and controls applicable to certain high-rise office buildings in the City of New York (Local Laws, 1973, No. 5, as amd by Local Laws, 1975, No. 26) are matters for the legislative body and not for the [***12] judiciary, the invalidation by Trial Term of most of the major safety innovations required by the statute and the substitution of its judgment for that of the City Council cannot be sustained.

 

1144 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bruno v. Codd


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


64 A.D.2d 582; 407 N.Y.S.2d 165; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12338



July 20, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, officials of the city police department, the county probation department, and the clerks of the county family court (officials), appealed the judgment by the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which denied the officials' motion to dismiss plaintiffs battered wives of police officers' action to require the officials to enforce the statutes meant to protect the wives.


OVERVIEW: The wives filed an action against the officials, and alleged that the officials did not enforce the laws meant to protect the wives from battering by their husbands because the husbands were police officers. The officials filed a motion to dismiss. The trial court denied the motion. The officials appealed. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and granted the motion to dismiss. The court held that there was nothing to support the wives' allegations that any official acted to deny the wives' rights, nor was there any evidence of a conspiracy. The court held that even were the allegations true, they failed to state a justiciable cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment denying the officials' motion to dismiss the wives' action to require the officials to enforce the laws, and granted the officials' motion to dismiss.

 

1145 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hotel Dorset Co. v. Trust for Cultural Resources


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


63 A.D.2d 157; 407 N.Y.S.2d 480; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11326



July 13, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on January 5, 1978, reversed, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, and summary judgment granted to plaintiff declaring the challenged legislation unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) in favor of defendants, museum and not-for-profit developer (museum). The landowner argued that the New York State Cultural Resources Act (SCRA) and the New York City Cultural Resources Act (CCRA) were special laws that violated N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1 and that were enacted without a home rule message in violation of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2.


OVERVIEW: The museum planned to develop a condominium tower in its air space and a museum wing. The state legislature enacted the SCRA and the CCRA to make the development possible through the grant of tax exemptions and condemnation power. The landowner's hotel was adjacent to the museum, and the museum sought to extinguish the landowner's air rights and an easement. The landowner filed an action against the museum. The trial court found in favor of the museum, and the landowner appealed. The court reversed, entering summary judgment for the landowner. The court held that the SCRA and the CCRA were unconstitutional. The statutory scheme was infirm because its eligibility requirements were so restrictive that within the entire state, the only institution that qualified for its benefits was the museum. There was no reasonable possibility that another cultural institution would ever become eligible. Accordingly, the SCRA was a special law masquerading as a general law. Allowing tax exemptions under it violated N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1. The condemnation power of the CCRA was not for a public use, so it violated N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2 because it was enacted without a home rule message.


OUTCOME: In the action by the landowner against the museum challenging the constitutionality of a state statutory scheme permitting tax exemptions and condemnations to benefit the museum, the court reversed the trial court's judgment that the statutes were constitutional and entered summary judgment in favor of the landowner.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- General Laws 

1. Whether a statute is a "general law", defined in the State Constitution (art IX, § 3, subd [d]) as one "which in terms and in effect applies alike [***4] to all counties * * * all cities, all towns or all villages", is a judicial issue.

Statutes -- General Laws 

2. For a statute to be deemed applicable to a class, and thus be viewed as a general law, there must be a reasonable prospect that more than one member will constitute the class in the foreseeable future.

Constitutional Law -- New York State Cultural Resources Act

3. Article 13-E of the General Municipal Law, known as the New York State Cultural Resources Act (SCRA) and applicable on its face to cities throughout the State, which authorizes creation for each city, by "special law", of a "trust for cultural resources", denominated a public benefit corporation, which can enter into an agreement with a "participating cultural institution" for the development of a "combined-use facility", consisting of the institutional portion, such as a museum, and a noninstitutional portion, such as income-producing apartments, and which contains provisions for such a facility to become exempt from real property taxation through the medium of the Trust, sets forth eligibility criteria for a "participating cultural institution" which are so restrictive that only the Museum [***5] of Modern Art in the City of New York qualifies, nor is there any reasonable possibility that any other institution within the State will ever become eligible; accordingly, SCRA is not a general law but a special law, and grants tax exemptions in violation of the constitutional prohibition against the granting of tax exemptions except by general law (NY Const, art XVI, § 1).

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Private Property by Eminent Domain

4. Private property can constitutionally be taken by eminent domain only for a public use, and whether a proposed condemnation is for a public purpose is a judicial question.

Constitutional Law -- New York State Cultural Resources Act -- New York City Cultural Resources Act

5. Article 13-E of the General Municipal Law, known as the New York State Cultural Resources Act, and article 13-F of the General Municipal Law, known as the New York City Cultural Resources Act, unconstitutionally grant condemnation power for the combined use of expansion of the Museum of Modern Art, a private corporation, and construction of a 44-story privately owned apartment tower over the museum; whether or not a private museum's expansion is a public [***6] use, the dominant purpose of the project is revenue production, and the power of condemnation cannot be authorized or exercised where the revenue production motive or some other nonpublic purpose is dominant rather than incidental.

Constitutional Law -- New York City Cultural Resources Act 

6. Article 13-F of the General Municipal Law, known as the New York City Cultural Resources Act, which, by its own terms, is a special law, and which was enacted by the Legislature for the purpose of providing assistance to the Museum of Modern Art, a private corporation, is an act "in relation to the property, affairs or government" of New York City, and, accordingly, the enactment of it without a home rule message from the New York City Council was violative of article IX (§ 2, subd [b]) of the State Constitution, which provides that the Legislature "[s]hall have the power to act in relation to the property, affairs or government of any local government only by general law, or by special law only (a) on request of two-thirds of the total membership of its legislative body or on request of its chief executive officer concurred in by a majority of such membership".

 

1146 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Joslin v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


63 A.D.2d 466; 406 N.Y.S.2d 938; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11366



July 13, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executor challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court of Erie County (New York), which denied a petition, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, that sought to declare 1977 N.Y. Laws 33 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: 1977 N.Y. Laws 33 amended N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 2402 to establish a single, state-wide schedule by increasing fees to be paid in surrogate's court proceedings previously charged in counties outside the City of New York to the level in effect within the city. The executor filed an action that challenged the constitutionality of the enactment. The supreme court denied the executor's petition, and he appealed. The court affirmed and held that § 2402, as amended, was constitutional, that the message of necessity sent by the governor to the legislature for an immediate vote on a bill that amended § 2402 was reasonable, that the newly enacted fee schedule did not deny citizens the right of access to the surrogate's court, and that such charges did not constitute taxes. The legislature was in a far better position than a reviewing court to weigh the variable factors which might have had a bearing on the fiscal problem. The legislature properly exercised its power to fix fees in surrogate's courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Surrogate's Court -- Fees

1. Chapter 33 of the Laws of 1977 which amended SCPA 2402 to establish a single, State-wide schedule for fees paid in Surrogate's Court proceedings is constitutional.

Constitutional  [***2] Law -- Message of Necessity from Governor to Legislature

2. A message of necessity sent by the Governor to the Legislature for an immediate vote on a bill, which included an amendment to SCPA 2402 setting fees in Surrogate's Court proceedings and which message stated that its enactment was necessary in connection with the 1977-1978 budget, complied with section 14 of article III of the New York Constitution; it was reasonable for the Governor to certify that legislative action was imperative in order that court fees would be fixed prior to the beginning of the fiscal year.

Courts -- Nullification of Act of Governor

3. The courts ordinarily should not intervene to nullify an act of the Governor addressed to legislative action which literally and reasonably conforms with constitutional requirements.

Courts -- Surrogate's Court -- Right of Access to Surrogate's Court

4. A newly enacted schedule of fees to be paid in Surrogate's Court proceedings contained in SCPA 2402 does not deny citizens the right of access to that court for the fees are commensurate with the value of the estate or subject matter of the proceeding and, therefore, it may not be said that due process [***3] is offended by denying access to the courts solely because of inability to pay; moderate filing fees are constitutionally permissible.

Courts -- Surrogate's Court -- Fees

5. Charges established under SCPA 2402 to be paid in Surrogate's Court proceedings do not constitute ad valorem taxes on intangible personal property as proscribed by section 3 of article XVI of the New York Constitution; they are broken down into seven categories based upon ranges of estate value, are modest in amount and are graded to a maximum of $ 450 regardless of the value of the estate; the schedule is not one based upon a fixed percentage of the value of the estate.

Courts -- Surrogate's Court -- Fees

6. The charges fixed in SCPA 2402 to be paid in Surrogate's Court proceedings do not constitute taxes imposed in violation of section 22 of article III of the New York Constitution, which requires that tax laws state the tax and the object to which it is to be applied, but pertain to specific court proceedings and are payable one time only to the court within which the proceedings are brought, thus presumptively establishing them as fees and not taxes. Although the fees imposed may exceed [***4] the cost or expense to a Surrogate's Court incurred in connection therewith that does not make the fee schedule a general revenue measure or tax, for it is unrealistic to require that court fees may be imposed only to the extent that they directly offset the operating costs of the collecting court which may not be fully determined solely by review of a court's local budget.

Taxation -- Definition of Taxes and Fees

7. Taxes are burdens of a pecuniary nature imposed generally upon individuals or property for defraying the cost of governmental functions payable into the general fund while fees are a visitation of the costs of special services upon the one who derives a benefit from them and are imposed to defray or help defray the cost of particular services.

 

1147 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Scott


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


64 A.D.2d 525; 406 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12270



July 11, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought relief from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which rendered by jury trial a conviction for criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree, and argued that the evidence did not support the conviction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought relief from a conviction and sentence for criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree and argued that the evidence did not support his conviction. The court held that the conviction, under N.Y. Penal Law § 220.21, required proof that appellant knowingly possessed the illicit substance. No evidence showed that appellant had knowledge of the contents of the case which contained the substance, or the nature of the negotiations between his co-worker and the alleged purchaser of the substance.
