Photographs and a recorded telephone conversation were in substance not indicative of illegal intent, and the evidence did not support the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction on the law for criminal possession of a controlled substance in the first degree, and dismissed the indictment. The evidence adduced at trial fell far short of proving appellant had knowing possession of a controlled substance.

 

1148 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fullilove v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


62 A.D.2d 798; 406 N.Y.S.2d 888; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10909; 17 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8432



July 6, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, governor and state agency, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), that declared Executive Order No. 45, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 3.45, unconstitutional, enjoined its implementation, and directed cancellation of implementing rules. Respondents, unions, contractors, and trade associations, brought the action for review of the order imposing affirmative action goals in state contracts.


OVERVIEW: The governor issued Executive Order No. 45, which called for the establishment of affirmative action goals in state contracts, and the state agency issued implementing regulations. Respondents challenged Executive Order No. 45 and the implementing regulations, asserting that the initiatives were unconstitutional exercises of legislative power by the executive branch. The supreme court ruled in favor of respondents. On appeal from that judgment, the court held that Executive Order No. 45 exceeded the governor's authority because it extended and expanded the requirements of N.Y. Lab. Law § 220-e and N.Y. Exec. Law § 296(12), which prohibited discrimination in state contracts and permitted contractors to implement state-approved affirmative action programs. Neither of those statutes implied that contractors were obligated to establish affirmative action programs regardless of their status as discriminators or non-discriminators. By issuing Executive Order No. 45, the governor assumed the power of the legislature to set state policy in an area of increasing public concern. The court further found that respondents had standing to bring the action. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, in favor of respondents, that declared the state executive order and implementing regulations unconstitutional. The court further directed cancellation of the regulations relating to affirmative action goals in state contracts, which were adopted by the governor and the state agency.


HEADNOTES

Governor -- Imposition of Affirmative Action Programs

1. An Executive Order promulgated by the Governor, which provides that all State contracts shall require contractors to undertake affirmative action programs for minority manpower utilization and that, before a contract is awarded, contractors must submit such programs, exceeds the authority of the executive, for the Legislature did not see fit to encompass in the statutes an affirmative action program and did not require [***2] that contractors must submit a program of affirmative action. The Executive Order goes beyond any specific requirement that contractors follow nondiscriminatory hiring practices and beyond the mere requirement that regulations be developed by the Commissioner of Human Rights and, therefore, is unenforceable.

Parties -- Standing

2. Since an Executive Order requires contractors to submit affirmative action programs for minority manpower utilization before a State contract is awarded dehors the existence of any rules or regulations, there is a justiciable controversy as to the legality-enforceability of the order and, accordingly, there is no lack of standing to maintain an action challenging the order.

 

1149 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kinsella v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


64 A.D.2d 738; 407 N.Y.S.2d 78; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12578



July 6, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Determination confirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher filed an action, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.LR. 78, to review a determination of respondent board of education (board) which dismissed the teacher from his position as a tenured teacher.


OVERVIEW: The teacher had been a tenured physical education teacher in the board's school district for approximately 15 years when the principal filed a charge against the teacher alleging that he had used excessive physical force on several occasions when disciplining students. Thereafter, the board unanimously found the teacher guilty of the three specific instances of excessive physical force contained in the original notice of charges. A majority of the board recommended discharge. The teacher challenged the board's decision in the federal district court, but the district court denied the teacher's request. The circuit court then affirmed the district court's decision. In determining the teacher's N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 petition, the court first held that the petition was timely. The court then held that there was ample evidence to conclude that on three occasions the teacher used physical force beyond that warranted by the circumstances. Moreover, the facts clearly supported a charge of incompetence. Likewise, the charge of insubordination was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the teacher's petition and affirmed the board's decision to terminate the teacher from his position.

 

1150 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fantis Foods, Inc. v. Standard Importing Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


63 A.D.2d 52; 406 N.Y.S.2d 763; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11314



June 27, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on May 5, 1977, affirmed. Respondent shall recover of appellant $ 60 costs and disbursements of this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant third party challenged a decision from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied the third party's motion to dismiss the third party complaint against it for lack of in personam jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The food company initiated suit against the importer alleging that the food company purchased food from the third party, a Greek corporation. Sometime during shipment of the food, the importer converted the food to its possession. When the food arrived in New York, the food company was refused delivery. The third party filed a motion to dismiss the third party complaint brought by the importer. The third party contended that the importer's claim was for breach of contract, that the requirements for in personam jurisdiction over the third party had not been met under N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 302, and that the contractual forum selection clause had to control. The court held that the third party would be held accountable for consequences in the state where it derived substantial revenue from interstate or international commerce. The court held that injury to the importer was foreseeable by the third party not merely because the importer was incorporated in and had its principal office in New York but because the third party by virtue of its monopolistic control in its dealings with the importer and the food company had to be deemed to foresee consequences of its actions in the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that dismissed the third party's motion to dismissed the third party action against it.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Nondomiciliaries

1. In an action involving the alleged conversion of several hundred barrels of cheese by a Greek corporation which, having a virtual monopoly over the sale and exportation of such cheese, thereby deriving substantial revenue from international commerce, and, consequently, being in a unique position to control the competitive relationship between two New York corporations, importers and distributors of foreign foods, had allegedly diverted the cheese from one of the New York corporations to the other, long-arm jurisdiction exists over the Greek corporation pursuant to CPLR 302 (subd [a], par 3, cl [ii]), applying the reasonable man standard to the test of foreseeability embodied in the [***2] statute as to whether the nondomiciliary should expect its tortious act to have consequences in New York.

Conflict of Laws -- Law Governing Contract

2. Although a clause in a contract between a Greek corporation and a New York corporation, providing that the forum for the solution of differences between the parties was the "court of Greece", would be applicable to a claim brought under the contract, it has no application to a claim by the New York corporation against the Greek corporation sounding in the tort of conversion for the conversion is a dispute outside the contract. Further, under the circumstances presented, it would not be right and proper to enforce the forum selection clause.

 

1151 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rasero


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


62 A.D.2d 845; 406 N.Y.S.2d 458; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10916



June 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, Bronx County, entered on April 8, 1976, reversed, on the law, the determination of defendant's guilt reinstated and the proceeding remanded for imposition of appropriate sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order from the Supreme Court (New York), which granted defendant police officer's motion to dismiss an indictment charging him with three counts of perjury in the first degree on the grounds of collateral estoppel, after the trial court found him guilty.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state sought review after the trial court granted defendant police officer's motion to dismiss an indictment charging him with three counts of perjury in the first degree. The trial court found defendant guilty, but granted the dismissal on the grounds of collateral estoppel, based upon the earlier acquittal of defendant's partner. On appeal, the court found that trial court erred in applying the doctrine of collateral estoppel because the subject of the prior prosecution was whether defendant's partner had perjured himself before the grand jury. Resolution of that issue had no legal bearing upon the independent issue of whether defendant committed perjury before the same grand jury. Therefore, the ultimate issue of defendant's perjury had not been previously litigated. Finally, the court concluded that reversal was neither violative of nor precluded by the principles underlying the prohibition against double jeopardy because the trial court had already found defendant guilty as charged.


OUTCOME: The trial court erred in dismissing the indictment against defendant police officer on the grounds of collateral estoppel, therefore the order dismissing the indictment was reversed, the determination of defendant's guilt was reinstated and the proceeding was remanded for sentence imposition.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Collateral Estoppel

1. Collateral estoppel may only be invoked in criminal cases where there is an identity of parties as well as an identity of issue. Accordingly, where two defendants were indicted for perjury concerning their testimony before a Grand Jury, an acquittal of one defendant on a prior trial does not operate to preclude a subsequent trial and conviction of a second defendant although both were indicted in connection with the same transaction; collateral estoppel is inapplicable as there is no identity of parties and the fact that one defendant was acquitted has no legal bearing upon the independent [***3] issue of whether the second defendant committed perjury.

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel

2. The doctrine of collateral estoppel may be applied in civil cases where (1) there is an identity of issue necessarily decided in the prior action and decisive of the present suit and (2) there was a full and fair opportunity afforded to the party against whom the estoppel is sought to be invoked to contest the decision now said to be controlling; thus the litigant barred in civil cases by collateral estoppel must have had an opportunity to contest the precise issue resolved in the prior action.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

3. The policies underlying the prohibition against double jeopardy are not violated by a reversal and remand where such will require neither a retrial nor any supplemental findings as to defendant's guilt. Accordingly, a defendant is not placed in double jeopardy by a reversal and remand where the trial court found him guilty but erroneously dismissed the indictment due to a misapplication of the doctrine of collateral estoppel as the only matter to be considered on remand is the appropriate sentence.

 

1152 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


63 A.D.2d 935; 406 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11958



June 20, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Supreme Court of Bronx County (New York), which entered judgment upon defendant's conviction on charges of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fifth degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on charges of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fifth degree and was sentenced to a term of 2 to 10 years. Defendant challenged his conviction. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that defendant was improperly cross-examined by the prosecutor on the basis of statements made by him during the course of a compulsory examination pursuant to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 23.07(e), however, the error in no way harmed defendant because he was convicted by the jury only on the charges he admitted to during trial. The court held that the statute, which provided sentences based on the total weight of the substance and not the actual amount of narcotics itself, was not unconstitutional because there was a rationale basis for the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fifth degree.

 

1153 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McNulty v. Chinlund


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


62 A.D.2d 682; 406 N.Y.S.2d 558; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10894



June 15, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law and the facts,  [***4] by reversing so much thereof as dismissed the complaint for failure to state a cause of action with respect to Parts 7004, 7025 and 7026 of title 9 of the Official Compilation of Codes, Rules and Regulations, and by granting a preliminary injunction enjoining the enforcement of Parts 7004, 7025 and 7026 of title 9 of the Official Compilation of Codes, Rules and Regulations, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sheriffs and defendant state commission of correction (commission) both sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted in part, and denied in part, a motion by the sheriffs for a preliminary injunction prohibiting the enforcement of certain regulations by the commission. The trial court also granted in part, and denied in part, the commission's motion to dismiss the claim.


OVERVIEW: The sheriffs sought to enjoin the enforcement of regulations adopted by the commission pursuant to N.Y. Correct. Law § 45(6), which concerned prisoner correspondence, visitation, access to media, religion, packages, and printed material. The sheriffs alleged that they had the constitutional and statutory duty to maintain and control county jails and the inmates confined therein; and that the commission's regulations unconstitutionally transferred that duty from elected officers to nonlocal appointed officers. The trial court granted the sheriffs' motion for an injunction as to the regulations pertaining to visitation, religion and access to media, and granted the commission's motion to dismiss with respect to the other regulations. On appeal, the court modified the judgment of the trial court. The court granted the sheriffs a preliminary injunction enjoining the enforcement of the correspondence, packages and publication regulations. The court found that the sheriffs established a reasonable probability of success in their actions, and the commission failed to prove it would be adversely affected by the injunction.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court, which granted in part, and denied in part, the sheriffs' action seeking a preliminary injunction prohibiting the commission from enforcing certain regulations.


HEADNOTES

Sheriffs and Constables -- Custody and Control of County Jails

1. Custody and control of county jails have not been conferred upon Sheriffs by the Constitution, although it declares them to be local constitutional officers (NY Const, art XIII, § 13), but by statute (Correction Law, § 500-c), and, having conferred those powers and duties, the Legislature has the power to limit, curtail or abolish them.

Constitutional Law -- Delegation by Legislature of Rule-Making Power

2. The Legislature may constitutionally grant rule-making power to the discretion of others, provided it limits the field in which they are to operate and provides standards to govern its exercise; it need not provide a precise and specific formula so long as it provides standards [***5] and guides in a broad outline, leaving to administrative officials the duty of arranging the details.

Prisons and Prisoners -- State Commission of Correction

3 The standards set forth in subdivision 6 of section 45 of the Correction Law, which authorizes the State Commission of Correction to "[promulgate] rules and regulations establishing minimum standards for the care, custody, correction, treatment, supervision, discipline, and other correctional programs for all persons confined in correctional facilities", are not so vague, indefinite or imprecise as to render them invalid; inherent in the delegation of the power to establish "minimum" standards is the requirement that the standards established be reasonable and not arbitrary or capricious in view of the subject matter to be regulated.

Administrative Law -- Rule-Making Power of Administrative Agencies

4. Administrative agencies can only promulgate rules to further the implementation of the law as it exists, and they have no authority to create a rule out of harmony with the statute or statutes being implemented.

Prisons and Prisoners -- State Commission of Correction -- Regulations

5. Where,  [***6] in an action by Sheriffs to enjoin the enforcement of regulations of the State Commission of Correction which concern prisoner correspondence, visitation, access to media, religion, packages, and printed material and publications (9 NYCRR Parts 7004, 7008, 7023, 7024, 7025, 7026), their complaint alleges facts which, if proven, would establish that those regulations create grave security risks, financial hardships, and health and fire hazards, and conflict with the Sheriffs' statutory duty of safekeeping of prisoners confined to their custody (Correction Law, § 500-c), the complaint is sufficient to survive a motion to dismiss.

Injunctions -- Preliminary Injunction

6. In order to become entitled to a preliminary injunction, plaintiffs who seek it must establish a prima facie right to relief, that irreparable injury will occur without the preliminary injunction, and that the balance of the equities is in their favor.

Prisons and Prisoners -- State Commission of Correction -- Regulations

7. Sheriffs who seek to enjoin the enforcement of regulations of the State Commission of Correction which concern prisoner correspondence, visitation, access to media, religion,  [***7] packages, and printed material and publications (9 NYCRR Parts 7004, 7008, 7023, 7024, 7025, 7026), and whose complaint alleges facts which, if proven, would establish that those regulations create grave security risks, financial hardships, and health and fire hazards, and conflict with the Sheriffs' statutory duty of safekeeping of prisoners confined to their custody (Correction Law, § 500-c), are entitled to a preliminary injunction; they have established a reasonable probability of success in their action, and the security and financial implications indicate the probability of irreparable injury occurring if the preliminary injunction is not granted, nor does it appear that the granting of a preliminary injunction will adversely affect the commission.

People v. Macbeth Realty Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


63 A.D.2d 908; 406 N.Y.S.2d 298; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11927



June 15, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants from using or occupying the premises for any purpose and ordering that the premises be closed and vacated.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants from using or occupying certain premises for any purpose and ordering that the premises be closed and vacated. The court found that there was sufficient evidence to warrant the preliminary injunction and closing order under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § C16-2.6, C16-2.8 and N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2321(5). However, the court found that the premises was a hotel wherein it appeared that the tenants of most of the rooms in the hotel had nothing to do with the prostitution and that the hotel was their home. The court found that, at least until such time as there was a final judgment, it would work an unjust hardship on such tenants to require them to leave their homes, the necessary effect of an order requiring the closing of the entire hotel. Accordingly, the court modified the order appealed from so as to limit the closing order.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order to limit the closing of the hotel to the two rooms which had been used in prostitution and any rooms which were vacant or became vacant during the pendency of the action. Defendants were restrained and enjoined during the pendency of the action from permitting the premises to be maintained as a public nuisance.

 

1155 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


V. C. Vitanza Sons, Inc. v. Ross


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


63 A.D.2d 1068; 406 N.Y.S.2d 160; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12170



June 15, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner contractor appealed from a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which vacated the stay of an order requiring respondent Industrial Commissioner of the State of New York (industrial commissioner) to have made a final determination within a set time period regarding the withholding of funds due to the contractor. The contractor also sought an order directing the immediate release of the funds.


OVERVIEW: The industrial commissioner withheld funds that were owed to the contractor under a public works contract for allegedly unpaid wages pursuant to N.Y. Lab. Law § 220-b. The industrial commissioner issued an interim determination two years later, but refused to release the funds pending further investigation. The special term granted the contractor an order of mandamus and directed the industrial commissioner to have made a final determination by a set date. The date was extended and then stayed. The contractor appealed, arguing for a lift of the stay, a final decision, a return of his funds, and a declaration that § 220-b was unconstitutional. The court granted the motion to vacate the stay and directed the industrial commissioner to have made a final determination within 60 days, citing § 220-b's requirement that the industrial commissioner was to have expeditiously resolved the issue of the withholding of the funds. The court refused to pass on the industrial commissioner's performance of his duties because his determination was not yet final and because the industrial commissioner's performance was not a viable challenge to the constitutionality of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court granted the contractor's motion for a lift of the stay ordering the industrial commissioner to have made a determination surrounding the withholding of the contractor's funds. The court ordered the industrial commissioner to have made a final determination within 60 days.

 

1156 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lombardi v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


63 A.D.2d 1058; 406 N.Y.S.2d 148; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12161



June 8, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner student appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York), which dismissed his petition, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, to review a refusal by respondent Commissioner of the New York Education Department to approve a contract between the student's local school district and a private school.


OVERVIEW: The student was neurologically impaired, and a committee recommended that he attend a private school. His local school district approved, but the Commissioner denied the school district's request to enter into a contract with the private school because the private school was not an approved school for the handicapped. The student challenged the Commissioner's decision, arguing that it was arbitrary and capricious. On appeal from the trial court's dismissal of the petition, the court affirmed, finding that there was insufficient information to establish whether or not an approved adequate alternative educational program to that of the private school was available. The court found that N.Y. Educ. Law art. 89, which authorized the approval of contracts for the benefit of handicapped children, was reform legislation. The court found that reform legislation was subject to a rational basis test. The court found that the Commissioner's decision should be upheld because his interpretation of the statute and the standards for contract approval was reasonable and rational. The court found that no violation of the student's constitutional or statutory rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1157 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Arthur Young & Co. v. Keith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


63 A.D.2d 878; 405 N.Y.S.2d 708; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11872



June 6, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant companies challenged the decision of the State Tax Commission (New York), which issued, at respondent consultant's request, a subpoena that sought production of the companies' tax returns in order to show the unconstitutionality of a taxing statute. The action was transferred to the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) by order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York).


OVERVIEW: The consultant brought an action before the Commission to recover taxes it paid under the Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law on its business. The consultants sought to demonstrate that the Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law was unconstitutional insofar as it denied the consultants equal protection of the law. They claimed that the companies were not required to pay such taxes upon fees received for similar services it rendered. The Commission provided the consultant with subpoenas to compel the companies to produce their income tax returns for specified years. The companies sought to quash the subpoena. The Commission issued an order compelling the companies to produce their tax records. The court reversed, holding that the sum involved and the convenience of the parties did not justify the change of venue granted by the trial court. The proceedings were not directed against the Commission, regardless of the trial court's rationalization. The claim of unconstitutionality would not have been advanced by the material subpoenaed to justify the trouble and expense to the companies, and the invasion of the companies' privacy that compliance with the subpoenas would have caused.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Commission's judgment to order the companies to produce their tax records and granted the companies' request to quash the subpoenas.

 

1158 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State Div. of Human Rights ex rel. Kozlowski v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


62 A.D.2d 617; 406 N.Y.S.2d 401; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10885; 28 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1731



May 26, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Determination unanimously confirmed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retiree sought review of a decision the State Human Rights Appeal Board (New York), which affirmed the human rights division's dismissal of retiree's complaint, charging that respondent division of state police discriminated against him on the basis of age. Retiree claimed that N.Y. Retire. & Soc. Sec. Law § 381-b(e), mandating retirement from the state police at age 55, violated N.Y. Exec. Law § 296(3-a) and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The retiree became employed by the division of state police in 1947. In 1969, when he was 50 years old, the state enacted a statute providing for a mandatory retirement age of 55 for officers entering the force after 1969. Officers already in service could elect to be covered under the plan. The retiree elected to be covered and withdrew his election to be covered under the previous plan, which provided for a mandatory retirement age of 70. Petitioner retired at age 55 and then filed a complaint with the State Division of Human Rights, charging age discrimination. The Division dismissed the complaint, finding that it did not have jurisdiction because of an irreconcilable conflict between N.Y. Ret. & Soc. Sec. Law § 381-b and N.Y. Exec. Law § 296, which prohibited age discrimination. The Appeals Board affirmed. Retiree sought review under N.Y. Exec. Law § 298 and the court affirmed, finding that unlawful discriminatory practices were limited to those set forth in § 296, which excepted retirement policies. The two statutes did not conflict because the retirement plan was not a subterfuge to evade the age discrimination laws.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the judgment in favor of the division of state police and ordered the petition dismissed. The court declared the statute setting the mandatory retirement age at 55 to be constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Unlawful Age Discrimination

1. Mandatory retirement at age 55 for uniformed members of the State Police under subdivision e of section 381-b of the Retirement and Social Security Law is not a subterfuge to evade the purposes of the age discrimination provisions of the Executive Law and, therefore, is not within its ban on age discrimination. (See Executive Law, § 291, subd 1; § 296, subd 3-a.)

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Unlawful Age Discrimination

2. Inasmuch as subdivision e of section 381-b of the Retirement and Social Security Law, calling for mandatory retirement of uniformed State Police at age 55, and subdivision 3-a of section 296 of the Executive Law, prohibiting unlawful age discrimination in employment, can be read together in a consistent manner, there is [***4] no conflict between the two statutes; even if the two statutes could not be reconciled, section 381-b would prevail because it was enacted at a later date.

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Unlawful Age Discrimination

3. The Legislature did not intend to repeal subdivision e of section 381-b of the Retirement and Social Security Law, providing for mandatory retirement of uniformed State Police at age 55, by its subsequent definition of "civil right" in subdivision 1 of section 291 of the Executive Law to include freedom from age discrimination, since it had expressly excepted qualified retirement plans from "unlawful discriminatory practices" relating to age discrimination ever since the enactment of subdivision 3-a of section 296 of the Executive Law, prior to the adoption of section 381-b.

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Unlawful Age Discrimination

4. In view of the demanding nature of uniformed police work, mandatory retirement for uniformed State Police officers at age 55 under subdivision e of section 381-b of the Retirement and Social Security Law does not violate the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment,  [***5] even though a mandatory retirement age of 60 is allowed for higher level personnel.

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Unlawful Age Discrimination

5. A uniformed police officer who voluntarily elected to be covered by the provisions of subdivision e of section 381-b of the Retirement and Social Security Law, thereby obtaining superior retirement benefits under that statute, is barred from attacking the statute on constitutional grounds.

 

1159 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Duncan & Hill Realty v. Dep't of State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


62 A.D.2d 690; 405 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10895



May 19, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Determination unanimously modified, in accordance with opinion by Witmer, J., and as modified, confirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brokerage firm sought review, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, of the decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which upheld the determination of respondent, the Department of State of the State of New York (Secretary of State), that suspended the brokerage firm's real estate broker's license for violation of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c.


OVERVIEW: The brokerage firm filled out a purchase offer form for the buyers to sign, which set the purchase price, detailed the terms of a purchase-money mortgage, and recited that the offer was subject to the seller's attorney's approval and the buyers deposit with the brokerage firm of their down payment to serve as commission in the event of default. The purchase offer form used by the brokerage firm contained no caveat alerting the parties that, when signed, the instrument became a binding contract, and cautioning them that it was preferable for them to consult an attorney. When the buyer decided to terminate the contract, he was informed that the down payment would not be returned. The buyer reported the matter to the Secretary of State which charged the brokerage firm with untrustworthiness and suspended its license. On appeal, the trial court affirmed. The court upheld the determination of violation of § 441-c, but held that because of the erratic enforcement of the statute, the brokerage firm should have only been censured. The court held that the brokerage firm engaged in the unauthorized practice of law by inserting the detailed terms of the mortgage in the offer.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the determination of violation of real property law by the brokerage firm, but modified the judgment to only censure the brokerage firm.


HEADNOTES

Brokers -- Real Estate Brokers -- Unauthorized Practice of Law

1. The preparation of simple contracts in real estate transactions by real estate brokers and agents who have a personal interest in the transaction but have not held themselves out to be attorneys at law does not constitute unauthorized practice of law.

Brokers -- Real Estate Brokers -- Unauthorized Practice of Law

2. The privilege accorded to real estate brokers and agents to prepare simple real estate contracts [***4] must be circumscribed for the benefit of the public to ensure that such professionals do not exceed the bounds of their competence and, to the detriment of the innocent public, prepare documents the execution of which requires a lawyer's scrutiny and expertise.

Brokers -- Real Estate Brokers

3. A real estate broker who not only used a purchase offer which did not contain a clause alerting the buyer to see his attorney before signing it but also inserted the detailed terms of a purchase-money mortgage in the offer, went beyond the privilege extended to brokers to prepare simple real estate contracts.

Brokers -- Real Estate Brokers -- Untrustworthiness

4. A determination by the Secretary of State that the acts of petitioner, a real estate broker who prepared a purchase offer contract in which he inserted the detailed terms of a purchase-money mortgage but failed to alert the buyer to see his attorney before signing it, constituted the practice of law, thus rendering petitioner untrustworthy and incompetent as a licensed realtor within the provisions of section 441-c of the Real Property Law, was supported by substantial evidence.

Brokers -- Real Estate  [***5] Brokers -- Untrustworthiness

5. In light of the previous practice on the part of real estate brokers and agents in respect to the preparation of simple real estate contracts, and the narrow line between permitted and forbidden services by them in this area and the prior feeble and erratic enforcement of section 441-c of the Real Property Law by the respondent Secretary of State, the action of respondent in suspending petitioner real estate broker's license for having gone beyond the privilege extended to brokers to prepare such contracts was excessive and the punishment should be reduced to censure only.

 

1160 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Parker v. Kolb


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


62 A.D.2d 128; 404 N.Y.S.2d 904; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10428



May 11, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed the teachers' petition against respondents, Commissioner of Mental Health and others, that challenged the requirement to work an extra hour violated state law and the equal protection clause.


OVERVIEW: The teachers, by an order of the commissioner, were required to teach an extra hour for mentally retarded children. The teachers brought suit and claimed that they should be treated like other teachers. The trial court dismissed he petition, and the teachers appealed. The court found that the commissioner was not required to limit the number of hours that the teachers were required to teach. Further, the court found that the requirement to teach extra hours did not violate the equal protection clause, as there was a reasonable basis for the commissioner's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which dismissed the teachers' petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Teachers -- Increase in Hours of Attendance

1. Pursuant to an order of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene, the hours in attendance of teachers employed by the department to instruct mentally handicapped children were properly increased by one hour to six hours which was one hour more than the hours of teachers of handicapped children throughout the State. Section 175.5 (a) of the Regulations of the Commissioner of Education does not require five as opposed to six hours but rather sets forth the minimum number of hours that schools must be in session to receive State aid and Part 200 of the regulations (handicapped children) does not indicate that the requiring of six hours is prohibited.

Schools -- Teachers -- Increase in Hours of Attendance

2. The Mental Hygiene Law confers on the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene authority to operate facilities under his jurisdiction without any obligation to conform [***3] the teaching hours of institution teachers with those of other teachers of mentally handicapped children throughout the State. Section 15.11 of the statute mandates that the department provide school-age patients with an education comparable to what they would receive in their local districts but does not regulate the maximum number of hours of its teachers.

Schools -- Teachers -- Increase in Hours of Attendance

3. Since conditions in State facilities may be sufficiently distinguishable from those in noninstitutional settings to require more teaching time to obtain substantially the same results as elsewhere, a directive of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene requiring an additional hour of teaching by department teachers of mentally handicapped children has a rational basis and is not unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection clause.

 

1161 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nicholas v. Kahn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


62 A.D.2d 302; 405 N.Y.S.2d 135; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10455



May 11, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed, on the law, without costs, petitions reinstated, and judgment directed to be entered declaring that Rules 1 through 8 promulgated by the Chairman of the Public Service [***3] Commission, dated June 8, 1976, effective June 18, 1976, are unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review from judgments of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which dismissed petitions to invalidate certain rules promulgated under N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 9 and N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 74 by respondent, Chairman of the Public Service Commission (chairman), with respect to personal investments of employees of the Public Service Commission (PSC) and the Public Service Department (PSD) and their family members.


OVERVIEW: The court was called upon to determine if certain rules promulgated by the chairman governing personal investments of employees of the PSC and PSD, as well as investments of members of their families, were beyond the authority of the chairman as delegated by the provisions of N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 9 and N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 74. The court held that rules one through five extended and expanded the delegated authority of the generic statutes and were an unwarranted exercise of legislative power. Thus, the delegated discretion was unfettered and the attempt of the chairman to supply specifics was an impermissible extension and expansion of his delegated authority. Accordingly, because the enactment of rules one through five was an unwarranted exercise of legislative power, the court concluded that the entire regulatory scheme was unconstitutional and reversed the trial court's judgments.


OUTCOME: The judgments were reversed on the law, the petitions were reinstated, and judgment was directed to be entered declaring that rules one through five promulgated by the chairman were unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Public Officers -- Financial Holdings of Public Employees

1. The State has a legitimate interest in the financial privacy of employees working in a State agency charged with critical and sensitive duties of supervision, and statutes which express that interest (Public Service Law, § 9; Public Officers Law, § 74; Executive Law, § 74) are constitutional.

Administrative Law -- Discretion of Administrative Agency

2. Legislation may constitutionally confer discretion upon an administrative agency, and a precise or specific formula need not be furnished in a field where flexibility in fashioning rules in furtherance of that legislation is desirable in light of the complexities extant in the area sought to be circumscribed; the Legislature may enact statutes in broad outline, leaving administrative details to the appropriate enforcing administrative official.

Public Utilities -- Public Service Commission -- Investments by Employees, Spouses and Children

3. Rules promulgated by the Chairman of the Public Service Commission which, inter alia, prohibit not only employees [***4] of the commission and the Public Service Department but also their spouses and unemancipated children from having any interest, direct or indirect, in companies directly or peripherally regulated by the commission, are unconstitutional as an unwarranted exercise of legislative power; they are beyond the authority delegated to the Chairman by section 9 of the Public Service Law and section 74 of the Public Officers Law, which, although forbidding an employee from having a direct or an "indirect interest" which is in "substantial conflict" with the proper discharge of his duties (Public Officers Law, § 74), do not prescribe that an "indirect interest" is one held by a wife or minor child nor prohibit such holdings by them per se; for the Chairman to decide that the only "indirect interest" is investments by wives and minor children is arbitrary, and expands legislative requirements because such a determination excludes all other possible indirect interests; moreover, provisions in the rules for exemptions therefrom, which confine review of applications for exemptions solely to considerations of whether the amount and extent of equity holdings in regulated companies by spouses and minor [***5] children are minimal enough to constitute good cause for exemption, impose purely subjective standards for review of such applications that must be stamped as arbitrary, and also expand existing legislation because they do not guarantee that only investments creating a "substantial conflict" of interest will be barred.

Actions -- Form of Action

4. Where the ultimate relief sought in an article 78 proceeding is a declaration of invalidity of promulgated rules, the proceeding should be converted to an action for a declaratory judgment.

 

1162 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hynes v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


62 A.D.2d 365; 405 N.Y.S.2d 56; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10843



May 9, 1978 



DISPOSITION: [Proceeding No. 1.] Resettled order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on September 15, 1977, unanimously modified, on the law and the facts and in the exercise of discretion, to limit the books and records required to be produced to those described in the prior subpoena issued by petitioner on October 18, 1976, updated to include such books and records for the period from November 30, 1975 through June 30, 1977, and otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements and without prejudice to any application by appellant to fix the time, terms and conditions of the Attorney-General's possession of the subpoenaed items.

[Proceeding No. 2.] Resettled order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on September 15, 1977, unanimously modified, on the law and the facts and in the exercise of discretion, to limit the books and records required to be produced to those described in the prior subpoena issued by petitioner on October 29, 1976, updated to include such books and records for the period from January 1, 1972 through [***7] June 30, 1977 and otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements and without prejudice to an application by appellant to fix the time, terms and conditions of the Attorney-General's possession of the subpoenaed items.

[Proceeding No. 3.] Resettled order and judgment (one paper), Supreme Court, New York County, entered on September 15, 1977, unanimously modified, on the law and the facts and in the exercise of discretion, to limit the books and records required to be produced to those described in the prior subpoena issued by petitioner on October 18, 1976, updated to include such books and records for the period from January 1, 1969 through June 30, 1977, and otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements and without prejudice to an application by appellant to fix the time, terms and conditions of the Attorney-General's possession of the subpoenaed items.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, operator of private proprietary home for adults, sought review of a resettled order and judgment of the Supreme Court of New York in New York County, granting an application to compel compliance with an office subpoena duces tecum issued by respondent Deputy Attorney-General and directing appellant to turn over the subpoenaed books and records for inspection, examination, and audit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant operated a private home for adults. Respondent Deputy Attorney-General issued an office subpoena duces tecum to produce certain books and records, and appellant sought to quash. The court granted motion to compel compliance and dismissed cross-motion to quash, and the decision was affirmed. Due to an amendment of the statute upon which subpoena issued, respondent issued a new subpoena duces tecum to appellant, updated to cover books and records for more recent period, and directed that a substantially increased variety and quantity of books and records be produced. Appellant opposed motion to compel compliance asserting no factual basis for it, overbroadness, and no good cause to require production, and argued amendments were unconstitutional. The court found that records required by the previous subpoena were sufficient. There was no showing of need for additional documents required by new subpoena, except that prior subpoena was updated to include documents therein maintained through more recent date. Resettled order and judgment was modified to limit production to those records described in prior subpoena, updated to include documents through more recent date.


OUTCOME: The court modified resettled order and judgment on the law and the facts to limit the books and records required to be produced to those described in the prior subpoena, updated to include such books and records for certain period, and otherwise affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Retention of Possession of Subpoenaed Records

1. Nothing in amendments (L 1977, ch 451) to section 63 (subd 8) of the Executive Law and CPLR 2305, which authorize the Attorney-General and his deputies to retain for inspection, examination and audit records [***8] subpoenaed by him pursuant to section 63 (subd 8) of the Executive Law, and which were enacted subsequent to a decision of the Court of Appeals holding that section 63 (subd 8) of the Executive Law, as it then read, did not authorize retention of such subpoenaed records ( Matter of Windsor Park Nursing Home v Hynes, 42 NY2d 243; see Matter of Heisler v Hynes, 42 NY2d 250, similarly limiting a Grand Jury subpoena duces tecum), vitiates the holding of the Court of Appeals in Matter of Friedman v Lefkowitz (42 NY2d 408), wherein the court upheld the issuance by a Deputy Attorney-General of an office subpoena duces tecum to compel the production of certain books and records of a private proprietary home for adults; the sole purpose of the amendments was to provide the statutory authorization, found lacking by the Court of Appeals in the Friedman case, for retention of possession of the records, and criteria set forth therein for determining the reasonableness of such possession, including whether good cause is shown for the issuance of the subpoena, are addressed to the time, terms and conditions of possession, and were not intended to limit the right to production, which [***9] was upheld in the Friedman case, but to define the newly conferred right to possession.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Retention of Possession of Subpoenaed Records

2. It is not unconstitutional to authorize a governmental agency to obtain possession of evidence obtained pursuant to a subpoena duces tecum without establishing probable cause to believe that a crime has been committed.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum

3. A subpoena duces tecum is not the equivalent of a search warrant, for which a showing of probable cause is required; to sustain the subpoena, it is sufficient to show that the inquiry is lawfully and properly authorized and the description of the materials sought is reasonably definite.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Retention of Possession of Subpoenaed Records

4. Chapter 451 of the Laws of 1977, which amends section 63 (subd 8) of the Executive Law, and CPLR 2305, and which provides that, whenever a governmental agency or officer thereof is authorized to issue a subpoena requiring the production of books or records, the issuing party shall have the right to the possession of such material for a period of time, and [***10] on terms and conditions as may reasonably be required for the inspection, examination or audit of the material, and that the reasonableness of such possession, time, terms and conditions shall be determined with consideration for, among other things, the good cause shown by the issuing party, the rights and needs of the person subpoenaed, and the feasibility and appropriateness of making copies of the material, is not unconstitutionally vague; the standards set forth therein are plainly sufficient to guide the court in fixing the limitations to be imposed upon the possession authorized by the statute.

 

1163 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Katz v. Shapiro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


62 A.D.2d 231; 405 N.Y.S.2d 315; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10443



April 27, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant operators sought review of the decision of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) that found that the annual financial reporting requirements imposed by respondent state on private proprietary homes for adults was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The lower court found that the annual financial reporting requirements that were imposed by the state on the operators who ran private proprietary homes for adults was constitutional. The court affirmed because it found that the state was not precluded by N.Y. Const. art. XVII, § 2 from requiring the operators to prepare and submit annual financial reports. The court found that the provisions of N.Y. Exec. Law § 758(9) did not deny the operators' right to equal protection of the laws even though not for profit homes for the aged did not have to report. The court found that the costs compelled by the requirement to prepare annual financial reports did not constitute an arbitrary and capricious interference with the operators' right to conduct its business in violation of their due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that found that the annual financial reporting requirements imposed by the state on the operators who ran private proprietary homes for adults was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Private Proprietary Homes for Adults

1. A statute prescribing annual financial reporting for private proprietary homes for adults is not precluded by or in violation of the provisions of section 2 of article XVII of the State Constitution, since that section, rather than limiting the powers of the State Board of Social Welfare to visitation, inspection and supervision of homes for the aged, affirms the right of the board to properly exercise its inherent police powers to further regulate the operation of homes for the aged. (Executive Law, § 758, subd 9 [former]; Social Services Law, § 461-b, subd 5.)

 [***3] Social Services -- Private Proprietary Homes for Adults

2. A statute prescribing annual financial reporting for private proprietary homes for adults, as distinguished from similar nonprofit facilities which provide identical services, has a rational basis and does not deny the proprietary facilities their right to equal protection. (Executive Law, § 758, subd 9 [former]; Social Services Law, § 461-b, subd 5.)

Social Services -- Private Proprietary Homes for Adults

3. A statute prescribing annual financial reporting for private proprietary homes for adults, necessarily resulting in additional costs, does not constitute an arbitrary and unreasonable interference with private business in violation of due process, since the regulation of such businesses is within the police power of the State and the requirement of annual financial statements is reasonably suited to that regulation. (Executive Law, § 758, subd 9 [former]; Social Services Law, § 461-b, subd 5.)
