Di Bella v. Di Bella


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


61 A.D.2d 956; 403 N.Y.S.2d 49; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10553



 March 23, 1978 

 

1175 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mandel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


61 A.D.2d 563; 403 N.Y.S.2d 63; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10100



March 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Two judgments of the Supreme Court, Queens County, rendered December 9, 1975 (one as to defendant Mandel and one as to defendant De Vito) and judgment of the same court, rendered January 6, 1976 (as to defendant Buckley), reversed, on the law, and new trial ordered. The findings of fact are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of two judgments of conviction ordered by the Supreme Court of the State of New York, County of Queens, on charges of rape in the first degree, sexual abuse in the first degree and assault in the second degree because the trial court erred by permitting the application of N.Y. Crim. Prac. Law § 60.42 that estopped defendants from cross-examining the victim about her prior conduct because the law was an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted and convicted of rape in the first degree, sodomy in the first degree, sexual abuse in the first degree, and assault in the second degree and challenged their convictions on the basis that the rape-shield law, contained in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law. § 60.42 was an ex-post facto law that violated U.S. Const. art. 1, § 10, amend. VI, XIV. While the court found N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.42 to be constitutional, it nonetheless reversed defendants' convictions. The court held that the evidence that was excluded by the trial court was of substantial probative value and was not offered to demean or harass the victim. The court reasoned that because such evidence went to the victim's credibility, mental and emotional condition, and her ability to perceive and recall past events it did not trigger protection under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.42. Furthermore, the court held that evidence of three similar false rape complaints was exempted from protection under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.42, as well as was evidence that the victim was a "schizophrenic-catatonic type."


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' rape, sexual abuse, and assault convictions because evidence of the victim's three prior false rape complaints and schizophrenic condition were not protected under New York's rape-shield law because these issues went to the victim's credibility, mental and emotional condition, and ability to perceive and recall past events and were of substantial probative value.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Evidence of Complainant's Mental History

1. In the prosecution of a sexual offense case, the defense should not be restricted from exploring hospital records, replete with references to the prior sexual conduct and history of the complainant, insofar as they are relevant to the credibility and emotional and mental history of the complainant, and in such a situation, where the defense was not fully allowed to explore such hospital records, the prosecutor committed reversible error in arguing [***2] upon summation that the complainant's mental problems were solely the result of "depression". (See CPL 60.42.)

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Improper Efforts to Discredit Testimony of Alibi Witnesses

2. It was improper and prejudicial, upon summation, for the prosecutor to argue that alibi witnesses' failure to appear at the District Attorney's office to provide information, although requested to do so, made their alibi testimony unworthy of belief.

 

1176 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Frank v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


61 A.D.2d 466; 402 N.Y.S.2d 860; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9761



March 15, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County, entered March 3, 1978, reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment denied, defendants' cross motion for summary judgment granted, and it is declared that Amendments One, Two and Three to the New York State Constitution approved at the general election held on November 8, 1977 were validly approved and are, and have been, a part of the Constitution of the State of New York, since January 1, 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State of New York and related others, challenged a decision by the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied their cross-motion for summary judgment and granted plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment. The supreme court ruled that the voter approval of amendments one, two, and three to the Constitution of the State of New York were null and void.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs alleged that the action of the 1977 legislature in submitting the amendments to the electorate to be voted on separately made the submission null and void, and that despite their approval by the voters, they never were, in fact, validly adopted. Plaintiffs argued that the concurrent resolution passed by both houses of the legislature in 1976 provided for the purported amendments to the state constitution to be presented for approval and ratification by the people as a single unit and package. The court disagreed with plaintiffs and found that the 1976 concurrent resolution did not contain a single reference to the manner in which the proposed amendments were to be submitted to the electorate for approval. Because it was clear beyond dispute that the wording of the proposed amendments as contained in the concurrent resolution adopted by the 1976 legislature was identical with the wording of the proposed amendments of the concurrent resolution adopted by the 1977 legislature, their approval by the people at the general election held in November 1977 should not have been frustrated. Finally, the amendments were presumptively constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the supreme court's judgment, denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment, and granted defendants' cross-motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Amendments to New York Constitution

1. Amendments to the New York Constitution, providing for a change in the way Judges of the Court of Appeals are selected, the creation of a commission on judicial conduct and the administration of the unified court system, which amendments [***2] were embodied in a concurrent resolution of the 1976 Legislature, were submitted to the electorate to be voted on separately by the 1977 Legislature and were approved by the People in November, 1977, are not null and void on the ground that the 1976 Legislature provided that the amendments be submitted to the electorate as a single package since the 1976 concurrent resolution did not contain any reference to the manner in which the amendments were to be submitted for approval, but rather stated, in accordance with section 1 of article XIX of the Constitution, that amendments were to be referred to the first regular legislative session [1977] convening after the next general election for members of the Legislature. The succeeding Legislature [1977] to which the proposed amendments were to be referred is the one that has the duty to submit them to the People "in such manner" as it shall prescribe (NY Const, art XIX, § 1) and that is exactly what the 1977 Legislature did, and, upon approval by the People, the amendments became part of the Constitution.

Constitutional Law -- Presumption of Constitutionality

2. The presumption of the constitutionality of an enactment of [***3] the Legislature is so strong that a plaintiff must prove the contrary beyond a reasonable doubt and, absent a clear showing of unconstitutionality, such an enactment must be sustained.

 

1177 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Calderon v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 323; 402 N.Y.S.2d 685; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9741; 3 Media L. Rep. 2454



March 1, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a decision of the Supreme Court at a Special Term in Erie County (New York), which found for plaintiff smoke shop owner on his action for declaratory judgment and found that certain provisions of the Anti-Obscenity and Display to Minors Ordinance, Ordinances of City of Buffalo, ch. 9, § 35, were unconstitutional and enjoined the enforcement of those provisions.


OVERVIEW: The shop owner was charged with a violation of the city's Anti-Obscenity and Display to Minors Ordinance. After the charge was dismissed upon the city's failure to appear for trial, he filed an action for a declaratory judgment that those provisions of the ordinance which prohibited the display of certain materials were unconstitutionally overbroad and vague. The trial court found in favor of the shop owner and enjoined enforcement of those provisions. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the shop owner had standing because he sought to resolve a First Amendment challenge to an ordinance under which he had once been criminally charged and threatened with further prosecution. The court held that Ordinances of City of Buffalo, ch. 9, § 35(j) was unconstitutionally overbroad in that it included material not obscene as to either minors or adults and thus entitled to First Amendment protection. In addition to the problem of overbroadness, the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. As to prohibitions against materials, which were "obscene, lewd, lascivious, or indecent," only "obscene" was defined and the other terms were open to a wide range of interpretations.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding that certain provisions of the city's Anti-Obscenity and Display to Minors Ordinance was unconstitutional and enjoining enforcement of those provisions was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Statute as Violative of First Amendment Rights

1. Where First Amendment rights are at stake, litigants are permitted to challenge a statute not because their own rights of free expression are violated, but because of a judicial prediction or assumption that the statute's very existence may cause others not before the court to refrain from constitutionally protected speech or expression, nor is a person required to show that his own conduct could not be regulated by a narrow construction of the statute under attack.

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Statute as Violative of First Amendment Rights

 [***3] 2. A litigant who has once been criminally charged under an ordinance should not be forced to await prosecution before challenging it as violative of First Amendment rights.

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Constitutional Validity of Statute

3. An action for a declaratory judgment is the proper method of testing the constitutional validity of a statute.

Crimes -- Obscenity

4. The State has a legitimate interest in attempting to stem the tide of commercialized obscenity, and, even more compelling than its interest in regulating the dissemination of obscenity to the public at large is its interest in protecting children from the onslaught of sexually offensive material.

Crimes -- Obscenity

5. Legislation aimed at protecting children from allegedly harmful expression must, like legislation enacted with respect to adults, be clearly drawn and the standards adopted must be reasonably precise so that those who are governed by the law and those who administer it will understand its meaning and application.

Crimes -- Obscenity

6. Legislation aimed at protecting children from allegedly harmful expression must be drawn so as [***4] not to bring about the suppression of the sale to adults of material that is, in fact, constitutionally protected.

Crimes -- Obscenity -- Display of Sexually Explicit Materials to Minors

7. Provisions of a city ordinance, which relate to the sale or exhibition of indecent materials to minors, and which, inter alia, prohibit the display of sexually explicit materials at newsstands or other business establishments frequented by minors or where minors are or may be invited as part of the general public, but which contain no requirement that the proscribed material be harmful to minors, and no exception for dissemination or conduct in the course of law enforcement activities or in bona fide scientific, educational or comparable research or study, are unconstitutionally overbroad, in that they include material not obscene as to either minors or adults and thus entitled to First Amendment protection.

Constitutional Law -- Legislation Carrying Penal Sanctions

8. Legislation carrying penal sanctions must be definite enough to give persons of ordinary intelligence a reasonable opportunity to know what is prohibited and must provide explicit standards in order to [***5] prevent arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.

Crimes -- Obscenity -- Display of Sexually Explicit Materials to Minors

9. Provisions of a city ordinance, which relate to the sale or exhibition of indecent materials to minors, and which, inter alia, prohibit the display of sexually explicit materials at newsstands or other business establishments frequented by minors or where minors are or may be invited as part of the general public, are unconstitutionally vague, in that they fail to define the materials proscribed with sufficient clarity and specificity.

 

1178 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Belle v. Town Bd. of Onondaga


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 352; 402 N.Y.S.2d 677; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9745



March 1, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, without costs, petition dismissed, and determination confirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town board sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Onondaga County (New York), which voided the town's adoption of an amendment to Town of Onondaga, N.Y., Town Code of Ethics art. II, § 3(k) (the Code of Ethics), and reinstated to their positions respondents, members of the town's zoning board of appeals, who had been removed following a public hearing.


OVERVIEW: The Code of Ethics art. II, § 3(k) prohibited members of the town zoning board of appeals from holding chairman positions of a political party, and the board members were removed from the zoning board of appeals. Upon petition, the trial court voided § 3(k) and directed that the board members be reinstated as members. On appeal pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, the court reversed the judgment, dismissed the petition, and confirmed the removal of the board members, ruling that their admitted political positions amounted to a clear violation of the Code of Ethics art. II, § 3(k). The court concluded that, although the issue of whether their removals as members were based on substantial evidence was not considered by the trial court, the court could consider the issue pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(g). The court held that the town did not exceed its authority in passing the Code of Ethics art. II, § 3(k), that its prohibition against holding office in a political party was reasonably calculated to achieve a legitimate public purpose, and that it was not inconsistent with the constitution and state law, which complied with N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, dismissed the petition, and confirmed the removal of the board members from their positions as members of the zoning board of appeals in the action testing the constitutionality of the local legislative enactment.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding Against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Appropriate

1. While an article 78 proceeding is generally not the proper method to test the constitutionality of a legislative enactment, it is an appropriate method of collaterally attacking an administrative action threatened or taken pursuant to an allegedly invalid law.

Municipal Corporations --  [***3] Local Laws

2. As a general proposition, local laws which do not prohibit what the State law permits nor allow what the State law forbids are not inconsistent with the State law.

Constitutional Law -- Powers of Local Governments to Adopt Local Laws

3. Any interpretation of the constitutional and statutory limitations upon the adoption by municipalities of local laws (NY Const, art IX, § 2; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd 1) which would prohibit municipal regulation of matters upon which the State Constitution and general laws are silent would contravene the right to legislate on matters of local concern, reserved to municipalities under article IX ( § 1) of the State Constitution; consequently, a "pre-emption doctrine" has evolved which precludes local legislation only in subject areas marked by a pervasive scheme of State legislation.

Municipal Corporations -- Municipal Code of Ethics

4. In enacting section 806 of the General Municipal Law, which mandates the governing body of each county, city, town, village and school district, and authorizes the governing body of any other municipality, to adopt a code of ethics setting forth standards of conduct [***4] for its officers and employees with respect to specified matters "and such other standards relating to the conduct of officers and employees as may be deemed advisable", and also provides that such codes "may regulate or prescribe conduct which is not expressly prohibited by this article * * * [and] may provide for the prohibition of conduct", the State has clearly expressed an intention not to occupy the field of ethical standards for officers and employees of local governments.

Municipal Corporations -- Local Laws

5. A local law should be sustained if it bears a substantial relation to matters within a field where legislative power is vested in the local legislative body, and is reasonably calculated to achieve a legitimate public purpose.

Municipal Corporations -- Municipal Code of Ethics -- Restrictions on Political Activities

6. Regulation and promulgation of ethical standards for public employees serve a legitimate public purpose, and restrictions on partisan political activity of public officers in this State have been sustained as necessary and proper methods of maintaining public confidence in government.

Municipal Corporations -- Municipal  [***5] Code of Ethics -- Restrictions on Political Activities

7. Municipalities may enact local laws restricting municipal officers, employees and board members from holding offices in political parties, under the authority of section 806 of the General Municipal Law, which mandates the governing body of each county, city, town, village and school district, and authorizes the governing body of any other municipality, to adopt a code of ethics setting forth standards of conduct for its officers and employees with respect to specified matters "and such other standards relating to the conduct of officers and employees as may be deemed advisable", and also provides that such codes "may regulate or prescribe conduct which is not expressly prohibited by this article * * * [and] may provide for the prohibition of conduct".

Constitutional Law -- Restrictions on Political Activities -- Municipal Officers and Employees

8. Legislation which prohibits municipal officers, employees and board members from holding offices in political parties is not in contravention of their rights under the First and Fourteenth Amendments.

 

1179 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


TJPC Restaurant Corp. v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 441; 402 N.Y.S.2d 483; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9758



March 1, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Petition granted [***2] and determination annulled, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner restaurant sought review pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to annul a determination by respondent New York State Liquor Authority, which found that the restaurant had permitted the licensed premises to become disorderly, because female entertainers were allowed to dance in the nude, under N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 106(6) but did not find that the conduct was lewd or indecent per se.


OVERVIEW: The restaurant apparently allowed female entertainers to dance in the nude, which prompted the action by the Authority. On review the court found that in the absence of regulation by the Authority that conduct not lewd or indecent per se was proscribed, the Authority could not impose sanctions for such conduct on licensees. The court noted that the challenged conduct was not found to be lewd or indecent per se, and it occurred prior to the adoption of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 53.1, which specified various types of prohibited performances. The court anticipated the argument that Rochester, N.Y. Municipal Code § 44-8(B) prohibited nudity in a public place and held that the ordinance was patently unconstitutional for overbreadth and could not have been relied upon as giving notice of prohibited conduct. The court noted that the city could not regulate the performance in the restaurant's premises because the state had pre-empted the field and gave the Authority exclusive jurisdiction to regulate such premises in N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 2.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the Authority's determination and granted the restaurant's petition.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

1. A determination by the State Liquor Authority which found that petitioner had permitted licensed premises to become disorderly because female entertainers were allowed to dance in the nude (see Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 106, subd 6), but which conduct was not found to be lewd or indecent per se and occurred prior to the adoption of statutes specifying various types of prohibited performances, should be annulled in the absence of a regulation by the agency giving notice that conduct not lewd or indecent per se was proscribed.

Constitutional Law-Prohibition Against Nudity in "Public Place"

2. An ordinance which prohibits nudity in a "public place", is patently unconstitutional for overbreadth and may not be relied upon as giving notice of prohibited conduct. (Municipal Code of City of Rochester, § 44-8, subd B.)

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

3. A city may not regulate entertainment in a licensee's premises, for the State has pre-empted the field and given the State Liquor Authority exclusive jurisdiction to regulate such premises. (Alcoholic Beverage Control [***3] Law, § 2.)

 

1180 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ruddy v. Connelie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


61 A.D.2d 372; 402 N.Y.S.2d 245; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9748; 22 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 599; 17 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8512; 22 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,784



February 23, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Division of State Police and the Superintendent of New York State Police, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, in proceedings under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 filed by petitioner trooper candidates, enjoined respondents from making appointments to the position of trooper in the New York State Police in any manner other than rank order from an existing eligibility list.


OVERVIEW: Respondents conducted a two-part competitive examination for the purpose of filling state trooper positions. The candidates were ranked in order of their examination scores and were placed on an eligibility list for appointment. When funding for hiring became available, respondents sought to depart from the test results in order to appoint ethnic minorities and female candidates. Petitioners, who were white males on the eligibility list, objected to the procedure. The trial court ruled in favor of petitioners, and the court affirmed. The court held that whatever the motivation, respondents' proposal fell beyond those topics to which consideration was constitutionally limited by N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 and had to be enjoined. Respondents could not constitutionally depart from the results of the competitive examination in order to make appointments allowing a preference to minorities and females. As respondents' course of conduct plainly exceeded the standard requiring that fitness alone be conclusive, the judgment for petitioners was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Preference Granted to Minorities -- Competitive Examination for State Trooper

1. The Division of State Police may not constitutionally depart from the apparent results of an examination to the extent of making appointments that allow a preference to ethnic minorities and females.

Civil Service -- Preference Granted to Minorities -- Competitive Examination for State Trooper

2. The use by the Division of State Police of a sexually mixed eligible list signifies that the position of trooper is not one for which the work demands individuals of one sex, and thereby eliminates application of subdivision 2 of section 60 of the Civil Service Law, limiting certification from an eligible list to one sex where the nature [***2] of the work requires sex selection.

 

1181 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Dodge Mill Land Corp. v. Amherst


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 216; 402 N.Y.S.2d 670; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9724



February 17, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, without costs, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment denied, defendants' motion for summary judgment granted and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court of Erie County (New York) which granted plaintiff corporation's motion for summary judgment, denied the town's motion for summary judgment, and declared the town's rezoning of the corporation's subject property unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: As part of a comprehensive rezoning map the town enacted a new ordinance which resulted in a portion of the corporation's property being zoned as residential. The corporation filed an action to challenge the zoning ordinance. The trial court held that the rezoning of the corporation's property was unconstitutional, granted the corporation's motion for summary judgment, and denied the town's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and granted the town's motion for summary judgment. The zoning lines drawn by the town allowed many of the surrounding properties to be used for business purposes. However, such invidious zoning lines were inconsequential because the corporation failed to present evidence of the bona fide efforts made to use, sell, or lease the property profitably, so as to entitle it to have such zoning declared invalid as applied to its property. In dismissing the corporation's action without prejudice the court remarked that upon such proof the corporation might succeed in an action to declare the ordinance invalid as to its property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment which granted the corporation's motion for summary judgment, denied the town's motion for summary judgment, and declared the town's rezoning of the corporation's subject property unconstitutional. The court granted the town's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the corporation's complaint without prejudice.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

1. A zoning ordinance based upon a comprehensive plan is entitled to the strongest possible presumption of validity.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

2. Unless the zoning restrictions in a district deprive an owner of profitable use of his property, he has no redress, even though the restrictions reduce the value of the property.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

3. When establishing a use district, the fact that properties in the district do not conform to the permitted uses neither prevents the municipality from creating the district nor the owners from continuing their occupation thereof as nonconforming uses, the latter, at least, until the municipality takes steps to phase them out.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

4. Proof that zoning restrictions deprive an owner of profitable use of his property entails a showing of the [***4] bona fide efforts made to use, sell or lease the property profitably under the new zoning.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Reclassification

5. A rezoning of property from business to residential use may, in certain circumstances, constitute an unconstitutional or arbitrary exercise of police power, and in such situations an owner may proceed directly to have the ordinance declared invalid as to his property without exhausting his administrative remedies under the ordinance.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Reclassification

6. Where plaintiff's property is at the boundary of a residential zone, with commercial zones adjoining on the west, the happenstance that two properties to the northeast and east of it enjoy nonconforming uses cannot serve to render the zoning ordinance invalid as an unconstitutional or arbitrary exercise of the police power.

 

1182 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Crystal v. Board of Education


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


61 A.D.2d 813; 402 N.Y.S.2d 47; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10261



 February 14, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, six school nurse teachers and a dental hygienist, appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which dismissed petitioners' action seeking an order compelling respondent school district to reinstate them.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners' positions were subject to vertical tenure as opposed to the horizontal tenure afforded regular teaching positions. Petitioners' positions were abolished in accordance with N.Y. Educ. Law § 2510(2). The trial court dismissed petitioners' action holding that the positions abolished were traditional special tenure areas that had not been radically restructured without reference to a controlling regulation or standard. Petitioners' contended that § 2510(2) was unconstitutional as applied to petitioners. The court disagreed, affirming the dismissal. To prove a violation of their equal protection rights, petitioners' carried the burden of showing § 2510(2) had been applied with the intent to discriminate. Petitioners' failed to carry their heavy burden and thus, their action was properly dismissed. Furthermore, the classification of petitioners' positions in a vertical tenure area had a rational basis and was not itself discriminatory.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of claim's order dismissing petitioners' action.

 

1183 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gersten v. Gersten


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


61 A.D.2d 745; 401 N.Y.S.2d 806; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10125



 February 7, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife filed an action for dissolution of marriage against defendant husband. The wife filed a motion for temporary alimony, temporary child support, and exclusive occupancy of the marital domicile. The husband sought review of the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied the husband's cross-motion to dismiss, granted the wife's motion, and enjoined the parties from litigating in Connecticut.


OVERVIEW: The husband was living in Connecticut with one child. The wife was living in New York with another child. The wife filed a dissolution of marriage action in New York. The husband filed an action in Connecticut. The husband claimed that the long-arm statute, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(b), was unconstitutional. The court modified the trial court's order. The court held that jurisdiction was properly obtained under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(b), that the wife was a resident of and domiciled in the state, that the state was the matrimonial domicile of the parties, that the husband's obligation to pay support accrued under the laws of the state, and that the trial court properly denied the husband's motion to dismiss. The court held, however, that one child was living in Connecticut, that there was an outstanding order in Connecticut regarding the child's custody, and that the injunction was required to be modified to permit proceedings in Connecticut with regard to the custody and visitation of the child living in Connecticut.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the husband's motion to dismiss the complaint. The court modified the trial court's injunction preventing the parties from further proceedings in Connecticut and added that proceedings with regard to custody and visitation of the child in Connecticut were not enjoined.

 

1184 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Ronald B. (Anonymous)


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


61 A.D.2d 204; 401 N.Y.S.2d 544; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9722



January 30, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Family Court, Kings County, dated September 1, 1977, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged the order of the Family Court, Kings County (New York), finding that he was a juvenile delinquent and requiring his placement with the Commissioner of Social Services for a period of 18 months.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile was found in possession of a gun at school. Refusing to suppress the gun and admitting into evidence a ballistics report, the lower court found appellant was a juvenile delinquent and ordered that he be placed with a state agency for a period of 18 months. On appeal, the court affirmed. U.S. Const. amend. IV required only that a search be reasonable. School officials, acting in loco parentis, had the right to frisk appellant based on a reasonable suspicion that he had a gun. When he made a suspicious movement, they merely grabbed his hand and found a gun in it. Further, the ballistics report was properly admitted under the business records exception to the hearsay rule. Part of the business of the police was litigation, and a test in furtherance of that purpose or to determine criminal activity, if reliable, was allowed into evidence without requiring expert testimony.


OUTCOME: The lower court's order finding appellant was a juvenile delinquent and requiring his placement with the Commissioner of Social Services for a period of 18 months was affirmed because the gun and the ballistics report were properly admitted into evidence.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

1. Students are protected from unreasonable searches and seizures by agents of the State, whether they are school officials or police officers.

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

2. A search by a school official need not be based on probable cause.

Schools -- Doctrine of In Loco Parentis

3. Officials in a school act in loco parentis, which concept allows school personnel to exercise such powers of control, restraint and correction over pupils as is reasonably necessary to facilitate the educational functions of a school.

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

4. In determining [***3] the reasonableness of a school search, factors to be considered include the child's age, history and record in the school, the prevalence and seriousness of the problem in the school to which the search was directed, and the exigency to make the search without delay.

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

5. A school official has the right to "frisk" a child based on a reasonable suspicion that he has a gun.

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

6. When school officials, in response to a suspicious movement made by a student, merely grabbed his hand and found a gun in it, the officials did not in fact search the student.

Schools -- Searches and Seizures by School Officials

7. A school official, standing in loco parentis to the children entrusted to his care, has the obligation to protect them while in his charge, so far as possible from harmful and dangerous influences.

Evidence -- Business Records

8. A ballistics report is admissible into evidence under the business records exception to the hearsay rule, thus eliminating the need for testimony of the police officer who conducted the test. ( CPLR 4518,  [***4] subd [a].)

 

1185 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rozler v. Franger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 46; 401 N.Y.S.2d 623; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9701



January 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, on the law, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant trustees appealed from a decision of the Special Term of Erie County (New York), which issued a judgment in favor of plaintiff mayor and determined that a local law, allowing the trustees to amend the Village Law, to be null and void.


OVERVIEW: The village trustees enacted local law 10, which vested in the trustees the power to appoint and terminate officials and employees of the village. The local law further provided for the repeal of certain sections of the village law that vested powers in the village mayor. The mayor sued to have the local law determined null and void because the village law was a general law and the Home Rule Law allowed enactment of local laws that were not inconsistent with general laws. The court reversed and remanded the judgment for the mayor. The village law was a general law because it applied to all villages. However, section 10 of the Home Rule Law did not prevent a village from superseding a village law in a way that was inconsistent with the village law. The words not inconsistent with any general law had to be read as being impliedly subject to an exception where the inconsistency has been specifically authorized by the legislature in the same section as the limitation. In order for section 10 of the Home Rule Law to have effect that was the way the statute had to be read. That construction was in keeping with the policy of liberally construing the Home Rule Law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's determination that a local law allowing the trustees to amend the Village Law to be null and void.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Power of Village to Amend or Supersede Village Law

1. Section 10 (subd 1, par [ii], cl e, subcl [3]) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, which permits a village to adopt a local law amending or superseding, in its application to it, any provision of the Village Law relating to the property, affairs or government of the village "notwithstanding that such provision is a general law, unless the legislature expressly shall have prohibited the adoption of such a local law", is not subject to the limitation, set forth in preceding provisions of that section, that any local law adopted thereunder must not be "inconsistent with any general law", and, accordingly, permits a village to amend or supersede the Village Law,  [***4] a general law, with respect to its own property, affairs or government.

Constitutional Law -- Powers of Local Governments to Adopt Local Laws

2. Section 10 (subd 1, par [ii], cl e, subcl [3]) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, which permits a village to amend or supersede the Village Law with respect to its own property, affairs or government, is not violative of article IX (§ 2) of the New York State Constitution, relating to the powers of local governments to adopt local laws.

Statutes -- General Laws -- Village Law

3. The Village Law is a general law as defined in the New York State Constitution (art IX, § 3, subd [d]) and the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 2, subd 5), both of which include in the definition of a general law a law or statute "which in terms and in effect applies alike to all * * * villages".

Statutes -- Construction

4. All parts of a statute must be harmonized with each other and with the general intent of a statute, and each part must, if possible, be given effect.

Statutes -- Construction

5. A subsequent statute in pari materia with an earlier one may be considered as an aid in the construction of the earlier [***5] statute.

Constitutional Law -- Powers of Local Governments to Adopt Local Laws

6. Section 2 of article IX of the New York State Constitution is not a limitation on the power of local governments to adopt and amend local laws, but is a grant of power "[in] addition to powers granted in the statute of local governments or in any other law" (subd [c]), including those powers granted by the Municipal Home Rule Law.

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers

7. The laws granting home rule powers are to be liberally construed.

Municipal Corporations -- Power of Village to Amend or Supersede Village Law

8. A village has the power, under section 10 (subd 1, par [ii], cl e, subcl [3]) of the Municipal Home Rule Law, to supersede or amend, in their particular application to the village, those provisions of the Village Law which vest in the Mayor of the village the power to appoint, supervise and terminate nonelected officers and employees of the village, and to enact a local law vesting that power in the village trustees.

 

1186 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bethlehem Steel Corp. v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


61 A.D.2d 147; 402 N.Y.S.2d 655; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9715



January 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Judgments unanimously modified, on the law, in accordance with opinion by Hancock, Jr., J., and, as modified affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school districts challenged judgments from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Erie County, Ontario County, and Monroe County (New York), which declared unconstitutional 1976 N.Y. Laws 349 and 484, and which determined that some appropriations for pension, health insurance expenses, and real property tax levies were illegal. The appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that 1976 N.Y. Laws 349 and 484 were unconstitutional, found that some appropriations for pension and health insurance expenses were illegal, and directed respondent corporation to adopt and publish a revised final budget to eliminate said amounts from the computation of the constitutional tax limitation. In the other actions, in which taxpayers sought to recover alleged illegal overpayments from their taxing districts, the trial court granted summary judgment for the amounts of the overpayments. On appeal, the court found that that 1976 N.Y. Laws 349 and 484 were unconstitutional and that the amounts appropriated for pension and health insurance expenses were improperly excluded from the tax limit. However, the court modified the judgments in the other actions to delete the provisions directing repayments. The court also modified the judgment in the first action to delete the provision directing the board of education to revise its final budget to exclude the appropriations for certain pension, social security and health insurance expenses.


OUTCOME: The court modified the special term judgments and, as modified, affirmed them without costs.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

1. The Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2), which establishes a period of probable usefulness of three years for the costs of retirement and Social Security for the cities of Buffalo and Rochester and certain school districts, when read in conjunction with subdivision (b) of section 11 of article VIII of the New York Constitution, permits these municipalities and school districts to exclude the amounts appropriated [***5] for retirement and Social Security costs from the computation of the total amount of revenue that may be raised by the real property tax levy under the limitations established by section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

2. The State Real Property Tax Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 3), a companion stand-by statute to the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2), in the event the latter is declared unconstitutional, allows the governing body of the cities of Buffalo and Rochester and certain school districts to request the State Comptroller to make State aid payments to the municipality in an amount not to exceed a maximum amount set forth for each municipality in the statute, which maximum amounts are equivalent to the amounts which the municipalities would have been able to exclude from the tax limitation under the Emergency City and School District Relief Act.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

3. The temporary nature of the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2), as opposed to the on-going grant of authority [***6] for the exclusion of retirement and pension contributions from the constitutional tax limit contained in paragraph 42-a of subdivision a of section 11.00 of the Local Finance Law, declared unconstitutional in Hurd v City of Buffalo (41 AD2d 402, affd 34 NY2d 628), makes it no less a measure to evade section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution, limiting taxes on real property, which is "palpably in violation of the plan and purpose" of the "unified and interdependent plan to control the taxing and debt-contracting power of the subdivisions of the State" condemned by the court in Hurd.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

4. Although the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2) limits the amount of appropriations for pension and Social Security benefits that may be excluded from the tax limit in the second, third and fourth fiscal years to the amount excluded in the first year, while paragraph 42-a of subdivision a of section 11.00 of the Local Finance Law, declared unconstitutional in Hurd v City of Buffalo, 41 AD2d 402, affd 34 NY2d 628), excluded all of the amounts for pensions and Social Security,  [***7] there is no greater reason for finding a rational basis in the legislative determination that pension and Social Security benefits have a period of probable usefulness of three years in the Emergency City and School District Relief Act than in paragraph 42-a of subdivision a of section 11.00 of the Finance Law.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

5. The Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2) does not provide a temporary emergency solution to the fiscal and social problems of the cities and school districts so as to warrant suspension of constitutional limitations under the State police power.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

6. In view of the fact that the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2) is a device to evade the plan to control the taxing and debt-contracting power of the subdivisions of the State, contrary to Hurd v City of Buffalo (41 AD2d 402, affd 34 NY2d 628), its companion stand-by statute, the State Real Property Tax Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 3), which has as its purpose the exact similar goal of financing the operations of certain [***8] cities and school districts and providing a temporary emergency solution to the problem currently being encountered by the cities and school districts in order to avoid fiscal and social chaos, is equally unconstitutional.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

7. That the State Real Property Tax Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 3) is labeled a State tax and that the tax is to be collected by the State and remitted to the cities and school districts through the device of State aid, does not make it a State tax, thus exempting it from the limitations imposed by section 10 of article VIII of the New York State Constitution, since it has the same purpose and effect as the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2), concededly a measure designed to permit local taxes.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

8. Because of its lack of uniformity of application, the State Real Property Tax Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 3) could not be considered to be a State tax without being in violation of the equal protection and due process clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and sections 6 [***9] and 11 of article I of the New York Constitution.

Parties -- Standing

9. The fact that the State Real Property Tax Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 3) is a companion stand-by statute to the Emergency City and School District Relief Act (L 1976, ch 349, § 2) and the cities and school districts will be permitted to take steps to implement it and have taxes levied under it only if and when the Court of Appeals declares the latter act to be unconstitutional, does not make the challenge to the Real Property Tax Act's constitutionality prospective, thus presenting no justiciable controversy.

Declaratory Judgments -- Advisory Judgment

10. The fact that the court may be required to determine the rights of the parties upon the happening of a future event does not mean that the declaratory judgment will be merely advisory; where the future event is an act contemplated by one of the parties, it is assumed that the parties will act in accordance with the law and thus the court's determination will have the immediate and practical effect of influencing their conduct.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

11. Chapter 484 of the Laws of 1976 (State [***10] Aid for School Districts Act), which assigns to the cost of current health and dental insurance coverage a period of probable usefulness of three years, resulting in the exclusion of those amounts from the tax limitations prescribed by section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution, is unconstitutional.

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

12. The 2% tax limit for "city purposes" imposed by subdivision (b) of section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution should not be construed as excluding sums appropriated for educational purposes.

Constitutional Law -- Limitation of Real Property Taxes

13. The tax limitations of section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution are not based on unlawful and suspect classifications so as to render the provision unconstitutional under the equal protection and due process clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution.

Taxation -- Repayment of Illegal Tax

14. Although the legislation relied upon in attempting to exclude appropriations for pension and Social Security expenses from school district tax limits (L 1976, ch 349) was unconstitutional [***11] in violation of section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution, to mandate repayments of those portions of real property taxes illegally collected under the 1976-1977 tax levies in reliance on the statute, which was not then under challenge, would place an impossible burden on the municipalities, which relied on the taxing authority granted in the statute in preparing their budgets.

 

1187 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mandels v. Liberty Mut. Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


60 A.D.2d 864; 401 N.Y.S.2d 255; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9898



January 16, 1978 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insurer appealed a decision of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which, granted plaintiff insured's motion to dismiss the insurer's affirmative defense and for summary judgment, inter alia, and declared that the insurer's disclaimer of indemnity of the insured's wife was improper, holding that a 1976 amendment to N.Y. Ins. Law § 167(3) came into effect before defendant persons' third-party cause of action arose.


OVERVIEW: The insured bought his liability policy in 1974. At the time, N.Y. Ins. Law § 167 provided that there was no obligation on the part of an insurance company to defend or indemnify the husband of an injured plaintiff who was sued for apportionment of damages. The insured's wife was injured in an accident with the persons on May 30, 1976. On August 1, 1976, an amendment to § 167 went into effect that provided for such coverage. The injured's wife brought an action against the persons, who then brought a third-party action against the insured seeking damage apportionment. The insurer disclaimed coverage. In the insured's ensuing action, the trial court granted the insured's motion to dismiss the insurer's affirmative defense and for summary judgment, and directed the insurer to defend and indemnify the insured for any judgment award against him in the third-party action. On appeal, the court reversed, and declared that the disclaimer was proper, concluding that the policy was purchased in consideration of a premium computed on the basis of § 167(3) prior to its amendment, and that any other interpretation might pose a question of unconstitutional impairment of contract.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment directing the insurer to defend and indemnify the insured in a third-party action for apportionment of damages in his wife's action against the persons. The court granted judgment in favor of the insurer and declared that its disclaimer was proper and that it was not required to defend and indemnify the insured for any judgment against him arising out of the acts alleged in the third-party action.

 

1188 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Zumbo v. Farmington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


60 A.D.2d 350; 401 N.Y.S.2d 121; 1978 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9680



January 13, 1978 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment unanimously reversed, with costs, and plaintiff's motion to dismiss affirmative defense granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, individual and wife, appealed an order from the Supreme Court, Wayne County (New York), which granted a motion by defendant town for summary judgment, denied their motion to dismiss the town's affirmative defense, and dismissed their negligence suit against the town after the individual was injured on the town's field.


OVERVIEW: The individual was injured on a recreational playing field during a softball game. The individual and his wife sued the town, claiming damages resulting from the town's negligent construction and maintenance of the field. The town moved for summary judgment, raising the affirmative defense of failure to comply with the Farmington, N.Y., Prior Notice Law Local Law § 16-1. The individual and his wife moved to dismiss the defense. When the Supreme Court granted the town's motion and dismissed the suit, the individual and his wife appealed. The court granted the motion to dismiss the defense and reversed the summary judgment to the town. No prior written notice of the unsafe condition of the field was required in order for the individual and his wife to sue the town. To the extent that the local law required such notice, it was inconsistent with the general laws governing requirements of prior notice of unsafe condition and was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss the town's affirmative defense and reversed the order, which granted the town's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

1. In order to successfully challenge a local law, a plaintiff must overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality that [***2] attaches to legislative enactments.

Municipal Corporations -- Notice of Claim -- General Laws

2. Section 50-e of the General Municipal Law is a general law, which is defined as one which in terms and in effect applies alike to all counties, all counties other than those wholly included within a city, all cities, all towns, or all villages (NY Const, art IX, § 3, subd [d], par [1]).

Municipal Corporations -- Prior Written Notice of Defective Condition

3. Section 50-e of the General Municipal Law allows municipalities to require notice of an unsafe condition as a condition precedent to a suit for damages caused thereby only with regard to a street, highway, bridge, culvert, sidewalk or crosswalk.

Municipal Corporations -- Local Laws

4. Where the State has acted upon a subject and, in so acting, has evidenced a desire that its general laws should pre-empt the possibility of varying local laws, a local enactment may be said to be inconsistent with State law because it prohibits that which has been found acceptable under State law.

Statutes -- Construction

5. Where a statute creates provisos or exceptions as to certain matters, the inclusion [***3] of such provisos or exceptions is generally considered to deny the existence of others not mentioned.

Statutes -- Construction -- Provisions in Derogation of Common Law

6. Requirements of prior written notice to a town of unsafe conditions are in derogation of the common law, and as such should not have any extension written into them.

Municipal Corporations -- Prior Written Notice of Defective Condition -- Playing Fields

7. The general laws of the State require no prior written notice of an unsafe condition of a recreational playing field in order to bring an action against a town for injuries sustained there, and to the extent that a local law requires such notice, it is inconsistent with the general laws limiting requirements of prior notice of unsafe condition to those pertaining to streets, highways, bridges, culverts, sidewalks or crosswalks and is, therefore, unconstitutional.

 

1189 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Coffman v. Coffman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


60 A.D.2d 181; 400 N.Y.S.2d 833; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14372



December 19, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order of the Supreme Court, Queens County, dated December 3, 1976, affirmed insofar as appealed from, with $ 50 costs and disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband sought review of the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Queens County (New York), which denied the husband's motion for summary judgment. Plaintiff wife's complaint sought an award equivalent to the value of any economic and property rights of which she was deprived by virtue of the judgment of divorce.


OVERVIEW: The wife filed a complaint pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170-a, seeking an award equivalent to the value of any economic and property rights of which she was deprived by virtue of the judgment of divorce obtained against her pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(5). The husband filed a motion to dismiss on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional in that, by its very terms, it was to be applied retroactively. The husband also alleged that it was an unconstitutional impairment of a contract and that the wife's waiver of any such rights precluded her from maintaining the action. The lower court denied the motion and the husband appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the waiver in the stipulation only dealt with the matters discussed therein. The court held that it was illogical to assume that at the time the release was executed, the parties could have contemplated that a statute would be enacted several years later. The court also held that the statute was not unconstitutional because it did not violate the prohibition against impairment of contracts contained in the Federal Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying the husband's motion for summary judgment in the wife's action seeking an award equivalent to the value of any economic and property rights of which she was deprived by virtue of the judgment of divorce.


HEADNOTES

 [***3] Release -- Uncontemplated Transactions

1. Although generally a release will only be set aside if duress, illegality or fraud is established, a releasor is entitled to prove that a general release was not intended to be applicable to uncontemplated transactions.

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute -- Remedial Statutes

2. Statutes are construed as prospective, unless the language of the statute, either expressly or by necessary implication, requires that it be given a retroactive construction; remedial statutes constitute an exception to that general rule, but only to the extent that they do not impair vested rights.

Statutes -- Remedial Statutes

3. Remedial statutes are those designed to correct imperfections in prior law by generally giving relief to the aggrieved party.

Husband and Wife -- Divorce -- Recovery of Marital Rights

4. Section 170-a of the Domestic Relations Law creates a cause of action for the recovery of marital rights unexpectedly lost due to the conversion into a no-fault divorce judgment against the plaintiff of separation arrangements made prior to January 21, 1970.

Husband and Wife -- Divorce -- Recovery  [***4] of Marital Rights

5. In an action brought pursuant to section 170-a of the Domestic Relations Law, recoverable marital rights may include Social Security and pension rights, the right to inherit from the spouse's estate, and a right in the marital status itself; additionally, the statute places a value on the prospective right of inheritance.

Husband and Wife -- Divorce -- Recovery of Marital Rights

6. At the trial of an action brought pursuant to section 170-a of the Domestic Relations Law to recover lost marital rights, such factors as the length of time the parties were separated, the wealth of the parties, the life expectancy of the parties, and any other appropriate factors may be considered to determine the extent of the award, if any, consistent with the provisions of subdivision c of section 170-a, which allows a court to deny any recovery "as justice may require."

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contract Rights -- Marriage

7. A statute which creates a cause of action for the recovery of marital rights unexpectedly lost due to the conversion into a no-fault divorce judgment against the plaintiff of separation arrangements made prior to January [***5] 21, 1970, does not unconstitutionally violate the prohibition against impairment of contracts contained in the Federal Constitution. (Domestic Relations Law, § 170-a.)

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contract Rights -- Marriage

8. Marriage is not a contract within the meaning of the Federal Constitution which prohibits the States from impairing the obligation of contracts.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

9. A presumption of constitutionality attaches to the enactment of a statute.

 

1190 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Padar Realty Co. v. Klein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


60 A.D.2d 533; 400 N.Y.S.2d 46; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14444



December 15, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant zoning board challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which annulled the zoning board's refusal to reinstate a building alteration permit that had been issued to appellee applicants.


OVERVIEW: The court determined that an administrative agency was entitled to render a reasoned interpretation and construction of relevant statutes and regulations where warranted. Sufficiently particularized circumstances tending to show a departure from prior interpretation and construction wherein it was claimed that a party is being selectively discriminated against in the initiation of the departure, warranted a hearing on the discrimination claim. Therefore, a hearing was appropriate in the case at bar and the claim was remanded.


OUTCOME: The judgment that annulled the zoning board's refusal to reinstate a building permit was reversed and remanded for a hearing.

 

1191 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mead v. First Trust & Deposit Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


60 A.D.2d 71; 400 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13965



December 9, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, and petition reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner administrative judge sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Onondaga County (New York), which granted respondent attorneys' motion to dismiss a petition that sought to vacate default judgments containing an allowance for attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner administrative judge instituted a proceeding against respondent attorneys, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 217a, in order to vacate default judgments that included attorney fees not awarded in compliance with N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3215. The lower court granted respondents' motion to dismiss the action instituted by petitioner. On appeal, the court found questions of fact existed as to whether the attorney fees included in the default judgments were reasonable and necessary, and whether there was quantum meruit proof as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3215. The court reversed the lower court's grant of respondents' motion to dismiss. The court held that the reason for reversal was not to deprive respondents of full payment of its actual necessary legal expenses in collecting the defaulted debt. The aim was to prevent respondents from receiving more than what was necessary for their services in accomplishing the default judgments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment, which granted respondent attorney's motion to dismiss a petition to vacate default judgments containing an allowance of attorney fees. Questions of fact existed as to whether the attorney fees included in the default judgments were reasonable and necessary, and if they were without necessary quantum meruit proof.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Vacating Default Judgments Obtained by Fraud or Other Illegality

1. Under a statute pertaining to the vacatur of default judgments, an Administrative Judge,  [***3] upon a proper showing that default judgments were obtained by fraud, misrepresentation, illegality, unconscionability, lack of due service, violations of law, or other illegalities, may bring a proceeding to vacate such judgments, where such default judgments have been obtained in a number of instances deemed sufficient by such Judge to justify such action. (Judiciary Law, § 217-a.)

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Attorneys' Fees

2. A contract provision to the effect that in the event of default in paying the obligation the debtor will pay to the creditor the reasonable expenses of collecting the debt, is lawful and proper, even where the parties agree that a specific percentage of the unpaid debt may be computed as the amount of the attorneys' fees and be included as part of the collection expenses to be incorporated in the judgment.

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Attorneys' Fees

3. Despite the validity of contractual provisions whereby parties agree that a specific percentage of the unpaid debt may be computed as the amount of the attorneys' fees and be included as part of the collection expenses to be incorporated in the judgment, when the precise [***4] issue of value has been directly presented, entry of default judgments containing an allowance for attorneys' fees computed at a fixed percentage without requiring quantum meruit proof, would be contrary to public policy because it would amount to enforcement of a penalty.

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Attorneys' Fees

4. The allowance contained in default judgments for a fixed percentage fee is viewed only as a maximum fee, limiting the amount of reasonable attorneys' fees which the creditor may charge upon proving the extent of the necessary services actually rendered.

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Attorneys' Fees

5. Where a default judgment contains an allowance for attorneys' fees computed at a fixed percentage, it has been held that the debtor stands in the position of an indemnitor to the creditor, and that the creditor cannot recover from the debtor on this contractual provision until he has incurred the expenses by paying his attorney.

Motions and Orders -- Motion to Dismiss Special Proceeding

6. On a motion to dismiss a special proceeding, principles governing motions for summary judgment in actions at law apply.

Motions  [***5] and Orders -- Motion to Dismiss Special Proceeding

7. If facts are peculiarly within the control or possession of the party moving to dismiss and are not available to the other party, the motion should be denied.

Judgments -- Default Judgments -- Attorneys' Fees

8. Where questions of fact exist as to whether attorneys' fees computed at a fixed percentage and included in default judgments were reasonable and necessary, entry of judgments by court clerks without quantum meruit proof as required by statute ( CPLR 3215, subd [a]) is improper and void.

 

1192 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cwikla


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


60 A.D.2d 40; 400 N.Y.S.2d 35; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13961



December 8, 1977 



DISPOSITION:  [***3] Judgment, Supreme Court, Bronx County rendered on May 30, 1975, modified, as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, only to the extent of reducing the sentence imposed upon defendant [Ford] for the crime of burglary in the first degree to an indeterminate term not to exceed 15 years ( CPL 470.15, subd 2, par c; subd 3 and CPL 470.20, subd 6), and otherwise affirmed.

Judgment, Supreme Court, Bronx County rendered on May 30, 1975, unanimously modified, on the law, only to the extent of reducing the sentence imposed upon defendant [Cwikla] for the crime of burglary in the first degree to an indeterminate term not to exceed 15 years ( CPL 470.15, subds 2, 3, 4, par [c]), and otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgments of the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), which convicted defendants of possession of a dangerous instrument and burglary in the first degree, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 140.30 (3), and sentenced them to a minimum of seven years and a maximum of 21 years.


OVERVIEW: One defendant was convicted of numerous offenses and sentenced to concurrent 15-year terms. Defendant appealed and was granted a new trial. At that new trial, defendants were both tried and convicted of possession of a dangerous weapon and burglary in the first degree and sentenced to a term of seven to 21 years. Defendants appealed their conviction for possession of dangerous weapon and burglary, claiming that a handkerchief was not a dangerous instrument pursuant to § 140.30 (3). The one defendant appealed his sentence, claiming a violation of due process because the trial court could not impose a stricter sentence on retrial. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that a handkerchief could be a dangerous instrument where it was used by defendants to bind and gag the victim, with the potential of causing great bodily harm or death. The court reduced the sentence, finding that the more severe sentence was not valid because the trial court improperly relied on the "aggregate" crimes charged, and there was no evidence of post-conviction conduct to validate the harsher sentence. The court reduced the other defendant's sentence so that it would not be unduly harsh and severe.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for possession of a dangerous instrument and burglary in the first degree, but reduced defendants' sentences.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Dangerous Instrument

1. An article not designed as a weapon, such as a handkerchief, may, in terms of temporary use, fit the statutory definition of a dangerous instrument. (Penal Law, § 10.00, subd 13.)

Crimes -- Dangerous Instrument

2. A statute which defines a "dangerous instrument" as "any instrument, article or substance * * * which, under the circumstances in which it is used * * * is readily capable of causing death or other serious physical [***4] injury", is not void for vagueness. (Penal Law, § 10.00, subd 13.)

Crimes -- Repugnant Verdict

3. Inconsistency in a verdict does not invalidate it, since each count is treated separately; only repugnance in a verdict will invalidate it, and repugnance can only occur where the crimes contain identical counts.

Crimes -- Repugnant Verdict

4. A finding that defendants did not cause the death of the victim or did not cause him physical injury in the commission of the burglary, is not repugnant to the finding that the handkerchief was a dangerous instrument, since the elements of the crimes for which defendants were acquitted were not identical to those involved in the crimes for which they were convicted.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Imposition of More Severe Sentence on Retrial

5. In order for a more severe sentence on retrial to be valid, the reasons for such sentence must rest upon the conviction of defendant for the same offense upon retrial and identifiable conduct on the part of the defendant occurring after the time of the original sentence, which must appear in the record.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Imposition of More Severe Sentence on Retrial

 [***5] 6. When a defendant is resentenced following a second trial, the sentence of the first trial court imposed on any one of the crimes for which defendant was convicted standing alone should control, rather than the sentence as it related to all of those crimes as an aggregate.

Crimes -- Sentence

7. The Penal Law does not require that a court, in sentencing a defendant for more than one crime, make the sentence for any crime depend upon the sentence of any of the others, except to consider whether the sentences shall be consecutive or concurrent.

Crimes -- Sentence

8. Where a defendant has been sentenced under a multicount indictment, a conviction under any count is to be considered a separate crime requiring upon sentence a separate judicial act.

Crimes -- Sentence

9. A sentence of 7 to 21 years imposed upon a defendant for the same crime to which his codefendant, upon resentence, could receive an indeterminate sentence of not more than 15 years, is unduly harsh and severe.

Harder v. New York State Dep't of Environmental Conservation


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


60 A.D.2d 664; 400 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14675



December 1, 1977 

 

1194 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. White


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


59 A.D.2d 347; 399 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13928



November 22, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Judgment, Supreme Court, New York County, rendered on March 19, 1976, unanimously modified, on the law, to the extent of vacating the sentence, reversing the order granting the motion to set aside the verdict, reinstating the verdict of guilty of robbery in the first degree and remanding [***2] for sentence thereon, and otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and the People cross-appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which convicted defendant of robbery in the second degree. The jury found defendant guilty, as charged, of robbery in the first degree. The trial court vacated the jury verdict on the ground that N.Y. Penal Law § 160.15 was unconstitutional for placing the burden on defendant of proving an affirmative defense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice entered a barbershop and, at gunpoint, took cash, a television set, and some clothes from the shopkeeper. There was no evidence that defendant's pistol was fired during the robbery. The witness gave a positive identification; a week after the crime, defendant saw the shopkeeper, approached him, and apologized for the robbery. After the jury found defendant guilty of first degree robbery and before sentencing, defendant moved under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 330.30(1), 440.10(1), para. (h) to set aside the verdict, asserting that N.Y. Penal Law § 160.15(4) was unconstitutional because it placed the burden of proof upon defendant to prove that the pistol displayed was unloaded or inoperable. The trial court agreed, set aside the verdict, and found defendant guilty of second degree robbery. Both sides appealed, and the court concluded that none of defendant's contentions merited disturbance of the judgment of conviction. The court rejected defendant's challenge of the constitutionality of § 160.1(4) and held that the People's contention that the order of vacatur was improper was meritorious. Section 160.1(4) was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's sentence, reversed the order granting defendant's motion to set aside the verdict, reinstated the verdict of guilty of robbery in the first degree, and remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Robbery -- Affirmative Defense

1. Subdivision 4 of section 160.15 of the Penal Law, which provides that, in the prosecution of an accused for robbery in the first degree, it is an affirmative defense that the firearm displayed was unloaded or inoperable, is not unconstitutional for placing the burden on the accused in violation of due process (see People v Coleman, 42 NY2d 837).

Crimes -- Right to Counsel

2. Although a defense counsel may not have tried the case in an errorless fashion, his representation, on the record as a whole, was not so wholly without substance as to make it a nullity.

Crimes -- Appeal

3. While the testimony of a witness as to admissions made to her by a codefendant after a robbery was not admissible against defendant, defense counsel having made no objection thereto, any error as to its admissibility was not preserved for appellate review.

Crimes -- Robbery

4. A verdict of guilty of robbery in the first degree having been improperly vacated on the ground that subdivision 4 of section 160.15 of the Penal Law, placing the burden of an affirmative defense [***3] on an accused to prove that a firearm displayed was unloaded or inoperable, was unconstitutional, a judgment convicting an accused of robbery in the second degree is modified, on the law, by reinstating the verdict.
