In re Sabatino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


59 A.D.2d 992; 399 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 14262



November 17, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner executrix sought review from an order of the Surrogate's Court, Albany County (New York), which denied her motion to vacate an order of attachment on her.


OVERVIEW: Respondent filed an ex parte motion for an order of attachment, which the trial court granted. The executrix moved to vacate an order of attachment, which the trial court denied without a hearing, finding that the executrix failed to meet her burden of proof. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the trial court erred in deciding contested material facts based solely upon the parties' affidavits. The court further held that statutory changes to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6223(b) placed the burden of proof upon the respondent, and thus provided further grounds for reversal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which had denied the executrix's motion to vacate an order of attachment, and remitted for further proceedings.

 

1196 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ause v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


59 A.D.2d 317; 399 N.Y.S.2d 526; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13569



November 14, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, in accordance with opinion by Hancock, Jr., J., and otherwise affirmed, and petition dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospital superintendent filed a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, county executive and board of review, after his employment was terminated. The Supreme Court, Erie County (New York) dismissed a cause of action alleging that his employment was terminated without a pre-termination hearing in violation of county regulations and ordered trials or hearings on the other causes of action. The parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The superintendent argued, inter alia, that his dismissal violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75, that the county executive's failure to make a public statement explaining his termination stigmatized him and deprived him of liberty and property without due process, and that he was unconstitutionally discharged for partisan political reasons. The trial court dismissed a cause of action regarding pre-termination hearing and ordered trials or hearings on the other causes of action. The court modified the trial court's order by granting the motions to dismiss of the county executive and board of review as to these causes of action, affirmed the order as modified, and dismissed the petition. The superintendent had no right to a hearing under § 75 because, as a holdover appointee, he was a temporary employee. Termination did not deprive him of a property interest because he did not have sufficient expectation of continued employment. Termination did not deprive him of a liberty interest because the reasons were communicated to him privately and were not stigmatic. His position was a policymaking position and thus was excepted from constitutional protections against patronage dismissals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order as to dismissal of the superintendent's cause of action alleging that county regulations entitled him to a pre-termination hearing. The court modified the order by granting the motions to dismiss of the county executive and board of review as to the causes of action under the New York Civil Service Law and the United States Constitution, and as modified affirmed the order and dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- termination of employment -- hearing -- dismissal for political purposes.

1. Special Term properly determined that petitioner could be dismissed from his position as Superintendent of the Erie County E. J. Meyer Memorial Hospital without a hearing, since the Erie County Charter and the Erie County Administrative Code require a hearing for officers appointed for the term of the county executive, as was petitioner, only if removal is prior to the end of such term. The term of the county executive ended prior to petitioner's removal, and petitioner was a holdover during the county executive's second term. Accordingly, petitioner could be dismissed without a hearing.

2. Petitioner is not entitled to the protection of section 75 of the Civil Service Law, since his appointment was for the term of the county executive and was not permanent.

3. Failure [***2] to issue a public statement explaining petitioner's termination did not deprive him of property or liberty without due process of law, since petitioner had insufficient expectation of continued employment to constitute a property interest and the failure to issue a statement cannot be held to infringe upon petitioner's liberty.

4. Dismissal of petitioner for partisan political reasons was not improper, since the duties and term of petitioner's position indicate that his is a policymaking position for which there is no protection from partisan dismissal.

People v. One 1976 Ford Van


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]

1199 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Curle v. Ward


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


59 A.D.2d 286; 399 N.Y.S.2d 308; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13562



November 10, 1977 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer appealed from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which granted a motion by plaintiff employee for summary judgment, reinstated him with back pay, enjoined the employer from disciplining the employee on the basis of alleged membership in the Ku Klux Klan, and declared unconstitutional and void a directive of defendant commissioner barring employees from membership in the Ku Klux Klan.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner issued a directive that did not permit employees of the correctional institution to be members of the Ku Klux Klan. The employee was terminated after he refused to answer question regarding his alleged membership in the Ku Klux Klan. The employee commenced proceedings pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, later converted to an action for declaratory judgment and a permanent injunction order, against defendants. The supreme court granted the employee his requested relief upon his motion for summary judgment. Upon review, the court affirmed the supreme court. The court held that the directive infringed upon the employee's First Amendment right to freedom of association, and defendants did not meet their heavy burden to demonstrate that the directive and the inquiry into the employee's association was necessary to protect a legitimate state interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment of the supreme court.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and prisoners -- dismissal of prison guard for membership in Ku Klux Klan -- freedom of association.

1. Plaintiff, a correction officer with an unblemished record, was unlawfully dismissed from service because of his membership in the Ku Klux Klan. The directive issued by defendant Commissioner of the Department of Correctional Services barring all employees from membership in the Ku Klux Klan, unconstitutionally infringes upon plaintiff's freedom of association as protected by the First Amendment. Defendant's directive establishes "guilt by association" alone which cannot be countenanced under [***2] the First Amendment. The exclusion of all persons affiliated with the Ku Klux Klan provides no room for examination of the nature of an employee's, affiliation: what he believes to be the goals of the organization, to what extent he adheres to those goals, and to what extent the performance of his duties is affected. The State has failed to meet its burden of demonstrating that membership in the Klan poses a real threat to its substantial interests in maintaining proper prison security and protecting inmates from uneven treatment. The State has offered no evidence that inmate fear of the Klan would disrupt the delicate peace of the correctional facilities or that membership in the Klan would prevent the member from properly carrying out his functions as a correction officer. Tranquility and elimination of discrimination in the prisons will come only through a thorough examination of the practices of all correction officers and by ferreting out those who violate departmental policy within the facilities.

2. Before it can even lawfully inquire into an employee's associations the State must show that such inquiry is necessary to protect a legitimate State interest.

Tessler v. Siegel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


59 A.D.2d 846; 399 N.Y.S.2d 218; 1977 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13993



November 3, 1977 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a child support action brought pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 4, respondent father appealed from an order of the Family Court, New York County (New York) awarding petitioner mother child support for the parties' infant daughter. The father contended that there was an equal protection violation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court awarded the mother child support without considering her financial circumstances. The trial court found that the father was solely responsible for the infant's support. The court reversed the order. The court held that pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 240 the responsibility for support should have been apportioned based on the means of the parties. The court ordered that the mother's financial status should be examined.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order awarding the mother child support. The court ordered examination of the mother's financial status.
